Fact	PETITIONER: <NAME> RAO JIWAJI <NAME> Vs. RESPONDENT: UNION OF INDIA DATE OF JUDGMENT: 15/12/1970 BENCH: <NAME>, <NAME>: <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, I.D.
Fact	CITATION: 1971 AIR 530 1971 SCR (3) 9 1971 SCC (1) 85 CITATOR INFO : R 1972 SC 202 (7) RF 1973 SC1461 (186,700,703,1100,1609,2152,21 RF 1975 SC2299 (275,637) E 1976 SC1207 (46,546) D 1977 SC1361 (192) R 1978 SC 803 (30,34) R 1981 SC1284 (1) RF 1982 SC 710 (25) RF 1986 SC1126 (47) D 1987 SC 522 (51) R 1987 SC1010 (71) RF 1989 SC1534 (11) ACT: Constitution of India, 1950- Article 366(22)- Scope of- Recognition of Rulers by President-Order by President "derecognising" all Rulers--Validity of order-Power, if political-If exercise of paramountcy rights.
Fact	Constitution of India 1950- Article 291- Article if creates an obligation to pay Privy Purse-Repudiation of obligation if act of State--"Charged on .... the  Consolidated Fund of India", meaning of-Article if a provision relating to" covenant within the meaning of Article 363.
Fact	Constitution of India, 1950- Article 363- Exclusion of jurisdiction of Courts-Scope of exclusionary clauses- Determining the meaning of articles 366(22), 291, 362 and 363, if within bar of Article 363-" Dispute arising out of provision of the Constitution relating to covenant", meaning Artic le if "recreation" of paramountcy.
Fact	Constitution of India, 1950- Article 362-If a provision "relating to" Covenent etc.
Fact	within the meaning of Article 363.
Fact	Constitution of India, 1950-Articles 19(1)(f) and 31 and Article 32- Order of President under Article 366(22)-- Order of President under Article 366(22) " derecognising" Rulers- Repudiation of liability to pay Privy Purse and denial of rights and privileges-If violation of fundamental rights- Maintainability of petition-Privy Purse-If property.
Fact	HEADNOTE: On the promulgation of the Indian Independence Act, 1947, the Princely States adjoining the Dominio n of India merged with the Dominion of India.
Fact	The instruments of merger provided for_the integration of the <ORG>s and guaranteed to the <ORG> the Privy Purse, succession according to law and custom to the gaddi of the <ORG> and personal rights, privileges, dignities and titles.
Fact	These instruments were concurred in and guaranteed by the Dominion of India.
Fact	Later, the <ORG>s integrated with the <ORG> of India under the Constitution of India, 1950, the <ORG> abandoning all authority in regard to their territories.
Fact	Special provisions were enacted in the Constitution regarding Privy Purses and the rights and privileges of the erstwhile <ORG>.
Fact	By article 291 , the sum-, guaranteed by the Dominion of India to any Ruler as Privy Purse under any covenant or agreement was to be charged on and paid out of <ORG> and the.
Fact	sums so paid were to be exempt from all taxes on income.
Fact	By article 362 the <ORG>, the <ORG> <ORG> and the executive of the <ORG> and the <ORG>s were enjoined to ha ve due regard to the guarantees and assurances under the covenants and agreements between the Governments of the Dominion of India and the heads of the former Indian States.
Fact	Also, provisions were made in various statutes conferring on the "Rulers" certain privileges and benefits.
Fact	By Art.
Fact	366(22) a "Ruler" was defined to mean the prince, chief or other person by whom covenant and agreements.
Fact	were entered into and who "for the time being" was recognised by the President as the Ruler and included any person who "for the time being" 10 was recognised by the President as the successor of such Ruler.
Fact	Article 363 excluded the jurisdiction of <ORG> and all other courts "in respect of any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant etc."
Fact	or in any dispute "in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of the Constitution relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant" etc.
Fact	On September 2, 1970, a Bill intituled the Con stitution (Twenty Fourth Amendment) Bill 1970, and providing that "Articles 291 and 362 of the Constitution and clause (22) of article 366 shall be omitted" was introduced in <ORG>.
Fact	The Bill was declared passed.
Fact	On September 5, 1970, the motion for consideration of the Bill did not obtain in <ORG> the requisite majority of not less than two-thirds of the members present and voting as required by Art.
Fact	368 of the Constitution.
Fact	The motion for introduction of the Bill was declared lost.
Fact	A few hours thereafter the President of India, purporting to exercise power under cl.
Fact	(22) of Art.
Fact	366 of the Constitution signed an instrument withdrawing recognition of all the Rulers.
Fact	A communication to the effect was sent to all Rulers in India who had been previously recognised under art.
Fact	366(22) of the Constitution.
Fact	The petitioners moved this Court under Art.
Fact	32 of the Constitution challenging the order of the President "derecognising" them as unconstitutional, ultra vires and void.
Fact	They contended that the  President had no power to withdraw recognition of Ruler once recognised; that assuming the President had such power, exercise of the power was coupled with duty to recognise his successor; that the order of the President "derecognsing" all the Rulers en masse amounted to arbitrary exercise of power for a collateral purpose; that the Order violated the constitutional mandates in articles 291 and 362; that article 291 created an obligation in <ORG> to pay the Privy Purse and Privy Purse was property; and that the Order being one without authority of law infringed the guarantee of the fundamental rights under Arts.
Fact	19(1)(f), 21 and 31 of the Constitution.
Fact	<ORG> contended, inter alia, that the petition was not maintainable, because, the source of the right to receive the Privy Purse and to be accorded the privileges claimed was a political agreement and the privy purse was in the nature of a political pension; that in recognising or derecognising a ruler the President exercised a politic al power which was a sovereign power and that the rights and obligations were liable to by varied or repudiated in accordance with "State policy"; that the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to enforce rights and obligations arising out of the covenant was excluded, because, the rights and obligations arose out of act of state; that the concept of paramountly of the British Crown was inherited by <ORG> and therefore recognition of Rulership was a "gift of the President"; and further that the petitioners stood excluded by article 363 , for, they were seeking either to enforce the covenants and agreements or were seeking to enforce the provisions of the Constitution "relating to" such covenants.
Fact	HELD: Per <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, <NAME>.
Fact	(<NAME> and <NAME>, <NAME>.
Fact	dissenting).
Fact	The Order of the President "derecognising" the Rulers is ultra vires and illegal.
Fact	[69 G; 100 C] (Per <NAME>, C.J.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) The action of the President withdrawing recognition of all Rulers is ultra vires a rticle 366(22) and a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) neither expressly nor by implication places the power in the hands of the President to say that although a Ruler is in existence or 11 a successor is available there shall be no ruler of any particular state.
RatioOfTheDecision	The definition contemplates the existence of the Ruler "for the time being".
RatioOfTheDecision	The phrase "for the time being" cannot mean that any person can be appointed who has no claim whatever or that temporary appointments may be made or that no appointment need be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	The continuity of a Ruler of an Indian State is obligatory so long as the Ruler is alive or a successor can be found.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obligation to recognise a Ruler is bound up with the other guarantees contained in articles 291 and 362 and the definition in article 366(22) is merely the key to find a particular Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The withdrawal of recognition from all the Rulers renders the guarantees, as also the relevant articles of the Constitution, inoperative.
RatioOfTheDecision	[58 A-H] (ii) The right to recognise a ruler, from out of sev eral claimants, is not an act of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The selection has to be in accordance with law and custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution gave the right to the President to recognise a Ruler for the time being; but it cannot be stretched to give a paramountly of the same character as that enjoyed by the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	To claim such a paramountcy one has to, ignore completely the arrangements by which the Rulers parted with their territories and ruling rights and were assured of their privy purses and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights became consti- tutionally protected rights which so long as the Ruler's line was not extinct belonged to the Ruler "for the time being.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, when the guarantees were given by the Constitution, paramountcy if any, went out.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 362 is the converse of paramountcy inasmuch as it compels the two limbs of government to have "due regard"' to the guarantees and assurances given to the Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor can article 363 be said to "recreate" paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article was intended to keep certain matters outside  the jurisdiction of the courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <NAME> are citizens of India and the President or <ORG> cannot invoke the doctrine of paramountcy to sustain an illegal inroad upon the rights of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	[51 H-52 B] (iii) The argument based on act of state is not of any more validity.
RatioOfTheDecision	An 'Act of <ORG>' is not available against a citizen.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a sovereign act which is neither grounded in law nor does it pretend to be so.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is "a catastrophic change constituting a new departure".
RatioOfTheDecision	Since there are no sovereign or political powers under our Constitution every action of the executive limb of government must seek justification in some law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very existence of article 363 , which it is said incorporates some kind of paramountcy or act of state, shows that there is no political power outside the law; otherwise an additional bar would hardly have been necessary.
Argument	[53 B] <NAME> v. Secretary of <ORG> for India,  I K.B.
Argument	613, <CITATION_1838105> ,  1 S.C.R.
Argument	537 and Secretary of <ORG> in <ORG> v. <NAME> <NAME>,  13 Moore P.C.
Argument	22, referred to.
Argument	(iv) Covenants and agreements cannot be said to create "imperfect obligations" since the Constitution takes the matter into itself and gives them its own guarantees.
Argument	In so far as those guarantees became a part of our Constitution and were included in various statutes they would be en- forceable according to the tenor of the Constitution and other laws subject, of course, to any bar created by article 363.

Argument	Further, the reasons for a decision by the President cannot be probed into in view of articles 74(2) and 361(1).
Argument	[56 E] (vi)  The argument on behalf of <ORG> that article 291 only lays down the source and manner of payment but creates no right to claim, receive or enforce payment is a complete misreading of the article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 291 makes the amount payable to the Ruler and, therefore, creates a right in him to demand it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words "shall be charged and paid out of etc."
RatioOfTheDecision	make the payment obligatory and when expanded the words read "shall be charged on and shall be paid out of" etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The direction to pay is in no uncertain terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	The recipient is mentioned in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) where the article says 'and the sums so paid to any Ruler' and this shows who is to be paid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the Article in addition to the source and manner also lays down that it shall be paid and paid free of taxes on income to the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article is self- supporting and self-ordaining.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result of charging a sum on <ORG> is to provide that this destination shall not be altered even by vote of <ORG> and the charging is sufficiently effe ctive for ensuring the right application.
RatioOfTheDecision	[62 D-63 B] (vii) Article 363 does not bar relief to the petitioners.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words provisions of this Constitution' in the latter part of article 363 arc not left unqualified.
RatioOfTheDecision	The draftsmen would have referred to the numbers of the articles if disputes of every kind under those articles stood excluded.
RatioOfTheDecision	The requirement is that it must be a provision "relating" to a treaty, covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words 'relating to' mean that the provision must bear upon treaties etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	as its dominant purpose or theme; it is not sufficient if treaties are mentioned there for some collateral purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	So tested, [65 D-E] (a) Article 362 is a provision relating to a treaty, covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its dominant theme is 'the rights, privileges and dignities of the Rulers under covenants and agreements and, therefore the provision is one relating to covenants and agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	[65 G-H] (b) Article 366(22) has for its dominant purpose the selection of Rulers through the application of the covenant and agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	When the President acts-within the four corners of his authority the matter is barred by article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	What the President has done is to take away recognition 'from all Rulers and such a power does not flow from article 366(22) and the bar of article 363 does not apply to such a dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The dispute arises neither from the covenants etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	nor from the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, it ceases to have the protection of article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	[66 C H] (c) Article 291 is not a provision relating to covenants and agreements but a special provision for the source of payment of <ORG> by charging them on <ORG> and for making the payment free of taxes on income; it does not in its dominant purpose and theme answer the description in the latter part of article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mention in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 of covenants and agreements is for its own purpose so that the amounts need not be specified.
RatioOfTheDecision	[67 H-68 B] [His Lordship did not express any opinion on the question whether withdrawal of recognition on grounds wh ich are sound and sufficient is capable of being questioned in a court of law.]
RatioOfTheDecision	13 (viii) The petitions are maintainable under article 32 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obligation to pay <ORG> being absolute the right to claim when due subsists in each ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	As soon as an appropriation Act is passed there established credit-debt and the outstanding Privy Purse becomes the property of the Ruler in the hands of the government.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also a sum certain and absolutely payable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore Privy Purse is property and any action to deprive the Rulers of their Privy Purses must be an infringement of articles 19 and 31.

Precedent	(Per <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.)
Precedent	(1) By the provisions enacted in article 366(2 2 ), 291 and 362 of the Constitution, the privileges of Rulers are made an integral part of the Constitution and they cannot be deprived of these privileges arbitrarily.
Precedent	Granting that under clause (22) of Art.
Precedent	366 the President may withdraw the recognition of a person as a Ruler, the power to nullify important provisions of the Constitution does not flow 'from that clause.
Precedent	The power conferred by the clause has to be exercised consistently with and in aid of the constitutional scheme.
Precedent	The power may be exercised, in the case of first recognition, only in favour of a person who has signed the Covenant, and in favour of his successor having, regard to the customs and laws governing the state if the Ruler dies or becomes incapable of functioning or his recognition is withdrawn.
Precedent	By the use of the expression "for the time being" in cl.
Precedent	(22) Art.
Precedent	366 the President is not invested with an authority to accord a temporary recognition to a Ruler nor with authority to recognise or not to recognise a Ruler arbitrarily; the  expression predicates that there shall be a Ruler of the Indian State, that if the first recognised Ruler dies or ceases to be a Ruller a successor shall be appointed and that there shall not be more rulers at a given time.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the express injunction in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	53(1) the executive power vested in the President is directed to be exercised in accordance with the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the power is intended to be exercised in aid of and not to destroy constitutional institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power is plainly coupled with duty-a duty.
RatioOfTheDecision	to maintain con- stitutional institutions, the constitutional provisions, the constitutional scheme and the sanctity of the solemn agreements entered into by the predecessor of <ORG>, which are accepted, recognised and incorporated in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	An order merely "derecognising" a Ruler without providing for continuation of the institution of Rulership, which is an integral part of the Constitutional scheme, is, therefore, plainly illegal.
RatioOfTheDecision	[74 C; 81 H-82 D, E; 83 A] [The  <ORG> did not express any final opinion on the question whether the expression "for the time being" in relation to the persons who had entered into covenants or agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	and in relation to the successor, implies that the President has the power in appropriate cases and for adequate reasons to withdraw recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	Also, the <ORG> did not decide the question whether in certain exceptional circumstances the President may in granting recognition to a successor depart in the larger interest of the country from the strict rule of custom governing succession to the, gaddi.
RatioOfTheDecision	[74 B-C; 82 G- H] 14 (ii) The President is not invested with any political power, transcending the Constitution which he may exercise to the prejudice of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitutional mechanism in a democratic polity does not contemplate existence of any function which may qua the citizens be designated as political.
RatioOfTheDecision	The history of negotiations which culminated in the integration of the territories of the Princely States before the commencement  of the Constitution clearly indicates that the recognition of the status of the Rulers and their rights was not temporary and also not liable to be varied or repudiated in accordance with "State policy".
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor does the Constitution invest the executive branch of the <ORG> with the power to abolish the concept of Rulership, Privy Purse and the privileges on the plea that these have become incompatible with "democracy, equality ,and social justice".
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of the President to determine the status of the Rulers by cancelling or withdrawing recognition to effectuate the policy of the <ORG> to abolish the concept of Rulership is therefore liable to be challenged in these petitions.

Precedent	(iii) An action not authorised by law against the citizens of the <ORG> cannot be supported under the shelter of paramountly.
Precedent	After the withdrawal  of British power and the extinction of paramountcy of the British <ORG>.
Precedent	did not and could not exercise any paramountcy over the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The functions of the President of India stem from the Constitution, not from a "concept of the British Crown" identified or unidentified.
RatioOfTheDecision	What the Constitution does not authorise, the President cannot grant.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rulership is therefore not a privilege which the President may in the exercise of his discretion bestow or withhold.
RatioOfTheDecision	[94 C-D] (iv) Clause (a) of article 291 which enacts that <ORG> "shall be charged on and be paid out of <ORG>" clearly raise an obligation to pay <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	A charge gives a right to receive payment out of a specified fund or property in preference over others.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the absence of clear indications to the contrary it would be difficult to hold that the expression "charge" used in the context of the financial matters of the state has a different meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution does not recognise an y sequence of priorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that does not alter the fundamental character of a charge that it specifies a fund out of which satisfaction of the expenditure charged must be made and that the prescribed expenditure shall have priority in payment to the person for whose benefit the expenditure is charged on the Fund.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitutional obligation to proceed in the manner set out in Arts.
Precedent	112, 113 & 114 imposed upon the President and the <ORG>, implies, a right in the person or persons in respect of whom the expenditure is to be incurred.
Precedent	That view is supported by other provisions in the Constitution.
Precedent	Clauses (a) & (b) of Art.
Precedent	291 must be read with articles 112, 113 and 114; they are parts of a single scheme.
Precedent	They contemplate that <ORG> shall be included in the financial statement as charged upon <ORG>; it shall be beyond the voting power of the <ORG>; its destination shall not be altered: it shall be paid to the Ruler after the Appropriation Bill is passed, and 15 when paid  it shall be free from liability to pay taxes on income.
Precedent	This is an integrated process, which cannot be interrupted without dislocating the constitutional mechanism.

Precedent	(v) Article 291 does not merely incorporate recognition of the obligation to pay privy purse under covenants incurred by <ORG>; it gives rise to a liability dehors the covenants.
Precedent	After the Constitution, the obligation to pay privy purse rested upon <ORG> not because it was inherited from the Dominion of India but because of the constitutional mandate under Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	The source of the obligation is in Art.
Precedent	291 and not in the covenants and agreements.
Precedent	Reference to the covenants and agreements in the article is for defining the privy purse.
Precedent	The obligation which arose out of the merger agreement and was on that account an act of state stied its origi nal character on acceptance by the Constitution.

Precedent	(vi) An obligation which arises out of a constitutional provision to pay to the citizens sums of money in recognition of obligations of the predecessor <ORG> may scarcely be called imperfect.
Precedent	A perfect obligation pertains to the domain of law and justice and imperfect obligation to the domain of benevolence.
Precedent	[89 E] (vii) The courts have jurisdiction to interpret and to determine the true meaning of articles 366(22), 291, 36 2 and 363 and the bar to the jurisdiction of the courts by article 363 is a limited bar.
Precedent	Article 363 excludes the jurisdiction of the courts only in respect of the matter specified therein.
Precedent	A provision which purports to.
Precedent	exclude the jurisdiction of the courts i n certain matters and to deprive the aggrieved party of the normal remedy will be strictly construed, for, it is a principle not to be whittled down that an aggrieved party will not, unless the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is by clear enactment or necessary implication bar-red, be denied recourse to the courts for determination of his rights.
Precedent	It is within the province of the <ORG> alone to determine what the dispute brought before it is, and to determine whether the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is, because it falls within one of the two limbs of article 363 , excluded qua that dispute.
Precedent	Jurisdiction to try a proceeding is barred under the first limb of article 363 if the dispute "arises" out of the provision of a covenant; it is barred under the second limb of the article if the court holds that the dispute is with respect to a right arising out of a provision of the Constitution relating to a covenant.
Precedent	A dispute that an order of an executive body is unauthorised, or a legislative measure is ultra vires, is not one  arising out of any covenant under the first limb of article 363 , merely because the order or the measure violates the right of the citizen which, but for the act or measure, were not in question.
Precedent	The dispute in such a case relates to the validity of the Act or the vires of the measures.
Precedent	Exclusion of the <ORG>'s jurisdiction by the terms of the relevant words in the second limb of Art.
Precedent	363 lies in a narrow field.
Precedent	The expression "provisions of this Constitution relating 16 to" means provisions having a dominant and immediate connection with; it does not mean merely having a reference to.
Precedent	If the constitutional provision relating to a covenant is the source of the right claimed to accrue, or liability claimed to arise, then clearly under the second limb the jurisdiction of the court to entertain a dispute with respect to the right or obligation is barred [94 H-95 D; 96 B-E, 99 F] (viii) It cannot be urged that the jurisdiction of the courts to enforce rights and obligations arising out of the covenants was excl uded because the rights and obligations arose out of acts of <ORG> and by constitutional provision that exclusion was affirmed and extended after the Constitution.
Precedent	There can be no act of <ORG> against its own citizens by the <ORG>.
Precedent	The Rulers who were before integration of their <ORG>s aliens qua <ORG> are now citizens.
Precedent	Their rights and obligations which arose from an act of <ORG> are now recognised and accepted by <ORG>.
Precedent	An act of state vanishes when the new sovereign recognises either expressly or by implication the rights flowing therefrom.
Fact	Enforcement of those rights and obligations is governed by the municipal laws, and unless the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is excluded in respect of any dispute, the courts will be competent to grant relief.
Fact	[93 <CITATION_19878> ,  6 S.C.R 461, referred to.
Fact	(ix) Article 366(22) is a provision relating to recognition of Rulers and that is the direct and only purpose of the provision.
Fact	It is not a provision relating to a covenant and the refere nce to the covenant or the agreement of the nature mentioned in article 291 is only for determining who may be recognised as a Ruler.
Fact	The limited exclusion of the, jurisdiction of courts in article 363 does not operate upon the claim for a privy purse relying upon article 291.
Fact	[97 C- E; 98 E] <NAME> v. <NAME>,  3 S.C.R, 201 and <CITATION_347856> , S.C.R.
Fact	631 distinguished and explained.
RatioOfTheDecision	[The observations in <NAME> that the essential political character of the guarantee for the payment of privy purse preserved by article 363 , and the obligation cannot be enforced in any municipal court held unnecessary for the purpose "of the decision in that case and incorrect.
RatioOfTheDecision	][98 D-E] (x) Reference to the covenant in article 291 merely identifies the sum payable as privy purse; it does not make the article a provision relating to the covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	The source of the right to receive the privy purse is the constitutional mandate; it is not the covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	A dispute as to the right to ,receive the priv y, purse is therefore not a dispute arising out of the covenant within the first limb of article 363 nor is a dispute with regard to a right accruing or obligation arising out of the provisions of a constitution relating to a covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	[99 B-C] (xi) Article 362 is plainly a provision relating to covenants within the meaning of article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	A claim to enforce the rights, privileged and dignities under the covenants will therefore be barred by the first limb of article 363 and a claim to enforce the recognition of right and privileges recognised by article 362 will be barred under the second limb of article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Jurisdiction of the courts will however not by excluded where the relief claimed is founded on a statutory provision enacted to give effect to personal rights under article 362 [99 D] 17 (xii) Therefore, the <ORG> will give effect to the constitutional mandate in art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 only if satisfied that the dispute arises out of any provision of a covenant which is in force and was entered into or executed befor e the commencement of the Constitution and to which "the predecessor of <ORG> was a party, or that it is in respect of rights, liabilities or obligations accruing or arising under any provision of the Constitution in relation to a covenant; but since he right to privy purse arises under article 291 the dispute in respect of which does not fall in either clause, the jurisdiction of the court is not excluded.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is not excluded in respect of disputes relating to personal rights and privileges which are granted by statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	[99 F- H] (xiii) Since the order of the President is without authority of law there is a clear infringement of the guarantee of the fundamental rights under articles 19(1) (f), 21 and 31 (1) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	[72 H] The <ORG> did not express any opinion (1) on the question whether a dispute that an executive act or legislative measure operating upon a right accruing or liability arising out of a provision is invalid, falls within the seco nd limb of article 363 and (ii) on the plea that the order was made for a collateral purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the view taken, the <ORG> did not deal with the plea that Rulership was "property" and the Order of the President deprived the petitioners of that property without authority of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	[73 A] Per <NAME>, J.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) The meaning given to the expression `Ruler' in article 366(22) is only for the purpose of the Constitution and not for any other purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rulers of Indian States disappeared as soon as their territories were merged with India and all those quondam Rulers became citizens of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their rulership is merely a status entitling them to Privy Purse and certain privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 291, 362 and 366(2)(a) and (b) (before Its deletion), as well as entry 34 of List I of Schedule VII referred to Rulers and, therefore, it became necessary to define that expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	[160 E-G; 161 A] (ii) Article 366(22) imposes a constitutional duty on the President and for that purpose has conferred on him certain powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power is one  coupled with duty.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President cannot create a successor; he can only recognise the successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	Recognition means the power to locate.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence the power conferred on the President under the second part of article 366(22) is a very limited power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power has to be exercised in accordance with law; in other words it has to be exercised as a quasi-judicial power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "for the time being" in the second part of the article is relevant as the question of recognition of a new ruler arises on the death of each Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression contemplates the continuity of Rulership so long as the Ruler who entered into covenant or agreement or a successor of his is in existence.
RatioOfTheDecision	[164 G, B-C] (iii) The power to recognise the Rulers does not include the power not to recognise.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President cannot do indirectly what the legislature cannot do directly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ruler as referred to in some of the provisions of the Constitution is an entity created by the Constitution to further certain purposes recognised by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That entity cannot be abolished either by the executive or by the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, it is not possible to spell out a power to abolish the Rulership under Article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	[165 G, H] 18 [His Lordships did not go into the question whether a Ruler once recognised can be derecognised by the President and, if so, under what circumstances.]
RatioOfTheDecision	(iv) The power of recognition of the Rulers cannot be claimed to be a facet of the paramountcy enjoyed by_the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy is the very antithesis of rule of law and the government of India cannot consider itself a superior, power in its relationship with the citizens of this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nature and scope of the power exercisable by the President under a provision of the Constitution must be spelled out from the language of the provision and from the purpose intended to be served by the provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	[166 E-G] (v) The plea of State policy is irrelevant in the context of this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution has laid down a policy, that policy cannot be departed from eithe r by the legislature or by the executive.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the legislature nor the executive, can have a policy which runs counter to the policy laid down by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	[159 E] (vi)The stand taken by <ORG> that the concept of <NAME>hip, Privy Purse and the privileges guaranteed to the <NAME> have become incompatible with "democracy equity and social justice" raise political issues.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court is not the forum for going into political issues, nor is it concerned with political passions surrounding the issues arising for decision in this case.
Precedent	[159 B] (vii) <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	concurring).
Precedent	There is nothing like a political power under our Constitution in the matter of relationship between the executive and the citizens.
Precedent	The Constitution recognises only three power viz:, the legislative, judicial and executive.
Precedent	The executive cannot exercise any sovereignty over its citizens.
Precedent	The legal sovereignty vests with the Constitution and the political sovereignty with the people.
Precedent	The President is a creature of t he Constitution; he can only act in accordance with the Constitution.

Argument	631. distinguished.

RatioOfTheDecision	[168 B] (ix)The impugned order is also unconstitutional for the reason that the power conferred under art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is exercised for a collateral purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	The circumstances under which the impugned order came to be made show that there was attempt to do indirectly what the government could not do directly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an exercise of power is impermissible under article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution or any of its provisions have ceased to serve the needs of the people, ways must be found to change them but it is impermissible to bypass the Constitution or its provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	For that reason also the impugned orders must be held to be ultra vires art.

Argument	(xi) Article 363 excludes the jurisdiction of courts only in respect of matters coming under article 362.

Argument	Privy Purse was taken out for special treatment by the Constitution under article 291 and therefore it is excluded from the general provision in article 362.

RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of most of the Rulers the right toreceive Privy Purse was an enforceable right even before article 291 came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not easy to accept the contention that what was an enforceable right was made unenforceable by the Constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	[177 D, E] (xiii)It is not possible to accept the contention that the expression "charged on...... <ORG> of India" in article 291 merely means that the amounts payable as Privy Purse are not notable and that the expression neither creates a right in favour of the person in whose benefit the charge is created nor is <ORG> pledged for the payment of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If an item of expenditure charged on <ORG> merely means that the expenditure is non-votable then there was no need t o provide in article 113 that "so much of the estimate as relates to expenditure charged upon <ORG> shall not be submitted to the vote of <ORG>".
RatioOfTheDecision	That part of article 113 was evidently enacted to make effective the statutory lien over <ORG> created in favour of the person to, 20 whom the payment had to be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	It emphasises the fact that the pledge created in favour of the person for whose benefit the charge is created by the Constitution, cannot be taken away even by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	No sooner the President recognises the Ruler of an Indian State, he becomes entitled to the Privy Purse guaranteed under article 291 from the date the Constitution came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every constitutional sanction for payment is necessarily a mandate to pay if that sanction relates to the discharge of an obligation; it is an enforceable mandate.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besidesthe executive cannot take the stand that it will not respect a mandate of the Constitution unless that mandate is enforceable in a court  of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether a particular constitutional mandate is enforceable or not, itis all the same binding on all the organs of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	[177 E-H;182 C-D] (xiv)A dispute whether the President has the power to abolish all Rulers under article 366(22) quite plainly cannot be held to be a dispute in respect of "any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of the Constitution relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant........ within the meaning at the second part of article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is in dispute is the true scope of the power of the President under article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	Power is an authority whereas a right in the context in which it is used in article 363 signifies property.
RatioOfTheDecision	A dispute as regards the interpretation of a provision of the Constitution is not a dispute within the contemplation of the second limb of article 363 as it is not a dispute in 'respect of any right, liability or obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "relating to" is a word of wide import but in the context in  which it is used in article 363 , it must have a narrower, meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word means "to bring into relation", or "establish relation between".
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, the provision of the Constitution in question must be linked with the merged agreements or covenants directly and immediately.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must have no independent existence.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) is an independent provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has nothing to do with the agreements and covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	[186 G-187 G] (xv) Article 362 clearly links itself with the agreements and covenants and it has no independent existence apart from the agreements and covenants; [182 G] [His Lordship did not find it necessary to go into the scope of article 362.]
RatioOfTheDecision	(xvi)The petitioners have established that their rights under articles 31 and 19(1)(f) have been contravened.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to receive the Privy Purse under article 291 , being a legal right, is a right enforceable through the courts of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	That right is undoubtedly property.
RatioOfTheDecision	The denial of those benefits which had been afforded the Rulers under statut es is again a contravention of the petitioners' fundamental right to property.

RatioOfTheDecision	The right to payment ,arises de-hors the charge on <ORG>, the right arises from recognition by article 291 of guarantee of payment of privy purses under a covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	[224 D; 225 G-H] (xiv) Therefore, article 362 is not the only article which falls within article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 363 uses the word provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word provisions indicate more than one article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has to be emphasised that article 291 , 363 and 366(22) have a most direct and visible relation to article 363.
Precedent	[228 B-C] (xv)"Rights, liabilities and obligations" in articles 294(b) and 295(1)(b) of the Constitution refer to other legal rights which were enforceable in a court of law.
Precedent	Privy purses under the covenants and merger agreements were no such legal rights enforceable in a court of law for the obvious reason that if prior to the Constitution the covenants and  merger agreements were sought to be enforced in a municipal court, the government would have demurred on the plea of Act of State.
Precedent	That plea in bar would be available to <ORG> as a defence to any claim under articles 294(b) and 295(1)(b).
Precedent	Articles 294(b) and 295(1)(b) deal with devolution of liabilities of the dominion and part B States respectively.
Precedent	The Constitution has dealt with privy purses and covenants in separate articles.
Precedent	Therefore,_articles 294(b) and 295(1)(b) can have no application to privy purses and privileges.
Precedent	[229 E- F] <ORG> and Ors.
Precedent	v. <ORG> and <ORG>.,  7 S.C.R.
Precedent	892 and <CITATION_1101230> .,  4 S.C.R.
Precedent	280, referred to.
Precedent	(xvi) In sum, the agreement to pay privy purses and to continue privileges of the Princes which were guaranteed by <ORG> before the Constitution were all political agreements born out of political bargains to achieve integration of Indian States with the Dominion of India.
Precedent	This political  bargain was carried into the Constitution by the insertion of article 291 for payment of privy purse, article 362 for continuance of privileges and article 366(22) for recognition of Princes, and the political character was preserved by inserting article 363 which bar the jurisdiction of the court in respect of disputes arising out of covenants and agreements and these articles which are related to the covenants and agreements.
Precedent	[229 G] (xvii) The petitioners cannot claim that the Order affects their rights under the various statutes.
Precedent	If the rights are derived from recognition of rulership by the President under article 366(22) and if the recognition ,cannot be impeached no right arises.
Precedent	[230 E] (xviii) Recognition of rulership is not a legal right nor is it a right to property.
Precedent	Privy purses is not a legal right to property and there is no fundamental right to privy purses.
Precedent	There is no fundamental right to rulership.
Precedent	The decisions of this Court which have held that article 363 is a bar to rights to privy purs e, personal rights and privileges and recognition of rulership from being agitated in courts, have spoken the words of the Constitution.

Statute	A reference to the Indian Independence Act, 1947 appears necessary at this stage.
Statute	The preamble of the Act stated that the Act was to make provision for the setting up in India of two independent Dominions and to provide for matters consequential on or connected with the setting u p of those Dominions and-to substitute certain provisions in the Government of India Act 1935.
Statute	Section 1 of the Act fixed the 15th day of August, 1947 as the appointed ate, from which the two independent Dominions were to come into existence.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 2 demarcated their territories, but without prejudice to the generality of the provisions of sub-section (3) of that section, the accession of Indian States to either of the two Dominions was not to be prevented.
RatioOfTheDecision	Immediately afterwards the India (Provisional Constitution) Order 1947 was promulgated and certain substitutions were made in the Government of India Act 1935 by the Governor-General by virtue of subsection (2) of Section 8 read with section 9 of the Indian Independence Act.
Statute	Sections 5 and 6 of the Government of India Act 1935 were replaced by the following sections "5.
Statute	Establishment of the Dominion :  (1)The Dominion of India established by the Indian Independence Act, 1947, shall as from the fifteenth day of August 1947, be a Union comprising :-  (a) t he Provinces hereinafter called Governors' Provinces;  (b)the Provinces hereinafter called Chief Commissioners' Provinces.
Statute	(c) the Indian States acceding to the Dominion in the manner hereinafter provided, and  (d) any other areas that may with the consent of the Dominion be uncured in the Dominion.
Statute	(2) The said Dominion of India.
Statute	is hereafter in this Act referred to as "the Dominion" and the said fifteenth day of August is hereafter in this Act referred to as 'the date of the establishment of the Dominion'.
Statute	31  6.
Statute	Accession of Indian States-  (1)An Indian State shall be deemed to have acceded to the Dominion if the Governor General has signified his acceptance of an Instrument of Accession executed by the Ruler thereof whereby the Ruler on behalf of the State :-  (a) declares that he accedes to the Dominion with the intent that the Governor-General, the Dominion Legislature, <ORG> and any other Dominion authority established for the purposes of the Dominion shall, by virtue of his Instrument o f Accession but subject al- ways to the terms thereof, and for the purposes only of the Dominion exercise in relation to the <ORG> such functions as may be vested in them by order under this Act; and  (b) assumes the obligation of ensuring that due effect is given within the <ORG> to the provisions of this Act so far as they are applicable therein by virtue of the Instrument of Accession.
Statute	(2) An Instrument of Accession shall specify the matters which the Ruler accepts as matters with respect to which <ORG> may make laws for the <ORG> and the limitations, if any, to which the power, of <ORG> to make laws for the <ORG>, and the exercise of the executive authority the Dominion in the <ORG>, are respectively to be subject.
Statute	(3)A Ruler may, by a supplementary Instrument executed by him and accepted by the Governor General vary the Instrument of Accession of his <ORG> by extending the functions which by virtue of that Instrument are exercisable by any Dominion authority in rel ation to his <ORG>.
Statute	(4)References in this Act to the Ruler of a <ORG> include references to any persons for the time being exercising the powers of the Ruler of the <ORG> whether by reason of the Ruler's minority or for any other reason.
Statute	(5)In this Act a <ORG> which has acceded to the Dominion is referred to as an acceding <ORG> and the Instrument by virtue of which a <ORG> has so acceded construed together with any supplementary Instrument executed under this section, is referred to as the Instrument of Accession of that <ORG>.
Statute	(6)As soon as may be after an Instrument of Accession or supplementary instrument has been accepted by the <ORG>rnor-General under this Section, copies of the Instrument and of the <ORG>rnor-General's acceptance thereof shall be laid before <ORG> and all courts shall take judicial notice of every such instrument and acceptance."
RatioOfTheDecision	In furtherance of these new provisions, the Instruments of Accession were executed on different dates, after negotiations between the <ORG> rnment of India and the Rulers, but nothing turns upon the date of an Instrument.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many Rulers had immediately signed Instruments of Merger, transferring full and exclusive authority, jurisdiction and powers in relation to the governance of their States to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	They were merged with the existing Provinces or were set up as Chief Commissioner's Provinces.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some others signed Instruments of Accession first and Instruments of Merger later.
RatioOfTheDecision	The remaining at first formed themselves into different <ORG>, making over the administration of their States to a Rajpramukh of <ORG> vesting in him all rights, authority and jurisdiction belonging to the Ruler which appertained to or were incidental to the Government of the Covenanting States.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this way several <ORG> or United States emerged.
RatioOfTheDecision	A brief reference to the Instrument of Accession, the Covenants and the Instruments of Merger is necessary at this stage.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Ruler of Gwalior, <NAME> of the present petitioner , joined the United State of Madhya Bharat as already indicated.

Statute	Articles XI to XV were as follows :  " ARTICLE XI (1) The Ruler of each Covenanting State shall be entitled to receive annually from the revenues of the.United States for his privy purse the amount specified against that Covenanting State in Schedule I :  Provided that the sums speci fied in the Schedule in respect of the Rulers of Gwalior and Indore shall be payable only to the present Rulers of these <ORG>s and not to their successors for whom provision will be made subsequently.

Fact	On November 24, 1949, on the passing of the Constitution of India, the <ORG> issued a Proclamation after a resolution of <ORG>.
Fact	It affirmed the constitutional relationship' between the United State and the Dominion of India and provided as follows "PROCLAMATION FOR MADHYA BHARAT Gwalior, the 24th November 1949 WHEREAS with the inauguration of the new Con- stitution for  the whole of India now being framed by <ORG>, the Government of India Act, 1935, which now governs the constitutional relationship between .this <ORG> and the Dominion of India, will stand repealed;  AND WHEREAS, in the best interests of the <ORG> of Madhya Bharat, which is closely linked with the rest of India by a community of interests in the economic, political and other fields, it is desirable that the constitutional relationship established between this <ORG> and the Dominion of India, should not only be continued as between this <ORG> and the contemplated Union of India but further strengthened, and the Constitution of India as drafted by <ORG>, which includes duly.
Fact	appointed representatives of this <ORG>, provides a suitable basis for doing so;  I, <NAME>, <NAME> of <ORG>, now hereby declare and direct-  That the Constitution of India shortly to be adopted by <ORG> shall be the Con stitution for the Madhya Bharat as for the other parts of India and shall be enforced as such in accordance with the tenor of its provisions;  That the provisions of the said Constitution shall, as from the date of its commencement' supersede and abrogate all other constitutional provisions inconsistent therewith which are at present in force in this <ORG>.
Fact	This in short is the constitutional history of the <ORG>s which united to form the United <ORG> of Madhya Bharat.
Fact	It is apparent that the Instrument of Accession and the Covenants operated as a constitution in little for the governance of the United <ORG>.
Fact	The identity of the United <ORG> as a semi-independent unit was preserved and the constitutional framework of this <ORG> was indicated.
Fact	The Covenant was an Act of <ORG> on the part of the Rulers.
Fact	It may be regarded also as such by <ORG> although no volition beyond concurrence, of the Government Played any Part whatever might I have been the diplomatic consultations between the acceding  <ORG> and <ORG>.
Fact	<ORG> merely accorded them recognition and guaranteed its provisions.
Fact	If treated as an Act of State it ended with the recognition.
Fact	It was also an Act of State on the part of the Rulers who surrendered their lights but the provisions that they evolved for the joint governance of their territories made a constitution proper of which the <ORG> were to take judicial notice and apply according to their tenor as occasion demanded.
Fact	From these documents flowed consequences which were binding alike upon the Covenanting States, the <ORG> of Madhya Bharat and <ORG> and the <ORG>-.
Fact	None of them could avoid these consequences.
Fact	The Merger agreements were much simpler documents.
Fact	As an illustration I may refer to the Bilaspur Merger agreement.
Fact	It was executed on the 15th August 1948 by the <ORG> of Bilaspur.
Fact	It consisted of five articles.
Fact	By the first article the <ORG> ceded to the <ORG> government full and exclusive authority, jurisdic tion and powers for and in relation to the governance of the <ORG>, agreeing to transfer the administration on October 12, 1948.
Fact	By article 2 the <ORG> was to receive annually a sum of Rs.
Fact	70,000/as privy purse free of taxes.
Fact	The sum included Rs.
Fact	10,000/- as an allowance for the <NAME>.
Fact	These amounts were to cover all expenses and were not to be increased nor reduced for any reason whatsoever.
Fact	By article 3 the <ORG> was entitled to the full ownership, use and enjoyment of all private properties (as distinct from <ORG> properties) belonging to him, and he was to furnish an inventory of such properties.
Fact	In case of dispute the matter was to be referred to such officers with judicial experience as the <ORG> government might nominate and the decision was to be binding on both parties.
Fact	By article 4 , the <ORG>, the Rajmata, the <NAME> and the Yuvrani were to enjoy all personal privileges en- joyed by them within and without the territories immediately before the 15th day of August 1947.
Fact	By article 5 the <ORG> go <ORG> guaranteed the succession, according to law and custom.
Fact	to the gaddi of the <ORG> and to the <NAME>'s perso nal rights, privileges dignities and titles.
Fact	The Merger agreement was signed by the <NAME> and Mr. <NAME>, Secretary in the Ministry of <ORG>s.
Fact	Although the Merger Agreement of the <NAME> of Bilaspur sufficiently illustrates the line followed it may be mentioned here that some of the Merger Agreements had more clauses than the one noticed.
