Fact	Four Articles in the Constitution are only relevant and are quoted here.
Statute	Article 291 was amended by the Constitution (Seventh Amendment) Act, 1956 by deleting clause (2) but is quoted here as it was before the Amendment :  "291 (1) Where under any covenant or agreement entered into by the Ruler of any Indian State before the commencement of this Constitution, the payment of any sums, free of tax, has been guaranteed or assured by the Government of the Dominion of India to any Ruler of such State as Privy Purse.
Statute	(a) such sums shall be charged on, and paid out of, <ORG>; and  (b) the sums so paid to any Ruler shall be exempt from all taxes on income.
Statute	(2) Where the territories of any such Indian State as aforesaid are comprised wit hin a State specified in Part A or Part B of the First Schedule.
Statute	there hall be charged on, and paid out of, <ORG> of that State such contribution, if any, in respect of the payments made by <ORG> under clause ( 1 ) and for such period as may, subject to any agreement entered into in that behalf under clause (1) of article 278 , be determined by order of the President".
Statute	This Article does not apply to the State of Jammu and Kashmir.
Statute	Article 366 contained a definition in (21) which was deleted by the Constitution (Seventh Amendment) Act 1956.
Statute	This definition may be read here :  "366.
Statute	In this Constitution, unless the context 'otherwise requires, the following expressions have the meanings hereby respectively assigned to them, that is to say- (21) "Rajpramukh" means-  (a) in relation to the State of Hyderabad, the person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Nizam of Hyderabad;  (b) in relation to the State of Jammu and Kashmir' or the State of Mysore, the  person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Maharaja of that State; and  (c) in relation to any other State specified in Part B of the First Schedule, the person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the <NAME> of the State, and includes in relation to any of the said States any person for the time being recognised by the President as competent to exercise the powers of the <NAME> in relation to that State;"  These two repeals were occasioned by the constitutional readjustment of States when Part B States disappeared.
Statute	The definitions became obsolete after the Reorganization and hence they were deleted.
Statute	Article 366 contained other definitions in (15) and (22) which may be quoted:  "(15) 'Indian State' means any territory which <ORG> recognised as such a State."
Statute	" (22) 'Ruler' in relation to an Indian State means the Prince, Chief or other person by whom any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of  articl e 291 was entered into and who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Ruler of the State, and includes any person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the successor of such Ruler;"  They are intact till today.
RatioOfTheDecision	So also two other Articles, namely, 362 and 363. of these the former does not apply to the State of Jammu and Kashmir, but the latter does.
RatioOfTheDecision	They may be quoted here :  "362.
Statute	In the exercise of the power of <ORG> or of the <ORG> of a State to make laws or in the exercise of the executive power of the <ORG> or of a State due regard shall be had to the guarantee or assurance given under any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of article 291 with respect to the personal rights, privileges and dignities of the Ruler of an Indian State.
Statute	363 (1).
Statute	Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution but subject to the provisions of article 143 , neither <ORG> nor any other court shall have jurisdiction in any dispute arising out of any  provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument which was entered into or executed before the commencement of this Constitution by any Ruler of an Indian State and to which the Government of the Dominion of India or any of its predecessors Governments was a party and which has or has been continued in operation after such com- mencement, or in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of this Constitution relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other smilar instrument.
Statute	(2) In this article--  (a) 'Indian State' means any territory recognised before the commencement of this Constitution by His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as being such a State; and  (b) 'Ruler' includes the Prince, Chief or other person recognised before such commencement of His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as the Ruler of any Indian State."
Statute	42  The in tention behind the definitions in 2(a) and (b) specially included here was to bind even those <ORG> who were recognized before and who might not have been recognised by the President under article 366(22).
Fact	The Indian States formed a significant but separate part of India before they merged with the rest of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is common knowledge that the aim of the Government of India Act, 1935 was to associate the Indian States with British India as equal partners in a loose federation.
RatioOfTheDecision	When India became independent by the Indian Independence Act 1947, British paramountly in respect of the Indian States lapsed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In theory the <ORG> became independent but, as shown above, in actual fact, almost all the <ORG> signed almost immediately, Instruments of Accession in August 1947 surrendering <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> immediately after Independence became divided into four classes :  (a) those who had signed Instruments of Accession;  (b) those who had signed instruments of Merger;  (c) those  who had formed themselves into <ORG> and the <ORG> had signed <ORG>;  (d) Hyderabad, Mysore and Jammu and Kashmir.
RatioOfTheDecision	The merged States were either directly administered by <ORG> as Chief Commissioner's Provinces or were handed over to the neighboring Provinces.
Fact	Thus 216 States merged in the adjoining Provinces, 61 States were converted into centrally administered areas and 275 States formed <ORG>.
Fact	Only three States retained their integrity; but when the Constitution came into force, they too became part of <ORG> on a later date.
Fact	They were Hyderabad, Mysore and Jammu and Kashmir.
Fact	The Indian States covered about 48 per cent of the area of the Indian Dominion.
Fact	The population of this area formed 28 per cent of the total population of the Dominion.
Fact	All the Rulers (including the Rajpramukhs of the <ORG>) issued proclamations of which reference has earlier been made in relation to Gwalior.
Fact	On the merger or integration of the States with <ORG> the Ru lers were left with a Privy Purse and a few of their personal privileges and properties.
Fact	The Privy Purses were fixed with due regard to the incomes of the <NAME>, before integration with a ceiling of Rs.
Fact	10 lakhs.
Fact	Eleven <NAME> were to be paid more than that sum as a personal Privy Purse.
Fact	The total amount of the Privy Purses came to Rs.
Fact	58 crores.
Fact	Today the highest Privy Purse is Rs.
Fact	26 lakhs per annum to the Ruler of Mysore and the lowest is Rs.
Fact	192 per annum to the Ruler of Kotodia.
Fact	43  The Privileges of the <NAME> included many items.
Fact	A memo- randum on these privileges was issued by <ORG> in 1949. it did not contain an exhaustive list but was drawn up to inform Provincial and Union Governments about them.
Fact	It contained an itemised list of 34 privileges.
Fact	They included several exemptions from the operation of Indian Laws, the enjoyment of <NAME> and personal property of the <NAME> and members of their families, the payment by the <ORG> of the marriage expenses of the brothers and sisters of  the <NAME>, immunity from some processes of courts of law, immunity from requisitioning of the private properties of the <NAME> and their families and so on and so forth.
Fact	During the negotiations letters were written to the <NAME> to assure them that the Privy Purse was fixed in prepatuity and the freedoms enjoyed by them would be continued.
Fact	The Privy Purses and the Privileges were continued till 6th September 1970.
Fact	Their payment or enjoyment was a part of the guarantee of the Constitution.
Fact	However <ORG> passed a Resolution on 25th June 1967 for their abolition.
Fact	In furtherance of this resolution <ORG> held several conferences with the representatives of the <NAME>.
Fact	Although shorn of all but a shadow of their former power and panoply, the <NAME> seemed to regard themselves as something different from the people or perhaps, as princes in exile.
Fact	They had their <ORG>, their Intendant-General and Conciliar Committee, thereby evoking a certain measure of hostility among  persons who were oblivious of the constitutional transition in India.
Fact	The summary of the proceedings of these conferences were marked collectively as Annexure A annexed to the affidavit of <ORG>.
Fact	It shows six meetings between November 3, 1967 and January 8, 1970.
Fact	There were perhaps a number of informal meetings and consultations.
Fact	Nothing seems to have been achieved.
Fact	<ORG> repeated their intention of withdrawing the recognition of the Rulers and stoppage of the Privy Purses and Privileges, and was prepared only for a negotiated settlement as to the terms on which the abolition should take place.
Fact	<ORG> was not prepared to enter into any negotiations and were <NAME> of a fresh settlement which might be broken just as simply as the past solemn engagements and assurances.
Fact	The Rulers who, before Independence, had always displayed the sentiment Ego et rex meus had realised that Princes were not the only people in Whose word trust should not be placed.
Fact	The Government of In dia acted rapidly.
Fact	The President in his speech to the <ORG> gave expression to the policy of Government.
Fact	A Resolution recommending the abolition was moved and passed in <ORG>.
Fact	A Bill was then moved in <ORG> intituled.
Fact	The Constitution (Twenty Fourth Amendment) Bill 1970.
Fact	It consisted of three clauses and a short statement of Objects and Reasons.
Fact	'the Statement read :  "The concept of rulership, with Privy Purses and Special Privileges unrelated to any current functions and social purposes, is incompatible with an egalitarian social order.
Fact	Government have therefore decided to terminate the Privy Purses and Privileges of the Rulers of former Indian States.
Fact	Hence this Bill.
Fact	14-5-1970 Y.
Fact	B. CHAVAN"  The Address of the President to the Joint Session of <ORG>, the Resolution above referred to and the Statement of Objects and Reasons all gave identical reasons.
Fact	The Bill was voted upon in <ORG> on September 2, 1970.
Fact	332 votes for and 154 votes against it, were cast.
Fact	It  was considered in <ORG> ,on September 5, 1970 and was defeated, 149 voting for and 75 against it.
Fact	It failed in <ORG> to reach the requisite majority of not less than two-thirds of the members present and voting.
Fact	The Bill originally gave no indication of the date when the Act was to come into operation but in <ORG> an amendment was accepted by which it was to come into force from October 15, 1970.
Fact	By the second clause the Bill omitted Article 291 and 362 of the Constitution and the third clause omitted Article 366(22).
Fact	The same evening the <ORG> is said to have met and to have decided to advise the President to withdraw the recognition of the Rulers.
Fact	The same night the President signed at Hyderabad an instrument withdrawing recognition of all the Rulers.
Fact	Separate orders were.
Fact	issued to all the Rulers on the 6th September 1970 and they were also notified in the <ORG> as already mentioned.
Fact	on September 7, 1970, the Finance Minister laid on the table of <ORG> a statement .
Argument	He claimed that the power of the President to withdraw the recognition of the Rulers was unquestioned and had also been suggested as alternative to the amendment of the Constitution, and that <ORG> was in fact going to use the power after the adoption of the Bill amending the Constitution.
Argument	He gave as his reason for the President's action that the Bill amending the Constitution was lost by a fraction of a vote in one of the <ORG>, that there was widespread support against this out- moded and antiquated system of Privy Purses', that even those who opposed the Bill supported the abolition and that it was <ORG>'s policy to put an and to the concept of Rulership and the abolition of Privy Purses and the Privileges.
Argument	He hinted that arrangements would be made to enable Rulers to make adjustments in the transitory period.
Fact	These petitions were then filed to question the action of the President and the <ORG> of India.
Fact	The petitioners put at the forefront the sentiments expressed at the time of the Merg er of the Indian States.
Fact	The Princes were then described as imbued with imagination, foresight and patriotism and as co-architects of a democratic and united India.
Fact	Sardar <NAME> as the Minister in the newly formed <ORG> made a speech on October 12, 1949 in <ORG> (<ORG>) in which he pointed out that <ORG> alone gave sufficient cash assets which, if invested, would cover payments to the <NAME> as Privy Purses.
Argument	and that the payments to the <NAME> represented one-fourth of what they were previously enjoying.
Argument	He said that there was nothing by which the <NAME> could be forced to merge their States with India and that the Privy Purses were quid pro quo for parting with the ruling power by the <NAME> and the dissolution of their States as separate units.
Argument	He regarded this as a small price for the bloodless revolution and avoidance of mischief.
Argument	He exhorted <ORG> that the Indian Peoples on their part should ensure fully the guarantee g iven to them and concluded:  "Our failure to do so would be a breach of faith and seriously prejudice the stabilization of the New' Order".
Fact	The same sentiments were reiterated by Mr. <NAME> (who was the Secretary to the <ORG>) in his recent book "The Story of the Integration of the Indian States",(1961) pp.
Fact	461 and 462.
Fact	He cataloged the number of villages, palaces, museums, buildings, stables garages, fleets of motor cars, aeroplanes etc.
Fact	surrendered by the <NAME>.
Argument	He pointed out that cash balances were to the tune of Rs.
Argument	77 crores and that palaces in Delhi alone were worth several lakhs of rupees.
Argument	According to him, the price paid as Privy Purses was not too high for integration and indeed it was insignificant when compared with what the <NAME> had lost.
Argument	The petitions are long argumentative documents and the reply affidavit equally so.
Argument	The verbosity of the petitions (which are almost identical) and the reply affidavit (which is common to all petitions) does not render the-task of the <ORG> in this im portant case any the easier.
Argument	It is, therefore, necessary to place in their proper perspective the respective cases of the parties.
Argument	46  According to the petitioners, the failure to amend the Constitution resulted in the retention in it, of the articles relevant to the Rulers' rights.
Argument	These Articles, particularly Articles 291 and 362 continued the obligation of the <ORG> to pay the Privy Purses and also to recognise the Privileges.
Argument	The Privy Purses stood charged on and were to be paid out of <ORG> and even <ORG> could not vote upon them.
Argument	The assurances and guarantees being that of the people in the Constitution, the Executive <ORG> could not by the indirect device of withdrawing the recognition of the Rulers avoid the obligation created by the Constitution.
Argument	These assurances and guarantees of the Constitution, the Accession and Integration were but steps and the fixation of Privy Purses and the recognition of the Privileges was not doubt a historical fact but the guarante es flowed from the Constitution and were independent of the historical fact, and had thus to be carried out according to the constitu- tional provisions.
Argument	They based their claim not on the agreements or the covenants but on the constitutional provisions.
Argument	According to them, the order of the President was in violation of the spirit and meaning of Articles 366(22), 291 and 362 and was an affront to <ORG> which had turned down the move for amendment of these articles.
Argument	The President's action robbed the articles of their content which <ORG> did not allow to be done and thus the order of the President indirectly had the effect of amending the Constitution.
Argument	The President's order itself was said to be <NAME> fide, ultra vires since his power was to recognise a Ruler at a time and for the time being or to withdraw recognition from a Ruler for cogent and valid reasons, naming in his place a successor, and not to withdraw recognition from all Rules en masse for no reason except that the concept of Rulership was co nsidered outmoded or that some persons held the view that it should not be continued.
Argument	According to the petitioners the gaddi of a Ruler had to be filled in accordance with the law and custom of the family and could not be left vacant.
Argument	The vast power to withdraw recognition from all the Rulers at the same time without nominating any successor could not and did not flow from the definition of a Ruler in Article 366(22) which contemplated the Continuance of a Ruler who had signed the Merger Agreement or his successor.
Argument	The President was thus guilty of a breach of his duties under the Constitution and acted outside his jurisdiction.
Argument	The act of the President was thus said to offend Articles 53, 394, 295 291 and 362 of the Constitution.
Argument	In supporting their petition under Article 32 , the petitioners claimed that important questions of deprivation of property and of personal liberty were involved.
Argument	As illustrations the petitioners contended that the right to receive Privy Purses was a right to property of which the R ulers stood deprived as also of other personal properties and benefits of exemptions under diverse laws was also an inroad upon property rights.
Argument	Since there was no authority of law and no compensation, the action was said to offend Articles 19(1)(f) and 31 of the Constitution.
Argument	They also claimed that Government was prevented by promissory estoppel and had acted in breach of a fiduciary duty.
Argument	In the reply affidavit filed by Mr. <NAME> (Joint Secretary in <ORG>) all the allegations and submissions (besides the patent facts) were denied.
Argument	The main contentions in reality were that this <ORG> lacked jurisdiction to enter upon this dispute in view of the express bar of Article 363 that the petitions did not lie as no right of property or personal liberty of the petitioners, was jeopardised and lastly that the action of the President was perfectly valid and binding as it was a political act in the exercise of the sovereignty of India, as to which this <ORG> could say nothing being outside it s jurisdiction. '
Argument	Article 363 , it was claimed, barred the jurisdiction of all <ORG> (including <ORG>) in any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument which was executed before the commencement of the Constitution and to which the Government of the Dominion of India or any of its predecessor Governments was a party and which had,-or had been continued in operation or in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability arising out of any of the provisions of the Constitution relating to any such treaty etc.
Argument	and the present was such a dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the article began with the words "Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution", the article could only be read by itself and even the chapter on Fundamental Rights was excluded.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason given was that these instruments were political agreements between High Contracting Parties and the Municipal <ORG> had no say in matters which were political or  Acts of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Covenants were not self-executing and created imperfect obligations and depended for their enforceability upon the willingness of Governments to im- plement them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the claim was based upon what was recognised in these instruments, this Court could not give any relief as it had no jurisdiction to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President's,powers to recognise a Ruler, which carried with it the power to withdraw such recognition flowed from Article 366(22) and this power being an incident of sovereignty and a political act was not questionable in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The bar of Article 363 covered such a case also because there was nothing to show that any recognition carrying with it a Privy Purse and Privileges was ever intended to be perennial even when the <ORG> policy demanded an abolition.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Privy Purse itself being-in the nature of a political pension, a claim to it was not property and no claim could arise if it was stopped.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 291 did not create any legal right but only laid down the source and me thod of payment of Privy Purse guaranteed by the Dominion of India and even if it were assumed that it was private property or that other property rights were affected by the withdrawal of the recognition, the matter was not justiciable because of the bar of Article 363 which applied to Articles 291 and 362.
RatioOfTheDecision	The pleadings in the case are long but the points are few.
RatioOfTheDecision	The case involves a positive and a negative approach in so far as this <ORG>-is concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	The positive approach involves the consideration of the reliefs that can be granted and the negative approach the bar operating under Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first approach requires consideration of the validity of the action of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that if the action of the President is valid and operative, the implications of that action must necessarily follow.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is invalid, for any reason, then the question of the bar of the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to give relief will arise.
Argument	<ORG> however places the bar at the very threshold and con tents that the dispute is such as is expressly barred by Article 363 but the petitioners contend that there is no dispute at all under Articles 291 and 362 or it is not of the kind contemplated by that Article.
Argument	<ORG> asks that the question of jurisdiction be decided first because in their opinion it is conclusive, while the other side contends that there is no( dispute once the invalidity of' the President's order is established, since articles 291 and 362 would then speak for themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	I intend considering first the question of the validity of the order of the President because everything turns on it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The arguments for and against that action may, therefore, be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to <ORG> the act is political and in the exercise of sovereignty and paramountly.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot, therefore, be questioned in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the petitioners it is not, and is a plain executive order open to question like any other such act and the bar of article 363 does not apply to such a  dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Union government invokes the analogy of the British Crown Paramountcy which lapsed on the Indian Independence.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection the claim is that the provisions of Article 363 and 366(15) and (22) preserve the paramountcy of the Crown in the President.
Precedent	This argument is independent of the question of bar of jurisdiction under Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	It seeks to put the President's act outside the jurisdiction of the <ORG> by reason of the nature of the act.
Precedent	A word may, therefore, be said about the paramountcy of the British Crown and what is meant.
Precedent	Reference was made in this connection by the Attorney General to the White Paper on Indian States, Mr. <NAME>'s book already referred to, and the account contained in a recent book 'The Great Divide' by Mr. <NAME>.
Precedent	He traced the paramountcy of the British Crown in India.
Precedent	I do not consider it necessary to refer to them.
Precedent	The best exposition of British Paramountcy is to be found in a famous letter by Lord Reading Viceroy of India addressed to <NAME> bad when the latter claimed rights of kingship.
Precedent	It is printed as Appendix I to the White Paper.
Precedent	This is what the Viceroy said " The Sovereignty of the British Crown is supreme in India, and therefore no ruler of an Indian State can justifiably claim to negotiate with <ORG> on an equal footing.
Precedent	Its supremacy is not based only upon treaties and engagements, but exists independently of them and, quite apart from its prerogative in matters relating to foreign powers and policies, it is the right and duty of <ORG>, while scrupulously respecting all treaties and engagements with the Indian States, to preserve peace and good order throughout India.
Precedent	The consequences that follow are so well known, and so clearly apply no less to Your Exalted Highness than to other Rulers, that it seems hardly necessary to point them out.
Precedent	But if illustrations are necessary.
Precedent	I would remind Your Exalted Highness that the Ruler of Hyderabad along with other Rulers received in 1862 a <ORG> declaratory of t he British <ORG>'s desire for the perpetuation of his <ORG> and <ORG>, subject to continued loyalty to the Crown : that no succession in the Masnad of Hyderabad is valid unless it is recognised by His Majesty the King-Emperor : and that the British <ORG> is the only arbiter in cases of disputed succession.
RatioOfTheDecision	5.The right of the British <ORG> to intervene in the internal affairs of Indian States is another instance of the consequences necessarily involved in the supremacy of the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	The British <ORG> have indeed shown again and again that they have no desire to exercise this right without grave reason.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the internal, no less than the external security which the Ruling Princes enjoy is due ultimately to the protecting power of the British <ORG>, and where Imperial interests are concerned, or the general welfare of the people of a State is seriously and grievously affected by the action of its <ORG>, it is with <ORG> that the ultimate responsibility of  taking remedial action, if necessary, must lie.
RatioOfTheDecision	The varying degrees of internal sovereignty which the Rulers enjoy are all subject to the due exercise by <ORG> of this responsibility.
RatioOfTheDecision	Other illustrations could be added no less inconsistent than the foregoing with the suggestion that except in matters relating to foreign powers and policies, <ORG> and <ORG> stand on a plane of equality.
RatioOfTheDecision	But I do not think I need pursue the subject further.
Precedent	I will merely add that the title "Faithful Ally" which Your Exalted Highness enjoys has not the effect of putting Your Government in a category separate from that of other States under paramountcy of the British Crown".
Precedent	The 1858 Act had recognised all treaties made by <ORG> with the Rulers, as binding on the Crown.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> in his dispatch of April 30, 1860 recommended the Perpetuation of the rule of the Princes over their States.
Precedent	This was accepted and a special power of adoption was reco gnised and new sanads were issued.
Precedent	The policy of annexation started by Lord <NAME> then ceased.
Precedent	The Ruler could, thereafter, be punished only for extreme bad conduct but even so the territory was not annexed.
Precedent	The Ruler was deposed but a successor was recognized in his place.
Precedent	This position continued down to 1935. in 1927 <ORG> clearly recognised the claim of the Princes that making any transfer of the Crown's rights and obligations in relation to the States, to persons not under the Crown's authority, would be conditional on the agreement of the States.
Precedent	This was particularly directed against an Indian Government responsible, to the Indian Legislature.
Precedent	To keep <ORG> away from the States, after the advent of the Government of India Act, 1935 the old political department under the charge of the Governor-General disappeared.
Precedent	Previously the Governor-General's Executive Council had left the States entirely to the Governor- General.
Precedent	The Act of 1935 formed the basis of a personal rela tionship between the States and the rest of India.
Precedent	This meant a reversal of the policy and the British Indian Executive was slowly deprived of all constitutional status vis-a-vis the States.
Precedent	A <ORG> Representative was introduced as the link between the States and British India.
Precedent	The Government of India Act 1935 had visualised a federation between British India and the Indian States but that scheme did not materialise.
Precedent	The Indian States were anxious to create sovereign States but the <ORG> prerogatives in respect of them continued.
Precedent	Lord <NAME>'s declaration promised no commitment about the States without their consent in any future constitution that the Indians might frame for themselves.
Precedent	This was implemented by instructions to the Governor-General not to hand over paramountcy to the future Indian Government and paramountcy, so long as it lasted was that of the <ORG> and not of <ORG>.
Precedent	When the Constitution came paramountcy had already lapsed.
Precedent	The Indian States were able to make several re servations in their own favour.
Precedent	They were anxious to frame their own Constitutions but many States could not withstand pressure of <ORG> and thought better of merging.
Precedent	with such reservations as the Merger Agreements made in their favour.
Precedent	The other States like Hyderabad, Mysore and Jammu & Kashmir on the one hand and the United States or <ORG> on the other also dropped the idea of separate Constitutions for themselves (except Jammu & Kashmir) and integrated with India, accepting the Indian Constitution.
Precedent	The Rulers were allowed to get a Privy Purse free of taxes on income and to enjoy personal property and privileges.
Precedent	Articles 291, 362, 366(15) and (22) were included to recognise those conditions on which surender of power had taken place.
Precedent	Article 363 was included to keep certain matters away from Courts and now the most important question is what was granted to the Rulers by the Constitution and how for their rights could be enforced in <ORG>.
Precedent	Paramountcy as such was no more  as there was no paramount power and no vassal.
Precedent	The <NAME> had lost their territories and their right to rule and administer them.
Precedent	They were left only a recognition of their original title, a privy purse, their private properties and a few privileges.
Precedent	These rights were the only indicia of their former soveignty but they enjoyed them by the force of the Constitution although in every respect they were ordinary citizens and not potentates.
Precedent	The paramountcy which the <ORG> exercised over them was different.
Precedent	Then the <ORG> had an absolute freedom to make and unmake <NAME> in the exercise of paramountcy.
Precedent	The Constitution ensured the position of the Ruler and his successor with regard to the Privy Purse and privileges, although leaving the President the right, to confer that status on a Ruler by recognition.
Precedent	This result was reached by treaties, covenants and agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The source or origin of paramountcy of the <ORG> was not the treaties, sanads or agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	There were no paramountcy rights by reason of which the  British were paramount but because they were paramount, therefore, they had paramountcy rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	When paramountcy lapsed it did not fall on the shoulders of Indian Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to recognise a Ruler from out of several claimants was not an act of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The selection had to be in accordance with law and custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was not the arbitrary power which made the conferral of Rulership a gift from the Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no provision to that effect in the Constitution or even the Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	That the Constitution gave the right to the President to recognise a Ruler for the time being, is apparent enough but it cannot be stretched to give a paramountcy of the same character as that enjoyed by the British crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	To claim such a parmountcy one has to ignore completely the arrangements by which the Rulers parted with their territories and Ruling rights and were assured of their Privy Purses and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	These rights became constitutionally protected rights which so long as the Ruler's line was  not extinct belonged to the Ruler for the time being.
RatioOfTheDecision	In one sentence when the guarantees were given by the Constitution, paramountcy, if any, went out.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it was intended that rightful claims could be disregarded, at any time, a very clear provision authorising that they be overridden would have been included.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand article 362 says in admonitory terms that in the exercise of the power of <ORG> or of the <ORG> of a State to make laws or in the exercise of executive power of the <ORG> or, of a State, due regard shall be had to the guarantee or assurance given in any such Covenant or Agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of Article 291 with respect to the personal rights, privileges, and dignities of the Ruler of an Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	This provision is rather the converse of paramountcy in as much as it compels the two limbs of Government to have 'due regard' to the guarantees and assurances given to the Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be no paramountcy against a citizen of India and the Rulers today  are not potentiates they were.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are citizens of India like other citizens albeit with some privileges and privy purses which other citizens do not get.
Precedent	That is an accident of history and with the concurrence of the Indian People in their <ORG>.
Precedent	The power that has been exercised against them must, therefore, be justified under the Constitution and the laws and not by invoking a nebulous doctrine of paramountcy which Lord <NAME> describes in his Dictionary of English Law thus The relationship of the Sovereign as Emperor of India to the rulers of the native States, terminated by the Indian Independence Act, 1947".
Precedent	The Attorney General contended that article 363 ' recreated' paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article was intended to keep certain matters outside the jurisdiction of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must be construed according to its own terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	No meaning, beyond what the words convey, can be attributed to those words by resorting to the imperial doctrine.
RatioOfTheDecision	What those words mean I shall consider later but I reject  the claim that the President or <ORG> can invoke the doctrine to sustain an illegal inroad upon the rights of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor is the argument that this was some kind of an 'act of <ORG>' of any more validity.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> has ruled on more than one occasion that an act of <ORG>' is not available against a citizen.
RatioOfTheDecision	An act of <ORG> is a sovereign act which is neither grounded on law nor does it pretend to be so.
Precedent	It was described by me, quoting from <ORG> v. Secretary of <ORG> for India(1) as 'a catastrophic change constituting a new departure', in the <CITATION_1838105> (2).
Precedent	I have not been able to better that expression.
Precedent	I further pointed out that 'in civil commotion or even in war or peace, the <ORG> cannot act 'catastrophically' outside the ordinary law and there is legal remedy for its wrongful 'acts against its own subjects or even a friendly alien within the <ORG>".
Precedent	I may again reaffirm the observations in that case 'based upon the statement of the law by Lord <NAME> down in Secretary of <ORG> in Council for <CITATION_1024380> (3).
Precedent	This is what I Said:  "The question thus ,Is always : Did the <ORG> or its agents purport to act "catastrophically' or subject to the ordinary course of the Law?
Precedent	This question was posed in Secretary of <ORG> in Council for India v. <NAME> by Lord <NAME> in these words :  "What was the real character of the act done in this case?
Precedent	Was it a seizure by arbitrary power on behalf of the <ORG> of Great Britain, of the dominations and property of a neighbouring <ORG>, an act not affecting to justify itself on grounds of Municipal Law?
Precedent	Or was it, in whole or in part, a possession taken by the <ORG> under colour of legal title of the property of the late <NAME> of Tanjore, in trust for those who, by law, might be entitled to it on the death of the last possessor?
Precedent	If it were the latter, the defence set up, of course, has no foundation."
RatioOfTheDecision	The defence is not available if there is only a colour of legaltitle against a citizen.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that event, t he action must fail unless supported by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since there are no sovereign or political powers under our Constitution every action of the Executive limb of Government must seek justification in some law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very existence of article 363 , which is said to incorporate some kind of (1)  I K.B.
Precedent	613 at 640 (2)  I S.C.R.
Precedent	537 at 544 (3)  13 Moore P.C.
Precedent	22.
Precedent	54  paramountcy or act of <ORG>,, shows that there is, no political power outside the law, otherwise an additional bar would hardly have been necessary.
Precedent	The learned Attorney General when faced by the rulings on the act of <ORG> of this Court and the English Courts, gave up the attempt for justification as such and pleaded that the Covenants and Agreements created 'imperfect obligations'.
Precedent	The phrase.
Precedent	'imperfect obligations' is more often to be met with in the Law of Contract but it was applied by <ORG> to political treaties in <ORG> of <ORG> v. <ORG>).
Precedent	There the claim was made by a retired,' Military Off icer for pension against the Directors of <ORG> based on certain treaties.
Precedent	It was held that such agreements lacked vinculum juris.
Precedent	The Phrase 'imperfect obligations' was thus used in regard to individuals as subjects of international rights and duties.
Precedent	The recognition in an international treaty or other instrument of rights inuring to the benefit of individuals other than the parties to the agreement, is sometimes 'held not to confer the right of enforceability at the instance of such other persons.
Precedent	Therefore, the beneficiary under these rights cannot take measures to enforce them by his own independent steps.
Precedent	In the <ORG> <ORG> observed "It is scarcely necessary to point out that the capacity to possess civil rights does not necessarily imply the capacity to exercise those rights oneself".
Precedent	Thus a rule of International Law formerly held the field that persons holding such rights are incapable of asserting them in the internat ional sphere or in <ORG>.
Precedent	The instrument may make them owners of rights and yet take away the remedy from them.
Precedent	This is the sense in which <NAME> used the phrase 'lacking in vinculum jurists This position has now altered and there is a rethinking on the subject.
Precedent	It is now gradually gaining recognition that if there be 'some municipal legislation giving enforceability to the right, then the right can be claimed in <ORG>.
Precedent	This change of view followed the Advisory Opinion of <ORG> in the jurisdiction of <ORG> in the matter of <ORG> officials in Danzing.
Precedent	(I) The rights given by a treaty received a broader acceptance there.
Precedent	This case gave an exposition of the.
Precedent	rights of individuals in the international (3) Advisory Opinion No.
Precedent	15, series B No.
Precedent	15.
Precedent	55  sphere and <ORG>.
Precedent	The argument of Poland in the case was that the agreement between Poland and Danzing regulating the conditions of employment of the <ORG> offi cials taken over in <ORG>, created rights only between Poland and Danzing and as that agreement was not incorporated in the laws of Poland, it created no rights for individuals, and that <ORG> had no jurisdiction to decide in respect of those rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Court did not accept the contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	It observed "It may be readily admitted that, according to a well-established principle of international law, the Beam-tenabkommen, being an international agreement, cannot, as such, create direct rights and obligations for private individuals.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it cannot be disputed that the very object of an international agreement, according to the intention of the contracting parties, may be the adoption by the Parties of some definite rules, creating individual rights and-  obligations and enforceable by the national <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That there is such an intention in the present case can be established by reference to the terms of the <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	(Page 17) Before dealing with the position of the Rulers themselv es, let me illustrate the application of this observation in our country in relation to third parties, safeguarded by an international agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Covenants and Merger Agreements contained clauses guaranteeing continuance of service to the Civil Servants and of their pensions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those Civil servants would not have been able to enforce these agreements in Municipal Courts by their own individual steps if there was no law or the rights were not otherwise recognised.
RatioOfTheDecision	But when they shared with the Civil servants of the former British India, the benefits of Articles 309-311 of the Constitution and the Rules governing such services, it is not possible to deny to them the benefits that the Constitution and the Rules confer.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Covenants, cannot then be said to create 'imperfect obligations' since the Constitution takes the matter into itself and gives them is own guarantees.
RatioOfTheDecision	The individual rights and obligations no doubt originally flowed from a contract between High Contracting Parties and might not have create a  vinculum juris in favour of third parties but the Constitution having granted rights and created corresponding obligations, those rights and obligations are enforceable in our <ORG>.
Precedent	This Court has ruled on many occasions that a recognition of rights by law or otherwise makes them _justiciable : see for example <CITATION_1726974> (1).
Precedent	(1)  7 S.C.R.

RatioOfTheDecision	To sustain the President& act repudiating the rights and obligations on the basis of a discarded theory of 'imperfect obligations' would drain the constitution and th e. laws of their efficacy by an executive act without amendment of the Constitution or the laws and that cannot be permitted.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is not a right for enforcement in foro Conscientiae to make good, or of which the performance could only be sught for by petition, memorial or remonstrance.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is a ,case for an action in <ORG> if the dispute is not barred by the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore there is no bar to the jurisdiction of this Court except that created by Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ambit of that bar will be worked out by me on the terms of that article later but before that bar can be applied one must know what is it that is in controversy here.
RatioOfTheDecision	The main dispute is as to the validity of the action of the President in withdrawing recognition from the <NAME> without an exception.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioners question the power, authority and jurisdiction of the President to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	They characterise the act as <NAME> fide, ultra vires and therefore a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	I will consider the matter in the same order.
RatioOfTheDecision	The charge of  <NAME> fide action in this connection can only mean want of good faith.
RatioOfTheDecision	Good faith according to the definition in the General Clauses Act means a thing which is in fact done honestly, whether it is done negligently or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words an act done honestly must be deemed to, be done in good faith.
Precedent	Mr. <NAME> described the act as wanting in good faith and relying on many cases contended that want of good faith must avoid the act.
Precedent	It is hardly necessary to refer to those cases here as it is well-settled that lack of bona fides unravels every transaction.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that it is open to Mr. <NAME> to describe the act as wanting in good faith without pleading any collateral fact.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further it is not open to me to probe the reasons for a decision by the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	To begin with under Article 74(2) the question, whether any and if so what, advice was tendered by the Ministers to the President cannot be inquired into by any Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again by Article 361(1) the President is not answerable to any Court for the e xercise and performance of the powers and duties of his office or for any act done or purporting to be done by him in the exercise of those powers and duties except in an investigation of a charge under Article 61.
RatioOfTheDecision	All that is saved is that appropriate proceedings against the government of India can be taken.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, whether the President acted rightly or wrongly in the matter may be decided against <ORG> without questioning the conduct of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the only question open is whether the act of the President was ultra vires the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question of ultra vires was put thus by the petitioners " An executive exercise of power must be in accordance with the Constitution under Article  53.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 362 says that the President must exercise the power with due regard to the guarantees and assurances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President in his action has completely disregarded Articles 291 and 362 and by withdrawing the recognition of the Princes has acted ultra vires the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under A rticle 73 the Executive power of the Union is co-terminous with the law making power of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	When <ORG> refused to amend the Constitution, the President's power did not extend that far by executive action.
RatioOfTheDecision	By his executive act the President has denuded articles 291 and 362 of their content for ever.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President was required to recognise the <NAME> and has with one stroke withdrawn the recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	He is trying to do indirectly what <ORG> refused to dot directly that is to say remove articles 291,362 and 366(22) from the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This has, been done without a hearing to the <NAME> and is in breach of accepted principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule of law prevails and no unconstitutional act of any authority, unsupported by law, can avail(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	The action is not only against the Constitution but it also affects a large body of tax and other concessions.
Precedent	Prominent among them are Wealth Tax Act 1957 Ss.2(p) and 5(1)(iii), Gift Tax Act 1958 S. 5(1)(xiv) , Hindu Succession Act , 1956 S. 5(ii i) , Income- tax Act 1922 Section 4(3) (x); Income-tax Act 1961 S. 10(19) , Estate Duty Act 1953, Section 33(1)(1) ; Part B States (Taxation Concessions) Order 1950, Clause 15.

Precedent	454, 460.
Precedent	58  intended that these laws would be rendered nugatory by the expedient of removing the Princes."
Precedent	The power to withdraw recognition from a Ruler is claimed by the Attorney General to be implicit in Article 366(22) be- cause it defines a Ruler in terms of recognition 'for the time being' by the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also contented that the power to recognise, itself includes the power to withdraw recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, necessary to see how far the President can go on the words of the ar ticle.
RatioOfTheDecision	The critical words in the articles are 'for the time being.'
RatioOfTheDecision	These words show that the Ruler is a person, who, to be considered as a Ruler must, at any given moment of time, be recognised by the President whether he be the original signatory of a Covenant or Agreement or his successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words thus indicate that only one person at a time can be recognised as the Ruler of a State.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also shows a continuity of succession so that an interregnum is avoided.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does show that Rulership cannot be permanent since the continuance as Ruler depends upon the continuance of the recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the definition neither expressly nor by implication places the power in the hands of the President to say that although a Ruler was in existence or a successor was available that, there shall be no Ruler of any particular State.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a power does not flow from the definition which contemplates the existence of a Ruler for the time being.
RatioOfTheDecision	The phrase 'for the time being' cannot mean that any person can be appointed who has  no claim whatever or that temporary appointments may be made or that no appointment need be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	The continuity of a Ruler of an Indian State is obligatory so long as the Ruler is alive or a successor can be found.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that where the line becomes extinct (as happened in some cases) or no suitable successor could be found that no Ruler need be recognised.
RatioOfTheDecision	But where the Ruler exists or there is a suitable successor the power to recognise a Ruler is implicit just as much as the power to withdraw recognition in suitable cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> cannot escape this obligation by invoking paramountcy or some state policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obligation to recognise a Ruler is bound up with the other guarantees contained in articles 291 and 362.
RatioOfTheDecision	The definition in article 366(22) is merely the key to find a particular Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The withdrawal of recognition from all the Rulers renders the guarantees as also the relevant articles of the Constitution inoperative.
RatioOfTheDecision	It could never be the intention of the Constitution that by an Ex ecutive act the operation of those articles would come to a stop.
RatioOfTheDecision	The action of the President has the indirect effect not only of abrogating these articles but also of rendering certain provisions in the Income-tax Act , Wealth Tax Act , the Gift Tax Act , the Codes of Civil and Criminal Procedures etc., completely otiose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Executive action can never be allowed to have that effect unless the power is explicitly conferred.
RatioOfTheDecision	The intention of Article 366(22) is exactly the converse of what <ORG> understands it to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	The answer of the first question is that the power of the President was wholly outside Article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	However wide that power, it does not extend to withdrawing recognition of all the Rulers by a mid-night order.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President was incompetent to, do so and, therefore, his act must be treated as a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	This question is independent of Article 363 and has no bearing upon: any Covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	It relates only to the power of the President in behalf of recognition of Rulers and withdrawa l of recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Court is, therefore, free from Article 363 to consider whether the act can be sustained or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Article only applies to acts within the four comers of the Article and not to acts wholly outside.
RatioOfTheDecision	I will show later how that bar can operate on Article 366(22) when I consider Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the present I state my conclusion that having considered the matter I am satisfied that the act must be declared to be ultra vires and a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	This, answers the first ground of attack in favour of the petitioners The question,, is however, reserved for answer whether I am barred by article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before I consider the matter from the angle of the Articles of the Constitution bearing upon the controversy and the bar of Article 363 I wish to dispose of one, matter which is also, in a manner of speaking, a bar at the very threshold.
RatioOfTheDecision	That bar would arise if this is not a petition coveted by Article 32.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioners seem to base their claim to relief on four grounds  (a) That the order of the Pres ident is a nullity;  (b) that by the order of the President their privy puruses are stopped and that is an infringement articles 19(1)(f) and 31;  (c) that the order also deprives them of their privileges and some are property rights and some affect personal liberty; and  (d) that statutory rights under certain statutes (already mentioned above) are destroyed and they result in deprivation of property through illegal taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is sufficient for this purpose to find out if any right of property is involved.

RatioOfTheDecision	A preliminary point arises under article 19 whether the <NAME> can be regarded as citizens.
