RatioOfTheDecision	I shall show later that the obligation to pay the Privy Purse to a Ruler is absolute and the right to claim it when due subsists in each Ruler.
Argument	This is a  petition for the enforcement of Fundamental Right to property and therefore the petitioners must show that a right to property is infringed or is in imminent danger of being threatened.
Argument	The learned Attorney General questioned the competency of these petitions and the claim that property rights were involved.
Argument	According to him the right is one to continue to receive a payment de futuro and no more.
RatioOfTheDecision	A right to receive payment is not, according to him, a right to property.
RatioOfTheDecision	The attempt is to equate the periodic payments as being in the nature of payments of debts.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that this creates a right in personam and not a right in rem.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, there is enforcement of an obligation in personam but not a right to reach property which can be said to belong to the <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not accept the contention of the learned Attorney-General.
Precedent	(1) [1954] 5 S.C.R.
Precedent	1, at page 6 (2) [1961] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	957 (3) [1968] 3 S.C.R.
Precedent	489 In his summary of the Law of Contract (p. 124) <NAME> re- marked that 'a debt...... according to t he popular conception of the term, is a sum of money belonging to one person (the creditor), but in the possession of another (the debtor).
Argument	He questioned this approach.
Argument	<NAME> contrasted property in possession and property in action and held contracts to be within the latter(1).
Argument	He was in effect thinking of a debt.
Argument	According to him property in action exists :  "Where a man hath not the occupation, but merely a bare right to occupy the thing in question, the possession whereof may, however, be, recovered by a suit or action at law.........".
Argument	He was of opinion that till then the thing or its equivalent, remains in suspense, and the injured party has only the right and not the occupation.
RatioOfTheDecision	It being a thing in potentia and not in esse it is only a thing in action and not possession.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> (The <ORG>) also says that till the fulfilment of the obligations the creditor has right only against the debtor and not against a thing.
RatioOfTheDecision	This old concept of property is no longer held to be true.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mark by (2) regards the  liability of the promisor as itself a thing which is capable of being bought and sold, assigned and transferred and if of money value, may itself be regarded as an object of ownership.
RatioOfTheDecision	An obligation according to him is as much a res as any other property and the only difference is in the mode of enjoyment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The creditor realizes this ownership by compelling the debtor to perform his obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	As illustration he gives a catalogue of passive 'rights of ownership.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> (Principles of Law Contract) supports him by pointing out that an obligation is a right of control exercisable by one person over others for acts which have a money value.
RatioOfTheDecision	The dynamic theory of obligations regards a debt as a claim to 'an equivalent in a value to a floating charge against the generality of things which are the properties of the debtor'.
RatioOfTheDecision	From this is developed the notion of a credit-debt where property rights arise from a promise, express or implied in respect of ascertained or readily ascertained sums of money.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus a debt or  a liability to pay money passes through four stages.
RatioOfTheDecision	First there is a debt not 'yet due.
RatioOfTheDecision	The debt has not yet become a part of the obligor's 'things' because no net liability has yet arisen.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second stage is when the liability may have arisen but is not either ascertained or admitted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here again the amount due has not become a part of the obligor's things.
Precedent	The third stage is reached when the liabi-  (1) (See Commentaries Vol.
Precedent	II XXv pp.
Precedent	396-398) (2) (Elements of Law 1871 6th Edition p. 320) lity is both ascertained and admitted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then it is property proper of the debtor in the creditor's hands.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law begins to recognise such property in insolvency, in dealing with it in fraud of creditors, fraudulent preference of one creditor against another, subrogation, equitable estoppel, stoppage intransitu etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	A credit-debt is then a debt fully provable and which is fixed and absolutely owing.
RatioOfTheDecision	The last stage is when the debt becomes a judgment debt by reason of a decree of a Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus an American Judge held 'outs tanding uncollected accounts' as property.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1843482> (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because of this that the French Law includes such obligations in mobiles.
RatioOfTheDecision	Applying these tests to the Privy Purses, it is clear that they would be property.
RatioOfTheDecision	As soon as an Appropriation Act is passed there is established a credit-debt and the outstanding Privy Purse becomes the property of the Ruler in the hands of Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also a sum certain and absolutely payable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Attorney-General however contends that Article 291 which charges the Privy Purse on the Consolidated Fund of India, to be paid to the Ruler, free of all taxes on income does not provide that it shall be paid and, therefore, the Article only lays down the source and manner of payment but creates no right to claim, receive or enforce payment.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my judgment this is a complete misreading of the Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word 'charged', is a term of art.
RatioOfTheDecision	In general law 'a charge' creates a pledge and also a priority in payment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word also denotes in <ORG> pra ctice non- votability.
RatioOfTheDecision	The latter meaning distinguishes it from those items which are payable indiscriminately from the same fund.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result of charging a sum on <ORG> is to provide that its destination shall not be altered even by vote of <ORG> and the charging is sufficiently effective for ensuring the right application.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also sometime,; creates priorities as in the Constitutions of some other countries.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution numerous items of payment are charged on <ORG> but no priority inter.
RatioOfTheDecision	se is established.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet Article 291 makes the.......... amount payable to the Ruler and, therefore, creates a right in him to demand it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words of the Article are 'shall be charged on and paid out of etc'.
Statute	The article makes the payment obligatory.
Statute	The words when expanded read 'shall be charged on and shall be paid out of etc'.
RatioOfTheDecision	the direction to pay is in no uncertain terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article is thus self-ordaining.
RatioOfTheDecision	The recipient is mentioned in (b) where (1) 123 La 717, 720.
RatioOfTheDecision	63  the  Article says 'and the sums so paid to any Ruler' and this shows who is to be paid.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the article in addition to the source and manner also lays down that it shall be paid and paid free of taxes on income to the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Article thus not only creates a liability but also a right in the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is self-supporting and self- ordaining.
Argument	The learned Attorney-General contends that even accepting all this as a valid construction of the article 291 of the Constitution, the petitioners must fail because they are seeking either to enforce the Covenants and Agreements or on seeking to enforce a provision of the Constitution relating to such Covenants and Agreements.
Argument	The same argument is also raised in respect of articles 362 and 366(22).
Argument	According to him the petitioners stand excludedby Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the crux of the case before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	The answer to this question depends on the meaning to be attributed to the four article in question, and determines the fate of these petitions.
RatioOfTheDecision	I begin with article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article was quoted in extenso earlier.
Argument	The learned Attorney-General used the historical events as background for his contention that Article 363 must be construed as giving an exclusive right of determination to the President on the subject of recognition and withdrawal of recognition.
Argument	He submitted that just as an act of <ORG> cannot be questioned in <ORG> so also the withdrawal of recognition cannot be called in question.
Argument	He cited a large number of authorities in support of his case that an act of <ORG> is not subject to the scrutiny of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question here is not one of an act of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor can any assurance be drawn from the doctrine of act of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	What we have to do is to construe the article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It bars jurisdiction of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It, has no bearing upon the rights of the Rulers as such.
RatioOfTheDecision	It neither increases.
RatioOfTheDecision	nor reduces those rights by an iota.
RatioOfTheDecision	I shall presently attempt to find out its meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before I do so I must ,say that it is a well-known rule of interpretation of provisions  barring the jurisdiction of Civil <ORG> that they must be strictly construed for the exclusion of the jurisdiction of <ORG>, and least of- all <ORG>, is not to be lightly inferred.
RatioOfTheDecision	The gist of the present dispute is whether the article bars the relief to the petitioners although as held by me, the order of the President is ultra vires.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article commences with the opening words 'notwithstand- ing anything in this Constitution'.
RatioOfTheDecision	These exclusionary words are no doubt potent enough to exclude every consideration arising from the other provisions of the Constitution including the Chapter on Fundamental Rights, but for that reason alone we must determine the scope of the articles strictly.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article goes on to say that jurisdiction of all <ORG> including <ORG> is barred except that the President may consult <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having said this the articles goes on to specify the matters on which the _jurisdiction is barred.
RatioOfTheDecision	This it does in two parts.
Statute	The first part is : 'in a ny dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty etc.
Statute	which was entered into or executed before the commencement of this Constitution by any Ruler of an Indian State to which <ORG> was a party and which has or has been continued in operation after such commencement'.
RatioOfTheDecision	This shows that a dispute relating to the enforcement, interpretation or breach of any treaty etc.
RulingByPresentCourt	is barred from the <ORG>' jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words 'arising out of the provisions, of a treaty etc.'
RatioOfTheDecision	limit the words.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus if a treaty, covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	is characterised as forged by any party, that would not be a dispute ,arising out of any provision of a treaty, covenant etc.. That dispute would be whether there is a genuine treaty or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	This illustration is given by me to show that the exclusion is not all-embracing.
RatioOfTheDecision	The dispute to be barred must be arise from a provision of the treaty etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second part .bars the <ORG>' jurisdiction 'in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or  obligation arising out of any of the provisions of this Constitution relating to any such treaty etc.'
RatioOfTheDecision	Here the dispute must be in respect of a right which accrues under a provision of the Constitution or the liability or obligation must arise similarly from a provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words 'provisions of this Constitution' are not left unqualified.
RatioOfTheDecision	They could not be left unqualified for then the latter part would have barred every dispute from the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions had to be pointed out.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article however does not refer to any article by its number.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the article had said 'in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of articles 291,362 and 366(22)' all controversy in this case would have been at an end.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the article uses a qualifying phrase which does not name but describes the provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	A search has, therefore, to be made with a view to determining which provision answers the description and which does not.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, we have to satisfy- ourselve s, before we deny out jurisdiction, that of the articles 291,362,366(22) which one, or all of them answer the description.
RatioOfTheDecision	The requirement is that the article must be a provision 'relating to' a treaty covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	I must therefore examine each of the three articles 291,362,366(22) to discover if all of them and, if not, which of them would fit in with those words.
RatioOfTheDecision	65  The learned Attorney-General practically read every word through some dictionary or other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words are 'relating to.
RatioOfTheDecision	They mean that the provisions must bear upon treaties etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	as its dominant purpose or theme.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not sufficient if the treaties etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	are mentioned there for some collateral purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the course of arguments I illustrated my meaning by referring to Article 102 which provides that a person is disqualified if he is an undischarged insolvent 'and asked the question whether the provision could be said to be relating to 'membership' or to 'insolvency' and got obvious answer that it is the former.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that it mentions ' insolvency', 'insanity' etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	does not make it any other than a provision relating to membership of parliament.
RatioOfTheDecision	The dominant purpose and theme of the article is one and one only and that has to be discovered before one can say that it is 'relating to' this or that.
RatioOfTheDecision	A similar illustration is to be found in article 105 (3) where a provision is to be found relating to powers etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	of Indian Parliament and not to those of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, in trying to find out whether any provision is `relating to' a treaty etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is not enough to find a mention of treaty etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	That may be for a subsidiary purpose, not sufficient to qualifiy for consideration as the dominant theme.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the dominant purpose and theme which alone determines the quality of the provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	I shall now apply this test to article 291 ,362 and 366(22) beginning with article 362 since to my mind it is the plainest of all and is definitely within the description.
RatioOfTheDecision	It provide directly for the enforcement of guarantees and assurances by requi ring <ORG>, the <ORG> and the <ORG> Union and the States to have 'due regard' for those guarantees and assurances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article can only be used to support a claim to rights, privileges and dignities.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its dominant theme is the rights, privileges and dignities of the Rulers under Covenants and Agreements and therefore, the provision is 'relating to' Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reference to Article, 291 does not influence the application of the test to Article 291 because that is merely a legislative device and does not tie the two Articles together.
RatioOfTheDecision	It only saves repetition of certain phrases already used in Article 291.
RatioOfTheDecision	If Article 362 were earlier in the Constitution the phrase would have occurred in it and would have been referred to in the other article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore no conclusion can be drawn from this description in Article 362.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore article 362 is one of the provision relating to a treaty, covenant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	A litigant invoking its aid really relies on a provision relatin g to a Coveant etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	I shall now consider Article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	That is only a de- finition clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is intended to point out who is the Ruler of which State.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does so by saying that a Ruler is a person  (a) who entered into a Covenant or Agreement before the commencement of the Constitution and the payment of any sum free of tax had been guaranteed or assured by <ORG> as privy purse or (b) the successor of such Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	For purposes of (a) the same repetition is again avoided by the same legislative device of referring to article 291 for brevity.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Article renders the certainty of assumption of Rulership to depend upon recognition and that recognition is worked out primarily under Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The.
RatioOfTheDecision	,dominant and immediate purpose and application of the Article depends upon Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have earlier said that the President in recognising a Ruler or withdrawing his re- cognition does not act arbitrarily but in the light of Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	A ll such instruments mention law and custom of the family except the Bhopal Agreement where a local statute has to be observed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The selection of a Ruler's successor thus has to be worked out under a Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Article, therefore, has for its dominant purpose the selection of Rulers through the application of the Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the President has exercised his jurisdiction and power to recognise ,a Ruler according to his understanding of the implications of a ,Covenant etc, no one else has jurisdiction to enter upon the same question unless it can be proved that the act was null and void in toto.
RatioOfTheDecision	When the President acts within the four corners of his authority the matter is barred by Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this were not so then the recognition of a Ruler or successor by the President would be subject to further confirmation by the <ORG> and that is not the meaning of article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	During the course of arguments I pointed out that if <NAME> Jhind were recognised as the <NAME>  of Hyderabad, there would be no application of Article 366(22) and the action so wholly arbitrary as not to be protected by Article  363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The answer was that the President would never do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	But who would have thought in 1950 that recognition of.
RatioOfTheDecision	all the Rulers would be withdrawn by a single order?
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, extreme examples are necessary to solve extreme cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have questioned the action of the President because the bar of Article 363 does not operate.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither is the recognition of an original signatory of a Covenant or Agreement involved, nor the recognition of a successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	The act is not even one which the Court leaves alone because the dis- cretion is exercised in a manner and to the extent a President in the proper discharge of his functions can go.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is done is to take away recognition from all Rulers and as such power does not flow from Article 366(22 ), the bar of Article 363 does not apply to such a dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	It arises neither from the Covenants etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	nor from the ,provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It ceases to have the protection of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 363 immediately follows Article 362.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although not much significance can be attached to the collocation of the articles, it is to be noticed that the exclusionary article wants us to search for a provision relating to a treaty etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	before staying our hands.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does leave the matter open when it could have ruled out the mystery by naming the articles under which relief was to be barred.
RatioOfTheDecision	By applying the test, I have indicated, the provision is located.
RatioOfTheDecision	One such provision is article 366(22) when the President acts within the discretion given by Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	There remains Article 291 to consider.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article was read and re-read before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every word in that article was commented upon and dictionaries were consulted.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not propose to refer to dictionaries at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words of the article are plain enough to me and I have only to discover its dominant and immediate purpose or theme to say whether it is a provision relating to Covenants or  Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	It, no doubt, begins by mentioning Covenants and Agreements but that is not all.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot from that fact alone bar ourselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	The relationship between the dominant purpose of the provision and the Covenants and Agreements still remains to be established and their involvement in the dispute must be found.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection we must ask the question : Is this provision in reality and substance a provision on the subject matter of Covenants and Agreements?
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not enough that it refers to the Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should make them the subject matter of enactment and decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Article when carefully analysed leads to these conclu- sions : The main and only purpose of the provision is to charge Privy Purses on the Consolidated Fund of India and make obligatory their payment free of taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	It narrows the guarantee of <ORG> from freedom from all taxes to freedom only from taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	Earlier I had occasion to show that the Princes had guaranteed to themselv es their Privy Purses free of all taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Dominion' Government had guaranteed or assured the same freedom.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution limits the freedom to taxes on income and creates a charge on the Consolidated Fund.
RatioOfTheDecision	There were other guarantees as in the Merger Agreements of Bilaspur and Bhopal (quoted earlier) which are ignored by the Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	The guarantee of the Dominion Gov- ernment is thus continued in a modified form.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reference to Covenants and Agreements is casual and subsidiary.
RatioOfTheDecision	The immediate and dominant purpose of the provision is to ensure payment of Privy Purses, to charge them on the Consolidated Fund and to make them free of taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument of the learned Attorney-General that it indicates only the source and manner of payment rather destroys the case for the application of article 363 than lends support to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mention therein of Covenants and Agreements is for its own purpose so that the amounts need not be specified.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection there is no difference between Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	290A  and 291 although the learned Attorney-General made much of the mention of the name of <ORG> in the former and absence of the names of the <NAME> in the latter, or again the mention of a specific sum in the former and no sum in the latter.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article is self sustaining and self- ordaining.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its purpose is not relating to Covenants etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	but to something else.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 291 differs from Articles 362 and 366(22) in this that the Privy Purses have already been settled and one has not to enforce the Covenants at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	One does not enforce the Covenants but the mandate of the Article itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whenever the Privy Purse is modified under the terms of a Covenant, the Article is again invoked- ab extra.
RatioOfTheDecision	That dispute isnot related to Article 291 and the bar of Article 363 operates.
RatioOfTheDecision	That matter is outside the jurisdiction of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only when the Privy Purse is a settled fact of which the <ORG> can take notice, without having to construe the Covenants for itself that the bar of Article 363 is avoid ed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case the Article does not answer the description of 'a dispute' or of the latter part of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	My conclusions on articles 291, 362, and 366(22) are that article 291 is not a provision relating to Covenants and Agreements but a special provision for the source of payment of privy purses by charging them on <ORG> and for making the payment free of taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not in its dominant purpose and theme answer the description in the latter part of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 362 is within the bar of Article 363 because its dominant purpose is to get recognised the Covenants and Agreements with Rulers However, in so far as the same guarantees find place in legislative measures the provisions of Article 362 need not be invoked and the dispute decided on the basis of those statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a case may not attract article 362 and consequently the bar of Article 363 may not also apply.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) is within the description so long as the President in recognising a Ruler or a suc cessor is effectuating the provisions of a Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may apply when the discretion exercised is relatable to his powers flowing from the Covenants read with the Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	However where the President acts wholly outside the provisions of Article 366(22) his action can be questioned because the bar applies to bona fide and legitimate action and not to ultra vires actions.
RatioOfTheDecision	69  The error in the case of <ORG> arises from cer- tain circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first is to think that the paramountcy of the <ORG> descended upon the President on Indian Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that paramountcy the recognition of a Ruler was a gift from the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of the history of integration of States and the: provisions of the Constitution in Articles 291, 362 and 366(22), there is no paramountcy left at all, if paramountcy could at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	be exercised against citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only discretion left is to select a suitable successor to a Ruler and perhaps to withdraw recognition on grounds which are sound and sufficient.
RatioOfTheDecision	W hether such.
RatioOfTheDecision	another kind of withdrawal of recognition may be equally capable of being questioned in a <ORG> of law, is a matter on which I do not express an opinion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore the President cannot claim a total immunity for his acts from the scrutiny of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the paramountcy of the Grand Moghul who could give Subehdarships to his Generals as he pleased nor the paramountcy of the British Crown has descended to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	This eorror is further enhanced by too facile a reading of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any tenuous connection between an Article and the Covenant or Agreement, how-ever remote, is not to be considered sufficient to make a provision fall within the description in the latter part of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Due, regard was not paid to the fact that the draftman would have re- ferred to numbers of Articles if the disputes of every kind under those article stood excluded.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Attorney-General relied in particular on some cases which he said had laid down that the act of recognition is a political act, that  it cannot be questioned before <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	He also referred to cases in which the question of the application of article 363 had arisen.
RatioOfTheDecision	My brother <NAME> in his judgment has sufficiently considered them and I am in such agreement with him that I find it unnecessary to repeat what he has said' there.
RulingByPresentCourt	I adopt his reasoning.
RulingByPresentCourt	In conclusion I hold the orders of the President to be ultra vires and declare them to be so.
RulingByPresentCourt	In consequence a writ of mandamus shall issue not to enforce the orders.

Argument	The Union Finance Minister laid on the table of <ORG>, on September 7, 1970, a statement, inter alia, that :  "........ Government is fortified in the belief that there is widespread, support in the country for putting an end to an out-moded and antiquated system which permitted the enjoyment of privileges and privy purses by a small section of our people without any corresponding social obligations on their part.
Argument	As it has been Government's declared policy to.
Argument	abolish these privileges and privy purses and also to put an end to the very concept of Rulership, Government felt they would be justified in de-recognising the Rulers and t hus putting an end to a period of political and' other uncertainties so undersirable in a matter of this nature.
Argument	Accordingly, President has decided to derecognise all the Rulers and thereby terminate their privy purses and privileges with immediate effect.

RatioOfTheDecision	If the order of the President is without authority of law, as the petitioner contends it is, there is a clear infringement of the guarantee of the fundamental rights under Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	19(1) (f), 21 and 31 (1) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	73  It is unnecessary in the view we take, to deal with the plea raised by Mr. <NAME> that Rulership is "property" and the order of the President deprives the petitioner of that property without authority of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Validity of the order of the President is challenged on the grounds that-(1) the President has no power to withdraw re- cognition of a Ruler once recognised; (2) exercise of the power to withdraw recognition, assuming that the President has  such power, is coupled with the duty to recognise his successor and an order made without recognising a successor is invalid; (3) the order of the President "de-recognising" all the Rulers en masse amounted to arbitrary exercise of power; and (4) in any event, the order was made for a collateral purpose, that is, to give effect to the "policy of the <ORG>" after the <ORG> was unable to secure the requisite majority in the <ORG> to the Constitution Amendment Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) of the Constitution reads "In this Constitution, unless the context otherwise requires, the following expressions have the meanings hereby respectively assigned to them, that is to say-  (22)"Ruler" in relation to an Indian State means the Prince, Chief or other person by whom any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of article 291 was entered into and who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Ruler of the State, and includes any person who for the time being is recognised by t he President as the successor of such Ruler."
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (15) of Art.
Precedent	366 defines an "Indian State" as meaning "any territory which <ORG> recognised as such a State'.-" Article 291 , as amended by the Constitution (Seventh Amendment) Act, 1956, reads as follows:  "Where under any covenant or agreement entered in by the Ruler of any Indian State before the commencement of this Constitution, the payment of any sums, free of tax, has been guaranteed or assured by <ORG> to any Ruler of such State, as privy purse--  (a) such sums shall be charged on, an paid out of, <ORG>; and  (b) the sums so paid to the Ruler shall be exemept from all taxes on income."
Precedent	74  The definition of "Ruler" in cl.
Precedent	(22) of Art.
Precedent	366 is in two parts : a person is a Ruler if he being (a) a Prince, Chief or other person who had entered into the covenant or agreement as is referred to in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , is for the time being recognised by the Presi dent as the Ruler; or  (b) if he is for the time being recognised by the President as the successor of the Ruler mentioned in part (a).
RatioOfTheDecision	Use of the expression "for the time being" in relation to the persons who had entered into covenants or agreements, and in relation to the successor, may perhaps imply that the President has the power inappropriate cases and for adequate reasons to withdraw recognition, but that is a matter on which it is unnecessary for the purpose of this petition to express any final opinion Granting that the President may withdraw recognition of a Ruler once granted, the power conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is exercisable only for good cause, i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	because of any personal disqualifications incurred by a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the provisions enacted in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22), 291 and 362 of the Constitution the privileges of Rulers are made an integral part of the constitutional scheme.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thereby a class of citizens are, for historical reasons, accorded special privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	They cannot be derprived of those privileges a rbitrarily,.
RatioOfTheDecision	for the foundation of our Constitution is firmly laid in the Rule of Law and no instrumentality of the <ORG>, not even the President as the head of the Executive, is invested with arbitrary authority.

RatioOfTheDecision	The Government of India Act, 1935, was a step in the direc- tion of achieving a political unity over the entire sub- continent : it envisaged a constitutional relationship between the Indian States and Provinces in British India on a federal basis.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the concept of a loose federation of disparate constituent units in which the power and authority of the Federation were to differ between one constituent unit and another was soon abandoned as inherently impracticable.

RatioOfTheDecision	The negotiations, the assurances given by leading statesmen, and the terms of the covenants and agreements were certainly not intended to be an exercise in futility.

RatioOfTheDecision	82  The plea raised by the Attorney-General that recognition of Rulership was a "gift of the President" or was "in the gift of the President" is not borne out by the position of and the nature of the powers and functions of the President under our constitutional scheme.
RatioOfTheDecision	President is made by the Constitution repository of the power to recognise the Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	That power may be, exercised consistently with and in aid of the constitutional scheme.
RatioOfTheDecision	A democratic Constitution founded in the Rule of Law does not envisage authority in any instrumentality of the <ORG> reminiscent of autocracy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to recognise a Ruler may be exercised in the case of first recognition only in favour of a person who has signed the covenant, and in favour of his successor having regard to the custom and laws governing the <ORG> if the Ruler dies, or becomes incapable of functioning or his recognition is withdrawn.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the use of the expression "for the time being" in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	( 22) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366 the President is not invested with authority to accord a temporary recognition to a Ruler, nor :with authority to recognise or not to recognise a Ruler arbitrarily : the expression "for the time being" predicates that there shall be a Ruler of the Indian State, that if the first recognised Ruler dies, or ceases to be a 'Ruler, a successor shall be appointed, and that there shall not be more Rulers than one at a given time.
RatioOfTheDecision	By express, injunction in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	53(1) of the Constitution the executive power vested in the President is directed to be exercised " in accordance with the Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	That power is intended to be exercised in aid of and not to destroy constitutional institutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Granting that power to recognise a Ruler carries with it the power to withdraw recognition of the Ruler, the power must be exercised bona fide, and in the larger interest of the people consistently with the provisions of the Constitution to maintain the institution of Rulership.
RatioOfTheDecision	Power may therefore be exercised in  the course of and for recognising another person as a successor to the Ruler, having regard to the laws and customs governing the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President is not competent to recognise a person as a Ruler who is not by the custom and laws governing succession to Rulership qualified to be a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President cannot obviously withdraw recognition of a Ruler and recognise another person as a matter of political patronage.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor can be lawfully depart from the laws and customs governing succession so as to introduce a person as a Ruler who' is not by ties of blood or affiliation related to the previous Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether in certain exceptional circumstances the President may in granting recognition to a successor depart in the larger interest of the country from the strict rule or custom governing succession to the gaddi, is a question which need not be decided.
RatioOfTheDecision	But unquestionably the President is not invested with authority to recognize a stranger as successor to the gaddi, or not to recognise any person at all as a s uccessor if he so chooses.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of the President is plainly coupled with a duty; a duty to maintain the constitutional institution, the constitutional provisions, the constitutional scheme, and the sanctity of solemn agreements entered into by the predecessor of <ORG> which are accepted, recognised and incorporated in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	An order merely "de-recognising" a Ruler without providing for continuation of the institution of Rulership which is an integral part of the constitutional scheme is, therefore, plainly illegal.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (22) of Art.

RatioOfTheDecision	The clause incorporates the history of momentous events which took place in India between 1947 and 1949 leaving a lasting impression upon our national and constitutional structure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 291, 362 and Part VII of the Constitution were when incorporated intended to grant recognition to the solemn promises on the strength of which the former Pr inces were invited by those at the helm of affairs to join the experiment for achieving for the millions their dream of securing a truly democratic form of Government in a united independent India, and clauses (15) & (22) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366 were intended to serve the purpose of identifying the persons who remained entitled to the benefits of those constitutional guarantees.

RatioOfTheDecision	(c) It was against this unpropritious background that <ORG> invited the Rulers of the States to accede on three subjects of <ORG>, External Affairs and Communications.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the time the proposal was put forward to the Rulers, an assurance was given to them that they would retain the status quo except for accession on these subjects.
RatioOfTheDecision	It had been made clear to them that there was no intention either to encroach on the internal autonomy or the sovereignty of the States or to fetter their discretion in respect of their acceptance of the new Constitution of India.

RatioOfTheDecision	The privy purse settlements are therefore in the nature of consideration for the surrender by the <ORG> of all their ruling powers and also for the dissolution of the States as separate units "The <ORG> have now discharged their part of the obligations by transferring all ruling powers and by agreeing to the integration of their States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The main part of our obligation under these agreements, is to ensure that the guarantees given by us in respect of privy purse are fully implemented.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our failure to do so would be a breach of faith and seriously prejudice the stabilisation of the new order."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the larger interest of achieving the unity of the country our statesmen chose to appeal to the patriotism of the Princes and not to rely upon the force of arms or methods of political agitation within the States.

RatioOfTheDecision	A discontented group of Princes was a serious threat to a smooth and orderly transition.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> resolved to honour, without reservation, the promises made to the Princes from time to time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clauses in the draft Constitution relating to the obligation of the Union to pay the privy purses and recognising certain rights, privileges and dignities till then enjoyed by the Princes, were intended to incorporate a just quid pro quo for surrender by them of their authority and powers and dissolution of their States.
RatioOfTheDecision	A legislative mechanism was devised to grant the benefit to the former Princes by making a provision for recognising them as Rulers, and of incorporating in the Constitution the guarantees of the privy purse and personal rights and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The former Princes were accordingly recognised as a class of citizens with special privileges granted to them because they had surrendered their powers; privileges and authority.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument that the President as t he head of the <ORG> may, in exercise of his executive power, destroy that institution, is plainly contrary to the fundamental concept of the Rule of Law.

RatioOfTheDecision	The power to specify may carry with it the, power to withdraw specification, but it is coupled with,, a duty to specify in a manner which makes the constitutional provisions operative.

RatioOfTheDecision	Under the general law relating to transfer of property, a charge does not give rise to a right in rem : the right is however more than a mere personal obligation, for it is a jus ad rem a right to payment out of property specified : <CITATION_1942179> (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_675375> (2).

RatioOfTheDecision	A Ruler seeking to enforce privileges which parliamentary statutes have recognised relies for right to relief upon the mandate of the statutes, and not of the cov enant.
Statute	Article 363 of the Constitution provides " (1) Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution but subject to the provisions of article 143 neither <ORG> nor any other court shall have jurisdiction in any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument which was entered into or executed before the commencement of this Constitution (1) 7 R.C.R.
Statute	892  --L744 SupCI/71 by any Ruler of an Indian "State and to which <ORG> or any of its predecessor Governments was a party and which has or has been continued in operation after such commencement, or in dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of this Constitution relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument.
Statute	(2) In this article--  (a) "Indian State" means any territory recognised before the commencement of this Constitution by  His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as being such a State; and  (b) "Ruler" includes the Prince, Chief or other person recognised before such commencement by His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as the Ruler of any Indian State."
Statute	Exclusion of the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is emphasized by the non-obstante clause with which the Article commences.
Argument	Notwithstanding the investment of jurisdiction upon this <ORG> by Art.
Argument	32 , notwithstanding the jurisdiction conferred upon the High <ORG> by Art.

RatioOfTheDecision	The exception carved out of the exclusion in respect of the jurisdiction conferred upon this <ORG> by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	143 is not a real exception for the jurisdiction of this <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	143 is merely advisory.
RatioOfTheDecision	The non- obstante clause however does not enlarge the field of e xclusion of judicial authority.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Attorney-General urged that the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to enforce rights and obligations arising out of the covenants entered into by the Rulers to which the Government of the Dominion or the predecessor Governments were par- ties, was excluded, because the rights and obligations arose out of acts of <ORG>, and by constitutional provision that exclusion was affirmed and extended after the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	An act of <ORG> need not, it is true, arise out of war or conquest : It may be the result of an agreement, and the terms of the agreements and the obligations flowing only from such agreements may not be enforced in the Municipal <ORG> of either <ORG>, unless the rights and obligations are recognized and accepted by the <ORG>s, or unless the document evidencing the act of <ORG> is itself the Constitution of the <ORG> or <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	But there can be no act of state against its own citizen by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Rulers who were before integration of their <ORG>s aliens qua the Dom inion Government are now citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their rights and obligations which arose from an act of state are now recognized and accepted by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Enforcement of those rights and obligations is governed by the, municipal laws, and unless the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is excluded in respect of any dispute, the <ORG> will be competent to grant relief.
RatioOfTheDecision	An act of state vanishes when the new sovereign recognizes either expressly or by implications the rights flowing therefrom : <CITATION_19878> (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	We are unable to agree with the Attorney-General that "old unidentified concept of paramountcy of the British Crown" was inherited by the <ORG>, by reason of the instruments of accession and merger agreements, and that "recognition of Rulership was a 'gift of the President', and not a matter of legal right, existing as it did in the area of paramountcy and remaining with <ORG>".
RatioOfTheDecision	The British Crown did not acquire paramountcy rights by any express grant, cession or transfer:it exercised paramount cy because it was the dominant power.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy had no legal origin, and no fixed concept: its dimensions depended upon what in a given situation the representatives of the British Crown thought expedient.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy meant those powers which the British authorities by the might of arms, and in disregard of the sovereignty and authority of the States chose to exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that paramountcy lapsed with the Indian Independence Act, 1947: even its shadows disappeared with the integration of the States with <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the withdrawal of the British power and extinction of paramountcy of the British power <ORG> did not and could not exercise any paramountcy over the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	In clause 3 of the Standstill Agreement it was expressly recited that "Nothing in the agreement includes the exercise of any paramouncy functions".
RatioOfTheDecision	The relations between the States and <ORG> were strictly governed by the instruments executed from time to time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Subject to the power conf erred in respect of certain matters of common interest to legislate and exercise executive authority, the Princes had sovereignty within their territories.
RatioOfTheDecision	With the advent of the Constitution the States ceased to exist, and the Princes and Chiefs who were recognized as Rulers were left with no sovereign authority in them.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is difficult to conceive of the (1)  6 S.C. R. 401 government of a democratic Republic exercising against its citizens "paramountcy" claimed to be inherited from an Imperial Power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power and authority which the <ORG> may exercise against its citizens and even aliens spring from and are strictly circumscribed by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fundamentals on which paramountcy rested-i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	the compulsion of geography and the essentials for ensuring security and special responsibility of <ORG> to protect all territories in India survived the enactment of the Indian Independence Act, for between August 15, 1947 and the date of integration, of the various States, <ORG>  India was the only fully sovereign authority.
RatioOfTheDecision	But paramountcy with its brazen-faced autocracy no longer survived the enactment of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under our Constitution an action not authorised by law against the citizens of the Union cannot 'be supported under the shelter of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The functions of the President of India stem from the Constitution-not from a "concept of the paramountcy of the British Crown" identified or unidentified.
RatioOfTheDecision	What the Constitution does not authorise, the President cannot grant.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rulership is therefore not a privilege which the President may in the exercise of his discretion bestow or withhold.
Precedent	-Jurisdiction of the Courts in matters specified is excluded not because the Union of India is a successor to the paramountcy of the British Crown,, nor because the rights and obligations accepted and recognized by the Constitution may still be regarded as flowing from acts of State : it is only excluded in respect of specific matters by the express provision in Art.
Precedent	363 of the Constit ution.
Argument	Jurisdiction of the <ORG> even in those matters is not barred "at the threshold" as contended by the Attorney-General.
Argument	The President cannot lay down the extent of this <ORG>'s juris- diction.
Argument	He is not made by the Constitution the arbiter of the extent of his authority, nor of the validity of his acts.
Argument	Action of the President, is liable to be tested for its validity before the <ORG> unless their jurisdiction is by express enactment or clear implication barred.
RatioOfTheDecision	To accede to the claim that the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is barred in respect of whatever the executive asserts is valid, is plainly to subvert the Rule of Law.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore within the province of the <ORG> alone to determine what the dispute brought before it is, and to determine whether the jurisdiction of the <ORG> is, because it falls within one of the two limbs of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , excluded qua that dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	In dealing with the dimensions of exclusion of the exercise of judicial power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , it is necessary to bear in mind certain b road considerations.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proper forum under our Constitution for determining a legal dispute is the <ORG> which is by training and experience, assisted by properly qualified advocates, fitted to perform that task.
RatioOfTheDecision	A provision which purports to exclude the jurisdiction of the <ORG>s in certain matters and to deprive the aggrieved party of the normal remedy will be strictly construed, for it is a principle not to be whittled down that an aggrieved party will not, unless the jurisdiction of the <ORG>s is by clear enactment or necessary implication barred, be denied recourse to the <ORG>s for determination of his rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> will interpret a statute as far as possible, agreeably to justice and reason and that in case of two or more interpretations, one which is more reasonable and just will be adopted, for there is always a presumption against the law maker intending injustice and unreason.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> will avoid imputing_to the <ORG> an intention to enact a provision which flouts notions of justice and  norms of fairplay, unless a contrary intention is manifest from words plain and unambiguous.
RatioOfTheDecision	A provision in a statute will not be construed to defeat its manifest purpose and general values which animate its,, structure.
RatioOfTheDecision	In an avowedly democratic polity, statutory provisions ensuring the security of fundamental human rights including the right to property will, unless the contrary mandate be precise and unqualified, be construed liberally so as to uphold the right.
RatioOfTheDecision	These rules apply to the interpretation of constitutional and statutory provisions alike.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) defines a "Ruler" as a Prince, Chief or other person who has entered into a covenant or agreement as is referred to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , and is recognized for the time being by the President and includes the successor of such Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 291 in defining the sum guaranteed or assured to the Ruler as privy purse refers to covenants and agreements entered into by the Rulers which guarantee or assure the payment of sums as privy purse free from tax.
RatioOfTheDecision	I t was contended on behalf of the <ORG> that the expression "relating to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 means " referring to", and since Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291, 362 and 366(22) refer to covenants, the <ORG> have no jurisdiction to entertain disputes with respect to rights arising from those provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In support of that argument counsel for the <ORG> referred us, to the diverse meanings in which the expression "relating to" is used.
RatioOfTheDecision	But a constitutional provision will not be interpreted in the attitute of a lexicographer, with one eye on the provision and the other on the lexicon.
RatioOfTheDecision	The meaning of a word or expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	used in the Constitution often is coloured by the context in which it occurs: the simpler and more Common the word or expression, the more meanings and shades of meanings it has.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the duty of the <ORG> to determine in what particular meaning and particular shade of meaning the word of expression was used by the Constitution makers, and in discharging the duty the <ORG> will take into account the context in which it occurs , the object to serve which it was used, its collocation, the general congruity with the concept or object it was intended to articulate and a host of other considerations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Above all, the <ORG> will avoid repugnancy with accepted norms of justice and reason.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "provisions of this Constitution relating to" in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 means provisions having a dominant and immediate connection with": it does not mean merely having a reference to.
RatioOfTheDecision	A wide meaning of the expression may exclude disputes from the jurisdiction of the <ORG>s in respect of rights or obligations, however indirect or tenuous the connection between the constitutional provision and the covenant may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	Jurisdiction to try a proceeding is barred under the first limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 if the dispute arises out of the provision of a covenant : it is barred under the second limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 if the <ORG> holds that the dispute is with respect to a right arising out of a provision of the Constitution relating to a covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	A dispute that an order of an  executive body is unauthorised, or a legislative measure is ultra vires, is not one arising out of any covenant under the firm limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , merely because the order or the measure violates the rights of the citizen which, but for the act or measure, were not in question.
RatioOfTheDecision	The dispute in such a case relates to the validity of the act or the vires of the measure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Exclusion of the <ORG>'s jurisdiction by the terms of the relevant words in the second limb lies in a narrow field.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the constitutional provision relating to a covenant is the source of the right claimed to accrue, or liability claimed to arise, then clearly under the second limb the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to entertain a dispute arising with respect to the right or obligation is barred.
RatioOfTheDecision	We need in the present case express no opinion on the question whether a dispute that an executive act or legislative measure operating upon a right accruing or liability arising out of a provision is invalid falls within the second limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a q uid pro quo for agreeing to surrender their power and authority, it was enacted in the Constitution that the Princes who had signed the covenant of the nature specified will be recognized as Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But under the treaties, covenants and agreements executed by the former Princes, there was no provision for recognition of Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President was invested by the Constitution with power to recognise Rulers under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	The status of the Rulers under the Constitution is not the status which the Princes had: their rights, privileges and functions are fundamentally different from those of the former Princes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some degree of obscurity is introduced by the use of the expression "Ruler" and "Ruler of an Indian State" in the Articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the mean-  97  ing is reasonably plain.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ruler as defined in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is a former Prince, Chief or other person who was on or after January 26, 1950, recognised as a Ruler, he having signed the covenant, or his successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Ruler of an Indian State means a Prince, or Chief  who was recognized before the, Constitution by the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Ruler of an Indian State had sovereign authority over his State.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Ruler recognized by the President rules over no territory, and exercises no sovereignty over any subjects.
RatioOfTheDecision	He has no status of a potentate and no privileges which are normally exercised by a potentate.
RatioOfTheDecision	He is a citizen of India with certain privileges accorded to him because he or his predecessor had surrendered his territory, his powers and his sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) is, in our judgment, a provision relating to recognition of Rulers: that is the direct and only purpose of the provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not a provision relating to a covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	The qualification of a person being recognized as a Ruler is undoubtedly that he is a Prince, Chief or other person who had entered into a covenant or agreement as is referred to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , or that he is the successor to such a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference to the covenant or the agreement of the nature mentioned in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is for determining who  may be recognized as a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Because of that reference the provision enacted with the object of conferring authority upon the President to recognize a Ruler, will not be deemed one relating to the covenant or agreement.
RulingByLowerCourt	The Attorney-General urged that this <ORG> has decided that the <ORG> have no jurisdiction to determine whether the order of the President under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	366(22) is valid, and that the <ORG> will not be justified in unsettling the law.
RulingByLowerCourt	The decisions relied upon are: <NAME> v. <NAME> (supra) and <CITATION_347856> (supra).
RatioOfTheDecision	In our judgment, in neither of these cases the question about the bar to the <ORG>'s jurisdiction by virtue of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 was directly in issue.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case (supra) this <ORG> upheld the claim that the privy purse payable to the Ruler of Jaora was exempt from attachment under s. 60 (1) (g) of the Code of Civil Procedure.
Precedent	The <ORG> in that case considered the nature of the privy purse and held that it was a "political pension" within the meaning o f s. 60(1) (g) of the Code of Civil Procedure.
Precedent	<NAME>, J., speaking for the <ORG>, after setting out the history of integration and absorption of <ORG>s, summarised the provisions of Arts.
Precedent	291, 362, 363 and 366(22) of the Constitution and observed (at p. 208):  "Now, the Covenant entered into by the Rulers of Madhya Bharat <ORG>s was a treaty entered into by the Rulers of independent <ORG>s by which they gave up their sovereignty over 'their respective territories and vested it in the new United <ORG> of Madhya Bharat.

RatioOfTheDecision	On the coming into force of the Constitution of India, the guarantee for the payment of periodical sums as privy purse is continued  by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 of the Constitution, but its essential political character is preserved by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 of the Constitution, and the obligation under this guarantee cannot be enforced in any municipal court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover, if the President refuses to recognise the person by whom the covenant was entered into as the Ruler of the State, he would not be, entitled to the amount payable as privy purse under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291."

RatioOfTheDecision	363 , and the obligation cannot be enforced in any municipal Court was not necessary for the purpose of the decision, and is, in our judgment, not correct.

RatioOfTheDecision	99  In the view we have expressed, the argument raised by Mr. <NAME> that even if cl.

RatioOfTheDecision	Reference to the covenant in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 merely identifies the sum payable as privy purse : it does not make Art.

Precedent	363 is raised.

RatioOfTheDecision	100  We further hold that the President is not invested with any political power transcending the Constitution, which he may exercise to the prejudice of ci tizens.

RatioOfTheDecision	Validity of the exercise of those powers is always amenable to the jurisdiction of the <ORG>, unless the jurisdiction is by precise enactment excluded.

RatioOfTheDecision	The only  article in the Constitution which mentions the recognition of a person as a Ruler is Art.

Statute	Clause 22 of the article provides "In this Constitution unless the context otherwise, requires, the following expressions have the meaning hereby respectively assigned to them, that is to say-  102  (22)"Ruler" in relation to an Indian State means the Prince, Chief or other person by whom any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of article 291 was entered into and who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Ruler of the State, and includes any person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the successor of such Ruler;"  Clause (15) of Art.

RatioOfTheDecision	(2)  The grievance of the petitioner in this series of petitions is the same' as the rights asserted by them flow from more or less similar transactions.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have to delve into the past history of India in order to appreciate the setting in which these pe rsons or their ancestors who were formerly Rulers of territories in India wore brought within the fold of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though not sovereign within the meaning of that expression in International Law these former Rulers had certain attributes of sovereignty during the days preceding the independence of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	As is well known to all students of history the achievement of setting up a British Empire in India was "in its early stages at any rate, brought about by the agents of <ORG> in India."
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> entered into treaties with Indian States in the early stages aiming at no more than securing for the <ORG> a privileged position in trade against its rivals.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the first time <ORG> asserted its authority and control over <ORG>'s activities both in.
RatioOfTheDecision	India and in England by the Regulating Act of 1773, under which the Governor of Bengal became the Governor-General in Council with a certain amount of control over the Presidencies of Bombay and Madras.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Marquis of Wellesley as the Governor-General felt convinced when he came to India in 1798 and saw the state of affairs here that the British must become the one paramount power in the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	He set up a system under which no Indian State which had accepted subsidiary alliance with the British could make any war or carry on negotiations with another State without the <ORG>'s knowledge and consent.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was during his time.
RatioOfTheDecision	that the British Dominion in India expanded considerably.
RatioOfTheDecision	He had practically eliminated the French influence in India and brought many States under the subsidiary alliance, the notable instances being Hyderabad, Travancore, Mysore, Baroda and Gwalior.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under this system of subsidiary alliance the bigger states were to maintain armies commanded by British officers for preservation of the public peace and their rulers were to cede certain territories for the upkeep of these forces; the smaller States were to pay a tribute to the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In return the <ORG> were to protect them, one and  all, against external aggression and internal rebellion.
RatioOfTheDecision	A British Resident was also installed in every State that accepted the subsidiary alliance.
RatioOfTheDecision	This process was carried on during the regime of <NAME> and <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Marquis of <NAME> who came out as a Governor-General in 1813 crushed the <NAME> and finally broke the Mahratta power and carried the spread of the British dominion over northern and central India to a stage which it was only left for Lord <NAME>, a quarter of a century later, to complete.
RatioOfTheDecision	He resumed <NAME>'s policy by extending the <ORG>'s supremacy and protection over almost all the Indian States.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the time he left the country in 1823, the British empire in India had been formed and its map in essentials drawn.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every State in India outside the Punjab and Sind was under the <ORG>'s control.
Precedent	The influence of the company over the internal administration of the States rapidly increased ,during the period following the retirement of Lord <NAME>.
Precedent	Residents became gradually  transformed into diplomatic agents representing a foreign power into executive and controlling officers of a superior government.
Precedent	The Charter of 1833 abolished the <ORG>'s trading activities and the <ORG> assumed the functions of <ORG>.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> acquired vast territories for the <ORG> conquering the Punjab and pushing the frontiers to the natural limits of India i.e.
Precedent	the base of the mountains of Afghanistan.
Precedent	Whatever may have been the cause which led to the Mutiny of the year 1857 it was realised by the British people that the Indian States could play a vital role as one of the bulwarks of British rule.
Precedent	An Act of 1858 intituled " An Act for the Better Government of India" provided by the 67th section that "all treaties made by the <ORG> shall be binding upon <NAME>".
Precedent	In her proclamation <NAME> made it clear that the Government would respect the rights, dignity and honour of Native Princes.
Precedent	The policy of annexation vigorously pursued by <NAME> gave way to the per petuation of the <ORG>s as separate entities.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> carried this new policy to its next logical step by recommending that the integrity of the <ORG>s should be preserved by perpetuating the rule of the Princes whose power to adopt heirs should be recognised.
Precedent	The Secretary of <ORG> for India agreed to this recommendation and sanads were granted to the Ruler under which in the event of the failure of the natural heirs, they were authorised to adopt their successors according to their law and custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	These sands were intended to remove mistrust and suspicion and knit the Native Sovereigns to the paramount power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The new policy was to punish the ruler for extreme misgovernment and if necessary to depose him but not to annex his <ORG> for misdeeds.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Indian <ORG>s thus became part and parcel of the British Empire in India.
Precedent	In The words of Lord C<NAME> :  105  "The territories under the sovereignty of the <ORG> became at once as important and as integral a part of India as territories under its direct domin ation.
Precedent	Together they form one direct care and the political system which the Moghuls had not completed and the Maharattas never contemplated is now an established fact of history."
Precedent	The next five decades were occupied with the task of evolving a machinery for controlling the <ORG>.
Precedent	A political department was set up under the direct charge of the Governor-General.
Precedent	It had at its disposal a service known as <ORG>, manned by officers taken from <ORG> and the <ORG>.
Precedent	It had a police force which was maintained partly by the revenues of <ORG> and partly by contributions made by the <ORG>.
Precedent	<ORG> had Residents and Political Agents in all important <ORG> and groups of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Secretary of <ORG> kept a close control over the activities of <ORG> mainly because of the interest of the Crown in matters affecting the rights and privileges of the Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Constitutionally the <ORG> were not part of the British India nor were  their inhabitants British subjects.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> had no power to legislate for the States or their people.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>'s relationship with the Indian States was conducted by the Governor-General in <ORG> and since he was in charge of the political <ORG>, his Executive <ORG> tended in practice to leave States' affairs to him which meant that the Political <ORG> came gradually to assume the position of a government within a government.
RatioOfTheDecision	With the building up of a strong Political <ORG> the <ORG> started asserting rights never claimed by <ORG> and even at times cutting across treaties.
RatioOfTheDecision	The most outstanding example and at the same time one of far-reaching consequence, in the relations of the paramount power with the <NAME> was the prerogative assumed of recognising succession in the case of natural heirs.
Precedent	The first ruling in this behalf was laid down by <ORG> in 1884 in a letter addressed to the Chief Commissioner of <ORG> in which it was stated  that succession to a native <ORG> is invalid until it receives in some form the sanction of the British authority.
Precedent	In the view of the Secretary of <ORG> expressed in 1891 it was admittedly the right and duty of Government to settle successions in the protected <ORG>s in India.
RatioOfTheDecision	This right it was claimed flowed essentially from the-position of the British as the Supreme power responsible for maintaining law and order throughout the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	That power alone had the necessary sanction to enforce decisions regarding disputed successions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Ruler thus did not inhert his gaddi as of right but as a gift from the paramount power.
RatioOfTheDecision	A definite pattern of <ORG> relationship with the <ORG>s had been developed b the time the first world War broke out in 1914.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Rulers rallied to fight for the Empire, and the organisation of the war effort involved closer coordination of administrative activity in the <ORG>s as well as in the Provinces.
RatioOfTheDecision	Throughout the country the tide of national aspirations was ri <NAME> fast.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although Britain claimed to be fighting a war to defend freedom and democracy the system of government by which she continued to hold India in imperial thrall was clearly at variance with her professed aims.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> recognised that the situation needed now handling.
Precedent	In 1917 <NAME>, the Secretary of <ORG> for India, announced that the policy of His Majesty's Government with which <ORG> was in complete accord, was that of an increasing association of Indians in every branch of the administration and the gradual development of self-governing institutions with a view to progressive realisation of responsible government in India as an integral part of the British Empire.
Precedent	The Secretary of <ORG> for India and the Viceroy Lord <NAME> published a joint report on Constitutional Reforms which was the first major investigation into the relations of the <ORG>s with the rest of India and with the paramount power.
Precedent	The authors of the report visualised that the Provinces would  ultimately become self-governing units held together by <ORG> which would deal solely with matters of common concern to all of them.
Precedent	With regard to the Rulers the authors of the report felt that the time had come to end their isolation and that steps should be taken for joint consultations by them for the furtherance of their common interest.
Precedent	There was a conference of ruling Princes and Chiefs in 1919 which recommended that the rulers of States having full and unrestricted powers of civil and criminal jurisdiction in their States, and the power to make their own laws should be termed sovereign Princes as against those who lacked such powers.
Precedent	This was however not favoured by <ORG>.
Precedent	In 1921 <ORG> was brought into being by a Royal Proclamation which announced that the Viceroy would take counsel of the <ORG> freely in matters relating to that territories of Indian States generally and in matters which affected these territories jointly With British India or with  the rest of the Empire.
Precedent	<ORG> would have no concern in the internal affairs of individual <ORG>s or relations of Individual <ORG>s with <ORG> while the existing rights of these states and their freedom of action would in no way be prejudiced or impaired.
Precedent	In the years following the first World War <ORG> in India gained, considerable impetus.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> who came out as Viceroy in 1926 felt that the political situation in the country demanded some gesture on the part of Britain.
Precedent	In March 1927 an announcement was made for appointing a statutory Commission to enquire into the working of <ORG> Act 1919 and to make recommendations regarding further constitutional advancement.
Precedent	At or about this time the Rulers of the Indian <ORG>s also demanded an impartial enquiry into the whole relationship between themselves and the paramount power.
Precedent	The Secretary of <ORG> appointed a Committee of three members headed by Sir <NAME> to enquire into the r elationship between the States and the paramount power and to suggest means for the more satisfactory adjustment of the existing economic relations, between the States and the British India.
Precedent	On behalf of the States it was contended before the <ORG> that all original sovereign powers except those which had been transferred with their consent to the <ORG> were still possessed by them and that such transfers could be effected only by the consent of the States and that the paramountcy of the British <ORG> was limited to certain matters-those relating to foreign affairs and external and internal security.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> was not prepared to accept this and held that none of the States overhad any International status.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> committee refused to define paramountcy but asserted that paramountcy must remain paramount; it must fulfil its obligations defining or adopting itself according to the shifting necessities of the time and the progressive development of the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	They however observed that if any Government  in the nature of <ORG> should be constituted in British India such <ORG> could clearly be a new <ORG> resting on a new written Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Committee noted the grave apprehension of the Princes on this score and recorded a strong opinion that in view of the fact of the historical nature of the relationship of the paramount power and the Princes the latter should not be transferred without their agreement to a relationship with a new <ORG> in British India responsible to an Indian Legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	This really laid the foundation of a policy whereby in later years a wedge was effectively driven between the States and the British India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Rulers were certainly disappointed with the findings of <ORG> with regard to their main hopes of being freed from the unfettered discretion of <ORG> 8-L744Sup.CI/71 to intervene in their internal affairs.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nationalist opinion in the country viewed the recommendations of <ORG> with grave apprehensi on and emphatic protests were entered in the report of a committee presided over by Pandit <NAME> and an All Parties Conference was arranged in 1928 to frame a Dominion Constitution for India.
RatioOfTheDecision	It gave a warning that it was inconceivable that the people of the states who were fired by the .same ambitions and aspirations as the people of British India would quietly submit to existing conditions for ever, or that the people of British India bound by the closest ties of family, race and religion to their brethren on the other side of an imaginary line would .never make common cause with them.

RatioOfTheDecision	Then came the Government of India Act 1935 which provided for a constitutional relationship between the Indian States and British India on a federal basis.
RatioOfTheDecision	A special feature of the scheme 'Was that whereas in the case of the provinces accession to the <ORG> was to be automatic in the case of the states it was to be voluntary.
RatioOfTheDecision	A State was to be considered to have acceded when its Ruler executed an Instrument of Accession and after it was accepted by <NAME>.

Precedent	After the assumption of power by <ORG> in England a Parliamentary delegation visited India and later the Secretary of <ORG> announced the Government's decision to send a delegation of three Cabinet Ministers to India.

Precedent	The plan provided for the entry of the States to the proposed Union' of India in the following manner :  (a) Paramountcy could neither be retained by the British Crown nor transferred to the new <ORG>.
Precedent	But according to the assurance given by the Rulers that they were ready and willing to do so, the States were expected to co-operate in the new development of India.

RatioOfTheDecision	In regard to States the plan laid down that the policy of His Majesty's Government towards the Indian States contained in the Cabinet Mission Memorandum of May 1945 remained unchanged.
RatioOfTheDecision	'At a Press Conference held by him Lord <NAME> gave it out that the date of transfer of power would be about 15th August, 1947.
