Fact	It provided for the setting up of two independent Dominions as and from the 15th August 1947.

RatioOfTheDecision	Shortly before the 15th August 'with the helpful efforts of Lord <NAME> negotiations were concluded and barring Hyderabad, Kashmir and Junagadh all the States within the geog raphical limits of <ORG> had acceded to the Indian Dominion by the 15th August.
RatioOfTheDecision	The accession of the Indian States to the Dominion of India established a new organic relationship between the States and <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second phase which rapidly followed involved a process of two-fold integration, consolidation of States into sizable administrative units and their democratization.
RatioOfTheDecision	With the advent of independence in India the popular urge in the States for attaining the same measure of freedom as was enjoyed by the people in the Provinces gained momentum and unleashed strong movements for the transfer of power from the Rule to the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the larger units were concerned democratization of administration could be a satisfactory solution of their constitutional problem.
RatioOfTheDecision	However in the case of small States responsible Government could have only proved a farce.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Rulers of smaller States were in no position to meet the demand.
RatioOfTheDecision	for equating the position of their people with that  of their countrymen in the Provinces.
RatioOfTheDecision	Without doubt the smaller State units could not have continued in modern conditions as separate entities; integration provided the only approach to the problem.
RatioOfTheDecision	The integration of States did not however follow a uniform pattern.
RatioOfTheDecision	Merger of States in the Provinces geographically continuous to them was one form of integration; the second was conversion of States into Centrally administered areas; and the third form was the creation of new viable units known as <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each of these forms was adopted according to size, geo graphy and other factors relating to each State or group of States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The problem of integration was first faced in Orissa where the States formed scattered bits of territory with no geographical contiguity.
RatioOfTheDecision	After long discussions with the Rulers of the States and the Minister of <ORG> it was eventually decided to integrate the small States with the adjoining Provinces.
RatioOfTheDecision	Agreements were signed by the Rulers of these States in Decemb er 1947 and on subsequent dates providing for cession by them to the Dominion of India full and exclusive authority, jurisdiction and power in relation to the governance of their <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There were several groups of <ORG> which with due regard to geographical, linguistic, social and cultural affinities of the people could be consolidated into sizable and viable units consisting entirely of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such cases, territories of <ORG> were united to form <ORG> of <ORG> on the basis of full transfer of power from the <ORG> to the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	A special feature of these <ORG> was the provision for the Rajpramukh as the constitutional head of the <ORG> who was to be elected by a Council of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The United <ORG> of Gwalior, Indore and Malwa and other small <ORG> came to be known as Madhya Bharat of which the Ruler of Gwalior became the Rajpramukh.
RatioOfTheDecision	Integration of Rajputana was completed in three stages.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a result of the application of the various merger and integration schemes 216 <ORG> were merged in Provi nces, 61 States were taken over as Centrally administered areas and 275 States were integrated into <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The process of the merger of the States with the Provinces or their constitution into Centrally Administered areas, transfer of power to the people was automatic in that the merged States became part of the Administrative units which were governed by the popular <ORG> and the <ORG> as the case might be.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the Provincially merged States were concerned, under the arrangements made virtually by the statutory orders issued under S. 290-A of the Government of India Act 1935 provision was made for the representation of the people of the merged States in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards <ORG> wherever practicable popular interim ministries were set up to conduct their administration.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Instruments of Merger and the covenants establishing the various units of States were in the nature of overall settlements with the Rulers who had executed them .

RatioOfTheDecision	The idea is to bar the jurisdiction of the courts including <ORG> in regard to adjudicating in respect of any disputes that might arise out of any treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instruments that might have been entered into by <ORG> or by any predecessor Government. "
RatioOfTheDecision	Questioned by a member as to who would decide, T. T. <NAME> replied The idea is that the court shall not decide in this particular matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is subject only to the provisions of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	119 by which the President may refer the matter to <ORG> and ask for its opinion and <ORG> would be bound to communicate its opinion to the President on any matter so referred by him.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> will also remember that there are a few arti cles in the Constitution, specifically 302-A (the present Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 ) and 267-A (the present Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 ) where there are references to these agreements, covenants, sanads etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	and even these are precluded from adjudication by any court.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> will recognise that it is very necessary that matters like these should not be made a matter of dispute that goes before a court and one which would well nigh probably upset certain arrangements that have been recommended and agreed to by <ORG> in determining the rela- tion between the rulers of States and <ORG> in the transitory period.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the Constitution is passed, the position will be clear.
RatioOfTheDecision	Practically all the States have come within the scope of Part VI-A and they will be governed by the provisions of this Constitution and, excepting so far as certain commitments are positively mentioned in the Constitution, and as I said the two Articles 267-A and 302-A the covenants will by and large not affect the working of the Constitut ion; and it is therefore necessary in view of the vast powers that have been conceded in this Constitution to the judiciary that anything that has occurred before the passing of this Constitution and which might incidentally be operatable after the passing of the Constitution must not be a subject matter of a dispute in a court of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	I think that Members of this <ORG> will understand that it is a very necessary provision so as to save unnecessary disputes by people which might feel that they have been affected or injured and who would rush to a court to make the court recognise such rights and other similar matters which have been practically extinguished by the provisions of this Constitution excepting in so far as certain articles of the Constitution preserved them."
RatioOfTheDecision	There was also some discussion with regard to the definition of "<NAME>" and "<NAME>" which figured in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	303 of the draft Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to Dr. <NAME> the de- finition of '<NAME>' was intended only for the limited purpo se of' making payments out of the privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	It had no other reference at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	He also said that the expression was deliberately used in order to give the power of recognition to the President.
Fact	123  After referring to the historical background of the settlement in W.P.
Fact	No.
Fact	376 of 1970 takes note of the attempt made to amend the Constitution by the Constitution (Twenty Fourth Amendment), Bill 1970 passed by <ORG> on 2nd September 1970.
Fact	It was however rejected by <ORG> on the 5th September, 1970.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same night the President signed an instrument withdrawing recognition of all the Rulers and orders were issued for and on his behalf to each and every Ruler in the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to The petition the order of the 6th September violated Articles.
Precedent	14, 19 (1)  (f), 21 (as per amendment allowed) and 31 (1 ) and.
Precedent	(2) of the Constitution.
Fact	The order was dubbed as unconstitutional, ultra vires, void and inoperative, arbitrary, malafide and a fraud on the Constitution on various grounds formulated in  paragraph 20, the notable ones being as follows :- (1) Art.
Fact	291 embodied the Constitutional acceptance and re- cognition of the guarantees or assurances regarding tax-tree privy purses., The privy purse guaranteed by the <ORG> under the Merger agreements or Covenants were further assured and guaranteed by the Constitution and charged on <ORG>.
Fact	Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 and 362 themselves created new and independent rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The pledge to pay privy purses and the guarantee regarding privileges etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	are inseverable from these accessions and mergers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obligation to pay privy purses and the said guarantee regarding privileges etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	which are inseverable from the accession and merger cannot be abolished by any law, much less by any executive action.
RatioOfTheDecision	(ii) (1) The President of India passed the order withdrawing the recognition of the petitioner and the other Rulers without applying his mind to the question of legality or propriety of the Order.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole and only object of the Order was to deprive t he petitioner and the other Rules of their privy purses and their personal rights and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The derecognition of all the Rulers en masse is itself the clearest possible proof that the whole object is to abolish the institution of Rulership altogether and all the rights and.
RatioOfTheDecision	privileges attached thereto.
RatioOfTheDecision	(iv) Under the agreements executed between the Dominion of India and the Rulers and the covenants concurred in and gua- ranteed by the Dominion of India, a Ruler is entitled to privy purse of a stipulated amount and to rights and privileges which he enjoyed before the 15th August 1947 and succession to his 'gaddi" in accordance with the law and custom of the family was guaranteed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once the President has recognised a person as entitled to receive privy purse and to be accorded rights and privileges due to, him as a Ruler, there can be no interference with his right to re- 744Sup.CI/71.
RatioOfTheDecision	124  ceive the privy purse or with his other rights and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution contains no substantive provision c onferring on the President a right to recognise or not to recognise a Ruler or to withdraw recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	All that the Constitution requires is an indication of the Indian States which are recognised as such under Art.
Precedent	366(15) and a Ruler with reference to such a State under Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	Arts.
Precedent	366(22) and 366(15) cast upon him a power or authority but a constitutional duty to recognise an existing fact and continue to do so in accordance with the provisions of the covenants and agreements.
Precedent	The Order being in clear contravention of Arts.
Precedent	291, 362 and 366(22) was also in contravention of Art 53(1) which required that the executive powers of the Union vesting in the President be exercised by him in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.
Precedent	(v) The right to receive privy purse and other rights of Rulers constitute property within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	19(1)(f) and 31.
Precedent	Deprivation of privy purses and other rights without authority of law contravenes Art.
Precedent	31(1) as the petitioner was to be expropriated of hi s moneys and his right to receive money periodically by way of privy without any compensation.
RatioOfTheDecision	(vi) The <ORG> purse was in substance and in reality compen- sation for the transfer by Rulers of inter alia their properties.
RatioOfTheDecision	(vii) There was a duty cast upon <ORG> to respect and implement the provisions of the Merger agreements and the Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioner's further contentions were that the order left the Merger agreements and covenants untouched and did not in any way abrogate or affect any of the assurances, guarantees and obligations under the agreements and covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the petition Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 covered cases of a dispute arising out of a settlement with a Ruler or a dispute in respect of a right or obligation founded on a provision of the Constitution relating to such a settlement but it did not cover the case of policy embodied in legislative or administrative action to abolish altogether the institution of Rulership and its rights and privileges and of privy purses.
RulingByLowerCourt	The p rayers formulated in the petition were as follows  (a) A writ, direction or order under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	32 of the Constitution declaring-the Order dated 6th September 1970 to be unconstitutional, ultra vires and void and further to quash the Order;  (b) a writ, direction or order declaring that the petitioner continues to be the Ruler and continues to be entitled to the privy purse and to his personal rights and privileges as a Ruler;  (c) a writ, direction or order directing <ORG> to continue to pay the privy purse to the petitioner and to continue' to recognise the Rulership and the personal rights and privileges of the petitioner and to implement and observe the provisions of the covenant/Merger agreement entered into with the petitioner.
RulingByLowerCourt	In the forefront of the counter affidavit of the Joint Secretary to <ORG> in <ORG> is the contention that "by reason of the provisions of Art.
Argument	363 of the Constitution this <ORG> has no jurisdiction to entertain the petition".
RatioOfTheDecision	Th e main propositions out forward in the said counter affidavit are as follows .-  (a) By the petition disputes had been raised which arose directly out of the provisions of the relevant covenant as also his alleged rights accruing under the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The covenant was a political agreement among High Con- tracting Parties and an act of <ORG> and as such could not form the subject matter of any proceeding in any municipal court.
RatioOfTheDecision	The guarantee given by the Dominion of India was only a political act and not a legal one.
RatioOfTheDecision	(c) Neither the covenants nor the Merger agreements nor any provision of the Constitution relating to the covenants or the Merger agreements confer any legal right on the petitioner or on any erstwhile Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	(d) The covenant being a political agreement, the alleged rights and obligations thereunder could not be and were not perennial and were inherently temporary in character and liable, to be varied or repudiated in accordance with <ORG> policy in the interests of the  people.
RatioOfTheDecision	(g) The power of the President to recognise or not to recognise a person as a Ruler was political in character and an incident of sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power included the power to recognise and the power to cease to recognise any person as a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	(k) The relevant covenant being a political agreement among High Contracting Parties and an-act of <ORG>, the petition has no legal right to the gaddi or the privy purse or any of the said privileges and as such neither the gaddi nor the privy purse or any of the said privileges is property within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	19(1)(f) or Art.
Precedent	31(1) or Art.
Precedent	31(2) of the Constitution.
Precedent	(1) If the <ORG> policy changed and the <ORG> decided not to pay such political" pension in future, a dispute arising from such decision was not justiciable in a municipal court.
Precedent	(m) Rulership or the succession thereto, the privy purse and the said privileges were inter alia the subject matter of an agreement and an agreement could not confer on the petitioner any fundamental right under  the Constitution.
Precedent	126  (n) Art.
Precedent	291 of the Constitution did not create any legal right in a person.
RatioOfTheDecision	It only laid down the source and method of payment of the privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article in laying down that the privy purse shall be charged on and paid out of <ORG> meant no more than that these sums would be sums within the meaning of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	112(2)(a) and 113(1) of the Constitution and would not be submitted to the vote of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	And secondly that such sums would be exempt from all taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if the article created a legal right in a person recognised by the President as a Ruler, to receive payment of privy purse Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 barred the enforcement of such right.
RatioOfTheDecision	(o) Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 of the Constitution did not create or impose any legal obligation on <ORG> or the <ORG> of a <ORG> or the <ORG> executive or the <ORG> executive in respect of the said privileges and even with respect thereto Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 barred jurisdiction of all courts in India.
RatioOfTheDecision	(p) The concept of Rulership, t he privy purses and the said privileges unrelated to any current functions and social purposes have become incompatible with democracy, equality and social justice in the context of India today.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the commencement of the Constitution many things have changed, many hereditary rights and unearned incomes have been restricted and many privileges and vested interests have been done away with.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question continuance of covenants and Merger agreements had been exercising the minds of <ORG> for many years past and the Constitution (Twenty Fourth Amendment) Bill was introduced with that object.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the grounds set forth in paragraph 20 of the petition were controverted.
RatioOfTheDecision	In particular it was said  (a) Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 did not cast an obligation on the Government to pay the privy purse and the obligation, if any; was not a legal obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The Order of 6th September 1970 did not violate Art.
Fact	291 or Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	To recognise or not to recognise any person as a Ruler was exercise of a political power which  was not dependent on any provision of the Constitution.
Precedent	(c) The covenants and Merger agreements were and continued to be political agreements and acts of State which could not be enforced in a court of law by reason of Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	(d) Art.
Precedent	366(22) impliedly conferred a power on the President to recognise or not to recognise a person as a Ruler and such a power was a political power.
Precedent	There was no provision in the Constitution which conferred-on the petitioner or any of the erstwhile rulers any rights to be recognised as Ruler and continues to be recognised as such or to privy purse or any of the privileges.
Precedent	(e) As neither the petitioner nor any erstwhile Ruler had or now has any legal right to the privy purse or to any of the privileges or to any of the alleged other rights enforceable in a court of law, there could be no question of the impugned order infringing Art.
Precedent	19(1)(f ), Art.
Precedent	31(1) or Art.
Precedent	31(2) and that in any event Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 barred the enforcement of any such alleged right.
RatioOfTheDecision	(f) The Rulers entered  into the covenants and Merger agree- ments by reason of political compulsion and in their own interests and not on the faith of any undertaking or guarantee on the part of the then Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the petitioner nor any erstwhile Ruler acted upon any assurance or guarantee on the part of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand a fiduciary duty was cast upon the respondent Government not to continue Feudal institutions and anachronistic systems against the interests of the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	(g) The petitioner has no fundamental right as claimed and Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 barred adjudication by a court of law with respect to the rights claimed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The crucial question in the petition is whether the petitioner is entitled to a declaration that the order withdrawing his recognition as a Ruler is beyond the scope of any executive action of the President.
Precedent	The only provision in the Constitution in which the recognition of a person as a Ruler appears is Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	The article being a Code to the meaning of the words used in  the Con- stitution we have to see exactly what it proposes to do and what it achieves.
Precedent	Unless a ruler can be identified for the purposes of the Constitution Art.
Precedent	291 , Art.
Precedent	362 and Art.
Precedent	363 cannot be applied.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (22) fixes the identity of the Ruler for the purposes of the Constitution as a Prince, Chief or other person by whom any covenant or agreement as is referred to in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	1 of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 was entered into.
RatioOfTheDecision	Obviously before a person can be a Ruler under this limb of the article he must be a person who had entered into the kind of agreement just now mentioned.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in order to be a Ruler for the purpose of the Constitution he is also to be recognised by the President as a Ruler of a State.
RatioOfTheDecision	This means that at the commencement of the Constitution claims of the former Rulers to be recognised as the Rulers of the respective States had to be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clearly the Constitution did not contemplate the eventuality of the President not choosing to recognise anybody as a Ruler or choosing only those whom he liked.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the setting in which the Rulers accepted the Constitution as binding on them and their States it must have been in their contemplation as also of the Constitution makers that all of them who were alive at the commencement of the Constitution would get such recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	So much for the time when the Constitution became effective to start with.
RatioOfTheDecision	But as Rulers are human beings and are not immortal the Constitution had to provide for the continuity of the line of Rulers and to lay down who would be a Ruler after the first set of Rulers was no more.
RatioOfTheDecision	This was done by providing that the President would recognise someone as a successor of the Ruler who had departed this life.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "for the time being" was not inserted for the purpose of giving power to the president to recognise a person or withdraw recognition from him as his fancy dictated.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was put in for the purpose of fixing the identity of the Ruler at a given point of time and to emphasise the fact that there could be only one Ruler for a State  at any point of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Read as a whole the clause proceeds on the assumption that the President had the right, power or duty or obligation to recognise some person as a Ruler both at the commencement of the Constitution and ever afterwards so long as the line of Rulers lasted and so long as these provisions were in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	A duty or power or right or obliation to recognise someone as successor to the Ruler is also embedded in the clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there were no covenants or agreements to guide him or bind him, the President could probably recognise and derecognise or withdraw recognition at his will and pleasure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clearly however the grant of such a power was not in the minds of the Constitution-makers.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the time when they entered into covenants and agreements, a solemn assurance or guarantee was given by the Dominion of India that succession to the gaddi of each Ruler would be according to law and custom of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would appear that invariably the rule of lineal male primogeniture coupled with the c ustom of adopting a son prevailed in the case of Hindu Rulers who composed of the bulk of the body.
RatioOfTheDecision	When on the eve of the Constitution being finally adopted the Rulers with the exception of two or three accepted the same as binding upon them and their States, it must follow that they accepted and adopted the Constitution of India because they thought and were assured that the provisions in it regarding themselves and their successors were to their satisfaction and were binding in nature.
RatioOfTheDecision	They certainly never imagined that they would be the play-things of the executive Government of the Union of India to be thrown out like pawns off the chequer board of politics at any moment when the Government felt that their presence was irksome or that they were anachronistic in the democratic set up of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	This democratic set up was what the Constitution ushered in albeit with a shadow of the past in the Rulers with attenuated pomp and pelf.
RatioOfTheDecision	The choice of a person as a Ruler to succeed another-on his death was certain ly not left to the mere caprice of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	He had to find out the successor and this he could do not by applying the ordinary rules of Hindu Law or <ORG> but by the law and custom attaching to the gaddi of a particular State.
RatioOfTheDecision	That <ORG> had no doubts about it is exemplified by several instances where on a question of disputed succession a reference was made to a very high judicial officer to find out the rightful successor with the help of other Rulers.
RulingByLowerCourt	I may mention only two such instances, namely, the appointment of <NAME>, Chief Justice of <ORG> and a retired Judge of <ORG> and their Highnesses <NAME> of Jaipur and <NAME> Kotah as members, to enquire into and report on the rights of the various claimants to the gaddi of Sirohi and the validity of the succession of <NAME>ghness <NAME> who was recognised as <NAME> Sirohi by the Crown Representative in May 1946 on the death of <NAME> ghness <NAME> who left no male heir of the body or adopted son.
RulingByLowerCourt	The insertion in <ORG> under date 7th October 1950 refers to this as also to the activities of the <ORG> and its conclusion that there was no such valid adoption of <NAME> into the <NAME> family as deprived him of his legal status as a member of the ruling family.
RulingByLowerCourt	The notification ends with the following :-  "Having carefully considered the report, the President accepts the findings of the <ORG> of Enquiry in their entirety.
Precedent	Accordingly in exercise of the powers vesting in him under Art.
Precedent	366(22) of the Constitution, the President is pleased to recognise <NAME> as the Ruler of Sirohi in place of the present minor Maharao <NAME> Bahadur who shall cease to be recognised as such with effect from the date of this Order."
Precedent	This clearly shows that the President, did not act in any arbitrary manner.
Precedent	The claims were investigated into with the help of one of the highest judici al officers of the land and reported on to the President The President thereupon withdrew recognition from Shri <NAME> and recognised <NAME> as the Ruler of Sirohi.
Precedent	To my mind Art.
Precedent	360'(22) read with the rules of succession in the Merger agreements and the covenants was given full effect.
Precedent	Recognition was given to the person lawfully entitled to be declared the successor to the gaddi and the same was with- drawn from a person who was held not entitled to it.
Precedent	The Act was certainly executive but in nature it was based on a judicial scrutiny and not on any political consideration or in an arbitrary fashion.
Precedent	Another instance of applying the law and custom of succession is afforded by the case of Dholpur which was enquired into by <NAME>, Chief Justice of <ORG> (as he then was) forming <ORG> with two Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	To my mind the Merger agreements and covenants did not be- come waste paper on the commencement of the Constitution to be consigned to the record roo m or any museum.
RatioOfTheDecision	So long as the above provisions enure in the Constitution a Ruler will have to be found for a <ORG> and such finding must be on the basis of the law and custom of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the assurance which was given to the Rulers when they accepted the Constitution and I see no reason why the Constitution should be interpreted in a way to set that .at naught.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the light of the above, my view is that Art.
Precedent	366(22) implied not merely a right or power but a duty or obligation to recognise a person as a Ruler i.e.
Precedent	a duty or obligation to do so and the power ,or duty to withdraw recognition must be confined to cases when the first recognition was not proper as in the case of the <NAME> succession.
Precedent	But the learned Attorney-General would interpret the same differently.
Precedent	He put forward his contention in the following propositions :-  (a) Recognition was only for the purpose of fixing the identity of a person for payment of privy purse and grant of privileges pursuant to the Constitutional provisions in A rts.
Precedent	291 and 362.
Precedent	In support of this he relied on the Debates in <ORG> to which reference has already been made.
Precedent	He relied on a decision of this Court in <CITATION_21305> (1).
Argument	There the appellant was the Ruler of the State of Bastar and had entered into an agreement with <ORG> whereby he had ceded the State of Bastar to <ORG> to be integrated with the Central Provinces and Berar.
Argument	He challenged the applicability to him of Madhya Pradesh Abolition of Proprietary Rights (Estates, Mahals, Alienated Lands) Act, 1950 (Madhya Pradesh Act I of 1951) meant to provide for the acquisition of the rights of proprietors in estates, mahals, alienated villages and alienated lands in Madhya Pradesh which was applicable to a person described as an ex-Ruler of an Indian State merged with Madhya Pradesh.
Argument	The appellant's contention was that he was still a sovereign Ruler and absolute owner of the villages to which the Act was sought to be applied.
Precedent	In the course of the judgme nt of this Court there is an observation at p. 506 reading (1)  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	501 "The effect of the Merger Agreement is clearly one by which factually a Ruler of an Indian State ceases to be a Ruler but for the purpose of the Constitution and for the purposes of the privy purse guaranteed, he is a Ruler as defined in Art.
Precedent	366(22) of the Constitution.
Precedent	There is nothing in the provisions of Art.
Precedent	366(22) which requires a court to recognise such a person as a Ruler for the purposes outside the Constitution."
Precedent	Earlier in the judgment at page 504 it was said "The expression 'Ruler' as defined in Art.
Precedent	366(22) of the Constitution applied only for interpreting the provisions of the Constitution."
RatioOfTheDecision	In my view these observations do not advance the contention of the Respondent as the Court was not there concerned with the question of power to recognise or withdraw recognition from a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only question before the Court was whether the appellant was an ex-  Ruler for the purposes of the Act., Reference may be usefully ma de to paragraph 241(3) at page 129 of the White Paper on Indian States under the heading "Recognition of Rulers" reading :  "The Rulers of the merged and integrated States have been guaranteed succession according to law and custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the Covenants and some of the Agreements of Merger, provision has been made for the procedure to be observed for the, settlement of the cases of disputed succession.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of Rulers of States forming Unions, every question of disputed succession is to be decided by <ORG> after referring to <ORG> and in accordance with the opi- nion of that Court."
RatioOfTheDecision	The above is followed by the quotation of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) and according to the White Paper "it is expected that in according recognition to Rulers, the President will show due regard to the provisions of the Covenants and Agreements of Merger in respect of the cases to which these provisions apply."
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The learned Attorney-General then submitted that the power of recognition was a political powe r in the paramountcy field to which <ORG> and thereafter <ORG> under the Constitution succeeded and for this he referred to White Paper, paragraph 266 at p. 143 reading:  "In spite of the declaration regarding the lapse of paramountcy, the fundamentals on which it rested remained.
RatioOfTheDecision	The essential defence and security requirements of the country and the compulsions of geography did not cease to be operative with the end of British rule in India.
RatioOfTheDecision	If anything, in the context of world events, they have become more imperative.
Precedent	<ORG> in India which succeeded the British was unquestionably the paramount power in India both de facto and, de jure and that Government, alone was the only completely independent sovereign in India."
Precedent	To my mind the British Crown was the paramount power in India because of the might of its power.
Precedent	Its power was so great compared to,that of the Rulers of the Indian States that it could annex any territory at any time and bring under subjugation  all the Rulers by compulsion or subsidiary alliances.
Precedent	There was no sanction of (International law behind it.
Precedent	Paramountcy after the British had come to be the foremost power in the.
Precedent	country was one of their own creation.
Precedent	In strict legal theory whatever paramountcy there was before the 15th August 1947 in the British Government lapsed with the passing of the Indian Independence Act.
Precedent	Thereafter the Dominion of India was free to do what it liked subject to world opinion and their own conscience.
Precedent	Paramountcy de facto there undoubtedly, was but speaking for myself I cannot ascribe any legal sanction to such paramountcy.
Precedent	The Rulers of Indian States submitted or agreed to the cession of their territory and the government of their people by <ORG> with which they merged and ultimately *the Government of the Union of India because they felt that it was in the best interests of their people and also of themselves.
Precedent	(c)The learned Attorney-General argued that paramountcy continued and the advent of  the Constitution did not put an end to it and the debates of <ORG> with regard to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	302-AA (present article 363 ) that the disputes covered by the said article were beyond the pale of adjudication of courts of law only recognised the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him the old concept of paramountcy was virtually- inherited by the Dominion of India before January 1950 by reason of the Instruments of Accession, Covenants and Merger agreements : the recognition of a Ruler which was the gift of Paramount power was not the matter of a legal right and was exercised as an act of paramountcy and retained the same character.
RatioOfTheDecision	He cited various decisions of this <ORG> to show that covenants,and Merger agreements have always been so interpreted, e.g.
Precedent	<CITATION_635617> .
Precedent	(1), <CITATION_12575> (2) and a number of other cases.
Precedent	He argued further that a plea which was available to the Dominion of India can now be put forward by its, successor government and in support of his contention relied on, the cases of <ORG> ry of <ORG> V.  (1) [1955] 1 S. C.R.
Precedent	4 5 at 429 (2) [1959] S.C.R.
Precedent	729 at 744 Kamachee Boys Sahaba(1), <CITATION_618467> (2), <NAME> v. Secretary of <ORG> (3) and several others.
Precedent	In the first case the British Government acting as sovereign power had seized the whole of the Raj of Tanjore as an escheat on the ground that the dignity of Rajah was extinct for want of male heir and this being on act of <ORG> <ORG> had no jurisdiction.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case (<NAME>) what was sought to 'be enforced was the liability of an ex-Ruler of Oudh which was annexed by the Government of India in 1856 on inter alia the ground that the claim was a charge upon the revenues of Oudh.
Precedent	The plaintiffs who filed the Bill in <ORG> sought to rely upon a statement of Lord <NAME>, President of <ORG> in <ORG> that "the transfer of the revenues of the Kingdom of Oudh carried with it a liability for such debts on the former government and were justly contracted".
Precedent	The plea in , demurer that the seizure of the property was an, act of <ORG> and that it was not liable to any review by a court of law or equity was upheld.
Precedent	The above and cases of the type to my mind are easily distinguishable.
Precedent	Once the <NAME> ceded their territory and accepted the Constitution of India as the Constitution of their <ORG>s they became citizens of India on the commencement of the Constitution and the plea of continuance of an act of <ORG> as against them cannot be accepted.
Precedent	The <NAME> became citizens of India like millions of others but in recognition of the past the Constitution gave them certain special rights like privy purses and assured them of continuance of personal privileges in terms of articles 291 and 362.

RatioOfTheDecision	This point would fall to be considered under 'Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 but before that one must refer to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 which is the pr op and pillar to the claim of privy purse.
Statute	This 'article places the payment of privy purse on a constitutional foundation.
Statute	It expressly refers to the covenants or agreements entered into by a Ruler of an Indian State before the commencement of the Constitution and provides for the disbursement thereof by directing that the sums shall be charged on and paid out of <ORG>.
Statute	In effect it means that the guarantee given by <ORG> for the payment of sums free of taxes by way of privy purse under convenants or agreements etc., is to be worked out and discharged by ensuring that the said sums shall be charged on and paid out of the Consolidated Fund.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to Mr. <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is mandatory.
RatioOfTheDecision	It creates new and independent rights and obligations by being engraved in the Constitution and as such beyond the reach of the <ORG> and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This new and independent right makes the article a self-ordaining and self-sustaining one.
RatioOfTheDecision	In cases where there is n o dispute about the amount of the privy purse no question of any reference to the covenant arises.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) The amounts of privy purse guaranteed by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 are the same as mentioned in the covenants but in other vital respects the provisions of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 constitute a marked departure from the provisions of the covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) First, whereas the liability under the covenant was that of the relevant <ORG> or the United <ORG>, it is made a liability of <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291; secondly, the amounts of privy purses are charged on <ORG> for the first time; thirdly, the amounts are guaranteed to be exempt from all taxes on income whereas under the covenants the amounts were to be free of all taxes whether imposed by the Government of the United <ORG> or <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) The covenants are referred to in the article only for the limited purpose of identifying the privy purses which are the subject matter of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article cannot be said to elate to convenants merely b ecause it refers to them for the limited purpose of identifying the privy purses.
RatioOfTheDecision	(5) Once Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is held to be mandatory there can be no dispute as to whether the privy purse will or will not be paid.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 only refers to bonafide disputes and not disputes which would merely amount to a mockery of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(6) The principle of harmonious construction would have to be applied.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 cannot be so construed as to violate the effect and mandate of articles 112, 113, 114, 291 and 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) would be violated because one of the main legal effects of recognition under that article is to entitle the recognised Ruler to the privy purse and denial of the privy purse would stultify one of the main objects of recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	(7) The second limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 read along with the first makes it clear that the whole object is to prevent disputes arising from covenants being raised in the garb of enforcing a right conferred by a provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the present series  of cases Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 does not apply since there is no dispute as to rights arising from the covenant and the constitutional provisions merely guarantee that right.
RatioOfTheDecision	(8) In any view of the matter any decision to repudiate the obligations under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 would be malafide and ultra vires.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power or jurisdiction cannot avail an authority to make an order or decision which is malafide and ultra vires because such an order or decision is a nullity and the-bar of jurisdiction under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 cannot be pleaded to protect a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	The submissions of the learned Attorney-General were  (a) The right to privy purse which accrues under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 clearly relates to a covanant : hence Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 bars any dispute in respect of such a right or recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constituent Assembly Debates go to show that this article was meant to give constitutional recognition to guarantees given by <ORG> and provided for the expenditure being charged on the <ORG> revenues subject to such recoveries as might be made from ti me to time from the Provinces and <ORG>s in respect of these payments.
RatioOfTheDecision	It did not create any new and independent right unrelated to the covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The second limb of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 bars any dispute under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 as would be apparent from the correspondence between <NAME>, the Secretary to <ORG> and <NAME>.

RatioOfTheDecision	(d) The covenant was an act of <ORG> and any violation of its terms cannot form the subject of any action in any municipal courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The guarantee given by <ORG> was in the nature of a treaty obligation contracted with the sovereign Rulers of independent <ORG>s and cannot be enforced by action in municipal courts; its sanction is political and not legal; on the coming into force of the Constitution of India the guarantee for payment of periodical sums as  privy purse is continued by Art.

Precedent	363 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	363 in effect recreated paramountcy and barred the adjudication of any dispute which had its seed in act-% of <ORG> by any court of law.

RatioOfTheDecision	It does not by itself create an independent right in the recipient.
RatioOfTheDecision	(f) Art.

Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my view, it is not necessary to examine all the contentions raised for and against the petitioner for the final conclusion to be arrived at.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be no doubt that the provision of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 was not a mere declaration of pious  intention which the executive could disregard at its whim or pleasure.
RatioOfTheDecision	So long as it finds a place in the Constitution it was meant to be acted upon.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was meant to assure the.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> that the privy purses which were con- tained in the covenants and agreements guaranteed by <ORG> were to be fully honoured and not cast away on a false morass of public opinion or buried under acts of <ORG>.
Precedent	No doubt the covenants or Merger agreements were acts of <ORG> but when the framers of the Constitution came to provide for the <NAME> by giving them assurance of continuance of the payment of privy purse and regard to their personal rights and privileges by enshrining them in the Constitution, in my view they never contemplated that the same was to be the play-thing of the executive.
Precedent	It was by the incorporation of Arts.
Precedent	291 and 362 that the Constitution-makers were able to get the willing consent and co-operation of the <NAME> to be brought within the fold of the Constitution.
Precedent	As observed  by <NAME> the settlements with the Rulers were overall settlements taking all the pros and cons of the situation into consideration the aspirations and ambitions of the people of the <ORG>s, their wish and desire to get independence of the same type which their brethren in the erstwhile British India had obtained, their right and determination to have a voice in the administration of the country through their elected representatives, their zeal for getting out of the arbitrariness of some of the Rulers, no less than the wish and desire of the Rulers to honour and accept the desires and ambitions of their people coupled with a desire, to live in peace at least with a part of their denuded status, their decimated right to property and a fraction of the personal privileges to which they were previously entitled.
Precedent	As <NAME> put it :  "The privy purses and the guarantee as to personal rights and privileges was the quid pro quo for the parting of their powers and their huge <ORG> s by the <NAME> and was the minimum which could be afforded to them."
Precedent	10-L744 Sup CI/71 Sardar <NAME> speaking in 1949 said that human memory was proverbially short and that in October 1949 people might not remember what had taken place in the years preceding, namely, the tremendous upheaval in the country since 1946 and the possibility of the <NAME> taking sides with States or peoples not favourably disposed towards India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Only twenty years have passed since then-too short a period to sweep overboard all that took place during the memorable years preceding the commencement of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The old order must change yielding place to new but the change should not be cataclysmic.
RatioOfTheDecision	at the sacrifice of the interests of fairly large number of persons who had helped to consolidate India in a manner far different from anything that had taken place in the past.
RatioOfTheDecision	However that may be we are only concerned with the legal aspect, the morals being for the country at large through their elected representativ es to decide.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 291 was undoubtedly meant to put the guarantee as to payment of privy purses contained in the covenants and agreements on a firm and sure footing.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it was not completely dissociated from the covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has a link with the covenants which were partially-projected into the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This article has its base in the covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its object was to give a lasting and permanent setting to the term in the covenants as to payment of privy purses.
RatioOfTheDecision	I find myself unable to hold that the article does not relate to a covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	'In my view it deals with a portion-the main portion of the entire stream of the covenant and makes it flow along a particular and well-defined channel--a channel which is not only well-defined but with a solid foundation and sides.
RatioOfTheDecision	Counsel on both sides were at pains to show what the effect of the expression 'charged on and paid out of the Consolidated Fund or India' meant.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the learned Attorney-General and Mr. <NAME> who followed him, the e xpression "charged on" was only a form of expression used for the purpose of financial estimates and Appropriation Bills.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was meant to distinguish certain items in the Appropriation Bills from grants which were votable at the will of <ORG> and the further direction for paying out thereafter did not advance matters.
Precedent	Ac- cording to Mr. <NAME> who referred to some of the financial provisions in the Constitution, a security was created thereby on, <ORG>, that there was something akin to a pledge of it for the payment of the privy purse giving rise to a new right.. in my view whatever the nature of their right it- is related to the covenants and as such within the fold of Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Before referring to any decisions on the point it may be useful to make an attempt to define the scope of Art.
Precedent	363 as if it was a case of first impression.
Precedent	The article purports to over-ride all other provisions of the Constitution excepting Article 143. in respect of recourse to any court of law for settlem ent of any disputes.
Precedent	covered by it.
Precedent	Article 143 is a provision enabling the President of India to obtain the opinion of this <ORG> by a reference on any question of law or fact of such public importance as merits a scrutiny by the highest court of the land.
Precedent	Article 143 is only anenabling provision but its scope, is so wide that on any question, of public importance-be it one of law or fact-the President may.
Precedent	refer to this <ORG> for its opinion.
Precedent	Save for the power of the President to refer a matter to this <ORG> for its opinion under Art.
Precedent	143 , Art.
Precedent	363 imposes an absolute bar on the jurisdiction of all courts to adjudicate upon disputes covered by it.
Precedent	of necessity,, the bar must apply to Art.
Precedent	32 also.
Precedent	Under the last mentioned article the Constitution reserves to everybody entitled to any right.
Precedent	covered by Part III i.e.
Precedent	the fundamental rights, to move this <ORG>., The amplitude of the right and the kind of directions which may be issued to enforce that right are contained in various clauses of the article.
Precedent	Non e of these clauses over-ride the all-embracing, provision of Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Rights, be they fundamental or otherwise which form the subject of any dispute covered by this article must, alike come under its bar.
Precedent	The disputes which fall within this bar may be of two kinds., Under the first limb of the article any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, engagement, covenant, sanad or other similar' instrument which was entered into or executed before the 26th January 1950 by any Ruler of an Indian State and to which the Dominion of the Government of India or any of its predecessor Governments was a party and which is or has been continued in operation after the said date, are not to be the subject matter of any judicial proceedings.
Precedent	Clearly, therefore, any one seeking to have his rights adjudicated upon on the basis of a covenant or agreement or Merger agreement or Instrument of Accession would be debarred from coming to court and ventilate his grievance about any violation of.
Precedent	his right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under the secon d limb of the article fall disputes in respect- of-.
RatioOfTheDecision	any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out.
RatioOfTheDecision	of any provisions of the Constitution relating to any treaty, agreement etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	To see whether any dispute falls within this limb one must examine the content of the right or the limit of the liability or obligation arising out of any constitutional provision which provision in its turn must relate to any treaty, agreement etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dispute means any contradiction or controversy.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whenever a person asserts or claims a right in respect of a subject matter and another person contradicts it or denies it, there is a dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	Disputes may be many and of various kinds.
RatioOfTheDecision	it may relate to a question of fact ,or a question of law which again may be a very simple or a complicated one.
RatioOfTheDecision	A question of law may arise about the interpretation of a contract; equally it may arise, about the interpretation of the provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But whatever be the quality or the nature of the controversy it would be a  dispute short of somebody trying to raise a contention which was absurd on the face of it e.g., ,that 'black means white.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right, liability or obligation in dispute must arise out of the provisions of the Constitution which has any bearing on any treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression " relating to" means inter alia "stand in some relation, to have bearing or concern, to pertain, to refer, to bring into association with or connection with."
RatioOfTheDecision	In my view Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is undoubtedly a provision of the Constitution relating to covenant, agreement etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	As I have already indicated Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is not merely a provision for finding out the .amount of the liability of the Dominion of India by way of privy purse to a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	It expressly refers to covenants or agreements ,entered into by the Ruler under which payment of sums free of tax had been guaranteed or assured by <ORG> as privy purse and gives the term as to privy purse a new shape and form Article 291 not refer s to the covenant, engagement etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	but certainly has a bearing on or concern with the same and is brought into association or connection with the same.
Statute	As already indicated, the article seeks to instill life and vigour into the term for payment of privy purse in the covenant by creating a new channel leading out of the guarantee of <ORG> which was no longer in existence and making it flow along a constitutional course by putting the liability of <ORG> for payment of the sums beyond any controversy.
Statute	The article places the payment beyond the reach of voting by <ORG> and expressly directs that the moneys shall be paid out of <ORG> and that the sums so paid shall be exempt from all taxes of income.
RatioOfTheDecision	I find myself unable to accept the argument of Mr. <NAME> that for the purpose of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	29.1 a reference to the covenants is only called for to find out the amount of privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that was the sole object of the article it might well hav e been achieved by using the following words or words to the like effect "all sums om money mentioned as privy purse of Rulers of Indian States in any engagements entered into by them and to which <ORG> of India was a party, shall be charged on and paid out of <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	If one was asked whether and if so, how the Constitution had dealt with the rights of the Rulers of Privy purses contained in the covenants and Merger agreements guaranteed by <ORG>, the answer would have to be that the same has been recognised and perpetuated in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 making assurance doubly sure by directing the charging of <ORG> with the amounts thereof and payment thereout without deduction of income-tax.
RatioOfTheDecision	So considered Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 must be held to be an, article of the Constitution relating to covenants or Merger agreements and any dispute as to payment of privy purse would come, under the bar of Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 363 has come up for consideratio n before this <ORG> in a number of cases and reference has been made to this article quite frequently in several decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	In one of the earliest decisions of this <ORG> in <CITATION_440324> (1) the <ORG> had to consider whether a suit filed on the 15th January 1950 (before the commencement of the Constitution) under the Original, Jurisdiction of the Federal <ORG> for a declaration that the various orders under which the State of Seraikella came to be administered as a part of Bihar and the laws under which those orders were made were ultra vires and the Province of Bihar had no authority to carry on the administration of the State, was dismissed by a majority of the Judges of this <ORG> as being barred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Among the contentions urged there was one that the suit which was filed before the 26th January 1950, stood transferred to Supreme <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	372(2) of the Constitution and that <ORG> was only prospective and of retrospective.

RatioOfTheDecision	observed that the all embracing opening  words of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 over-rode the operation of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	374(2).
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Chief Justice also said "If the plaintiff contends that that agreement (agreement of 15th December 1947) is not binding on it, it cannot enforce its rights under the original jurisdiction of the <ORG>.
Fact	If the plaintiff has a grievance and a right to a relief which the defendants contend it has not, the forum to seek redress is not the Supreme <ORG> exercising its original jurisdiction on the transfer of the suit from the Federal <ORG>."
Fact	(1) 1951 S.C.R.
Argument	174  <CITATION_1414177> (1) the Ruler of the erstwhile State of Sonepur in orissa which had merged with Orissa complained of a violation of his rights and privileges by the inclusive definition of a "person' in s. 2(i) of the Orissa Agricultural Income Tax Act, 1947 (Orissa Act 24 of 1947).
Argument	His case in substance was that as a Ruler of a State he had been immune from payment of agricultural income-tax when it was imposed in 1947 and by articles IV and V of the Merger agreement executed by, h im, the Dominion of India had guaranteed to him all his personal rights, privileges etc.

RatioOfTheDecision	291 of the Constitution.
Fact	The petitioner is entitled to a ,privy purse of Rs.
Fact	10 lacs per year.
Fact	He is also entitled to other rights and privileges arising from the Covenants.
Fact	Prior to August 15, 1947, the Ruler of Gwalior was a Sovereign though his sovereignty was subject to the paramountcy of the British Crown; but that paramountcy lapsed on August 15, 1947 as a result of the Indian Independence Act, 1947.
Precedent	Consequently the Ruler ,of Gwalior as well as other Rulers became absolute Sovereigns.
Precedent	In law they were free to accede to either of the two Dominions of India and Pakistan or to remain independent.
Precedent	But by stages the Indian States adjoining the Dominion of India merged in the Dominion of India.
Precedent	After their merger the Rulers of those States had ,no ruling powers.
Precedent	They had only such rights and privileges as were recognized or created under the Covenants entered into by them with <ORG> and those embodied in the Constitution.
Precedent	On the c oming into force of the Constitution all the former Rulers of the Indian States that had merged with the Dominion of, India as well as their quantum subjects became citizens of India having all the rights and duties of citizens of this country.
Precedent	From about 1967, there was a move in the ruling party to abolish the privy purses guaranteed to the Rulers under the Constitution as well as the privileges guaranteed to them under the Covenants and agreements and recognised in Art.
Precedent	362.
Fact	Consequently the <ORG> moved in <ORG> on September 2, 1970, the Constitution (Twenty fourth) Amendment Bill, 1970 to delete certain provisions of the Constitution relating to the guarantees given to the Rulers about their privy purses as well as privileges.
Fact	That bill was passed in <ORG> but it failed to get the requisite majority in <ORG>.
Fact	The motion for consideration of the bill was rejected at about 4-30 p.m. on September 5, 1970.

Fact	(22) of Art.

Precedent	The farther contention of the peti-  155  tioners is that the rights conferred on them under Arts.

Fact	Before proceeding to consider and pronounce on the issues formulated above, it would be useful to briefly refer to the historical background leading to the merger of the Indian States in the Indian Union as both the petitioners and the respondent have laid great stress on the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the time of the British rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	there were over 500 Indian States possessing varying degrees of sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the matter of internal administration, most of the Rulers had complete freedom.
RatioOfTheDecision	But their sovereignty was subject to the treaties, engagements and sanads entere d into by them with the British Crown and also the paramountcy of the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy was an undefined concept.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was an all pervading power.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> declined to define its scope but said that "paramoutcy was paramount'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy meant just what <ORG> choose, it to mean.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was a convenient fiction devised by the imperial power to further its imperial interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy did not flow from treaties or international law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The sanction behind it was the British military strength.
RatioOfTheDecision	Subject to the Imperial needs the Rulers of Indian States were left free to govern their 'States as they thought best though in few cases, when the Rulers were guilty of gross atrocities the paramount power intervened even in their internal administration.
RatioOfTheDecision	Government of India Act, 1935 visualised a Federation consisting of provinces as well as Indian States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The States were expected to accede to the Federation on limited number of subjects retaining their sovereignty in respect of other subj ects.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the States were so jealous of their rights that it was not possible to persuade them to join the Federation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence, the Federal part of the Constitution visualised by the Government of India Act, 1935 did not come into being.
RatioOfTheDecision	After World War 11 when it became inevitable for <ORG> to grant freedom to this country, the question as to the future relationship of the Indian States with the Dominion of India assumed importance.
RatioOfTheDecision	As there was no agreement between the concerned parties, <ORG> under the Independence Act, 1947 divided the then British India into two parts, India and Pakistan.
Precedent	So far as the Indian States were concerned, it allowed its paramountcy to lapse and those States were asked, if they so choose, to enter the new relationship with one or the other of the Dominions or remain independent.
Precedent	The paramountcy of the British Crown was not inherited either by India or by.
Precedent	Pakistan.
Precedent	It was allowed to lapse.
Precedent	This situation created a crisis.
Precedent	There was an imminent thr eat to the unity of India, politically as well as economically.
Precedent	The situation called for the highest degree of.
Precedent	statesmanship on the part of our leaders.
Precedent	Naturally the <NAME> of the Indian States were anxious to remain as independent sovereigns but they could not have been oblivious of the internal and external dangers to their authority.
Precedent	It was a highly explosive situation.
Precedent	Sardar <NAME> with his political sagacity and pragmatic approach, availing himself.
Precedent	of the co-operation of Lord <NAME> and the assistance of his energetic and tactful <CITATION_152218> first persuaded practically all the <NAME> to accede to India on three subjects viz.
Precedent	Defence, External Affairs and Communications and thereafter stage by stage drew them closer to the Dominion of India and finally persuaded them to merge with the Dominion of India.
Precedent	All this was done in the course of about two years, a feat unparalled in history.
Precedent	The saga of the integration of the Indian States into the Dominion of India will remain the most  exciting and most glorious chapter in the history of our country.
Precedent	This mighty achievement could not have been had peacefully but for the patriotism and farsightedness of many of the Rulers of the Indian States.
Precedent	<NAME> told <ORG> that the Rulers of the Indian States were the co- architects of India's unity.
Precedent	But it was said on behalf of the respondent that the Rulers merged, their States in the Dominion out of sheer necessity and not out of any patriotism, they were not in a position to resist the compulsion of geography and pressure of their subjects in favour of self Government and therefore they merely made a virtue of necessity.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that they acted in self-interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	But there can be ,no doubt that it was enlightened self-interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> told <ORG> on October 12, 1949 : "There was nothing to compel or induce the Rulers to merge the identity of their States.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any use of force would have not only been against our professed principle but would have also c aused serious repercussions.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the <NAME> had elected to stay out, they would have continued to draw the heavy civil list which they were drawing before and in large, number of, cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	they could have continued to.
RatioOfTheDecision	enjoy unrestricted use of the <ORG> revenues.
RatioOfTheDecision	The minimum which %ye could offer to them.
RatioOfTheDecision	as quid pro quo for parting with their ruling powers was to guarantee to them _privy purses an certain privileges on a reasonable and defined basis."
RatioOfTheDecision	Proceeding further the <NAME> exhorted <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The capacity for mischief and trouble on the part of the <NAME> if the settlement with them would not have been reached on a negotiated basis was far, greater than could be imagined at this stage.
RatioOfTheDecision	Let us do justice to them, let us place ourselves in their position 'and then assess the, value of their sacrifice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <NAME> have now discharged their part of the obligations by transferring all the ruling powers and by agreeing to the integration of their <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	I The main part of our obligation under  these Agreements is to ensure that-the guarantees given by us in respect of privy purses are fully implemented.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our failure to do so would be a breach of faith and and seriously prejudice the stabilisation of the new order".
RatioOfTheDecision	Even quite recently, both our President and the 'Home Minister acknowledged with gratitude the sacrifice made by, the 'Indian Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it was argued onbehalf of the respondent that we should not take those utterances at their face value.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was indirectly suggested that those expressions were platitudes intended to achieve some political purposes.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that be so, all that one can say is, mysterious are the ways of politics.
RatioOfTheDecision	The respondent in its counter-affidavit has taken the stand that the people of this country having become conscious of their social.
RatioOfTheDecision	and economic rights would not tolerate any longer the concept of Rulership or the privy purse or any of the privileges incorporated in the Covenants and Merger Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	There- fore it was the duty of the Government to give effect t o the will of the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has also taken the stand that the concept of Rulership, privy purse and the privileges guaranteed to, the Rulers without any relatable function and responsibility have become incompatible with democracy, equity and social justice in the context- of India of today.
RatioOfTheDecision	These contentions raise political issues.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> is not the forum for going into these issues nor is it concerned with the political passions surrounding the issues arising for decision in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our primary function in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	is to interpret the relevant provision of the Constitution and to see.
RatioOfTheDecision	whether the complaint of the petitioners that some of their fundamental rights have been infringed is correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also not for this <ORG>, except to the extent it bears on the question of interpretation of the Constitution, to go into the historical background of any constitutional provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the meaning of a provision is plain and unambiguous, its historical background becomes irrelevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if there is any  ambiguity, 'in interpreting the same, it is.
RatioOfTheDecision	permissible, for the <ORG> to take into consideration the object intended to be achieved by that provision as well as the surrounding circumstances which may bring out the intention of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The respondent, though in a somewhat vague way, has raised the plea of <ORG> policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	That plea appears to me to be irrelevant in the context of this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution has laid down a policy, as is contended on behalf of the petitioners, with respect to matters with which we are concerned, that policy cannot be departed from either by the legislature or by the executive.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the legislature nor the executive can have a policy which runs counter to the policy laid down by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this country the voice of the Constitution is paramount.
RatioOfTheDecision	On matters on which the Constitution speaks, no one else can speak, Every organ of the <ORG> in this country has to function within the limits prescribed by the Constitution.It has no power dehors that  derived from the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its powers are only those The learned Attorney-General in the course of his arguments, time and again, tried to impress on us that the will of the people has to be respected and as it is the desire of the people that Rulership should be abolished, it had become imperative for the Government to advise the President to make the impugned orders.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioners deny that there is any such public opinion.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are not in a position to go into this controversy.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our duty is to obey the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question of public opinion is not relevant for our purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many of the safeguards provided in the Constitution are for the benefit of the minorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Government might have acted with the best of intentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the real question is whether it has acted within the powers conferred on it by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection it would be worthwhile to borrow and adapt some (1) the observations of Chief Justice <NAME> in <CITATION_554839> (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	If the courts in this country f ace up to, important and none too easy task of declaring void any of the important policy decisions taken by the <ORG> it is not out of any desire to tilt at executive authority in a crusader's spirit, but in the discharge of a duty plainly laid upon them by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is especially true as regards the fundamental rights, as to which this Court has been assigned the role of a sentinel on the quivive.
RatioOfTheDecision	In these cases as in other cases we do not seek to sit in judgment on Govern- ment's policies.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the concern of the legislative and the executive organs of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the Constitution has imposed a special duty on this Court to preserve and protect the Constitution--we, only seek to discharge that duty.
Precedent	Now coming to the scope of cl.
Precedent	(22) of Art.
Precedent	366 , it is necessary to notice that Art.
Statute	366 is an article which defines 30, expressions appearing in one or more of the articles in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article starts by saying that "In this Constitution, unless the context otherwise req uires, the following expressions have the meanings hereby respectively assigned to them.......".
RatioOfTheDecision	From this it is clear that the meaning given to the expressions mentioned in that article are only for the purpose of the Constitution and not for any other purpose as he ld by this Court in <CITATION_21305> (2 ).
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (15) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366 defines an "Indian State" as meaning "any territory which <ORG> recognised 'as such a State".
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noted that no "Indian State" as such exists after the Constitution came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	But yet as that expression has been used in the Constitution in some places for certain purposes, it became necessary to define, that expression and not because that there is an Indian State now.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly Rulers of Indian States disappeared as s oon as their territories were merged in India and all those quandum Rulers became citizens of India-see <NAME> case (supra) and <CITATION_1953650> .
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) The Rulers referred to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) have no kingdom or subj ects to rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	They have no ruling power.
RatioOfTheDecision	They do not have dual capacity firstly 'as citizens of India and secondly as Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their rulership is merely a status entitling them to privy purse and (1)  S.C.R.
Precedent	597 at p. 605.
Precedent	(3) [1964] 5, S.C.R.
Precedent	I (2) [1961] 2, S.C.R, 501.
Precedent	161  certain privileges.
Precedent	As Arts.
Precedent	291, 362, 366(21)(a) and (b) (before its deletion) as well as Entry 34 of List I of Sch.
RatioOfTheDecision	VII refer to Rulers, it became necessary to define that expression.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) defines "Ruler" thus:  "Ruler" in relation to an Indian State means the Prince, Chief or other person by whom any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in clause (1) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 was entered into and who for the time being is recognised by the President as the Ruler of the State and include any person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the successor of such Ruler.  "
Statute	This clause has two parts namely :  (1) the Prince, Chief or other person of an Indian State who had entered into any Covenant or Agreement as is r eferred to in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 and who is for the time being recognised by the President as the Ruler of the State; and (2) any person who for the time being is recognised by the President as the successor of such a Ruler namely the Ruler who entered into the Covenant or Agreement referred to earlier and recognised by the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words "other person" in the first part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) means someone analogous to a Prince or Chief of a former Indian State who had entered into the Covenant or Agreement referred to in that clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be some third person because, no person other than a ruler of an Indian State had entered into any Covenant or Agreement with the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words "other person" should be read ajusidem genesis with the words "other person" should be read ejusidem genesis with the words were known by various names such as <NAME>,, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	To avoid listing all those names in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22 ), the draftsman has used the words "other person" but the, me aning of those words has been made clear by the words accompanying the words "other person' viz.
RatioOfTheDecision	by whom any such agreemeat as is referred to in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 was entered into and who for the time being is recognised by the President as Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now coming to the second part of that clause, 'here again the words "any person" refers to the person who at the relevant time is the successor of the person who entered into the Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is made clear by the expression "for the time being is recognised by the President as the successor of such Ruler", such Ruler being the Ruler referred to in the first limb of the clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) contemplates two classes of persons who are to be recognised by the President as Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first group consists of those persons who entered into the Covenant with the Dominion of India and the second group their successors.
RatioOfTheDecision	Coming to the first group, the President has no power to recognise any one other than who had entered into the Covenant or Agreement and so , far as the second group is concerned, he can only recognise the successor of the person who had entered into the Covenant or the Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Successor" is a term of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Succession is regulated by law or custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	It, is no doubt true that it "or the President to decide as to who is the, successor for the time being ,of the person who had entered into the Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President cannot create a successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	He can only recognise the successor.
RatioOfTheDecision	His power is only to find out who is the successor at the relevant time of the Ruler who entered into the Covenant ,or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	Recognition is not the same thing as appointment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Recognition means the power to locate and not a power to create.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence the power conferred on the President under the second part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is a very limited power.
RatioOfTheDecision	That power is no doubt an executive power but the same has to be exercised in accordance with law.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words it has to be exercised as a ,quasi-judicial power.
RatioOfTheDecision	SD far as the first part is concerned, the Presid ent has no power to recognise any person other than the Ruler who entered into the Covenant or Agreement with the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	We shall presently see that he has a constitutional duty to recognise, the Ruler of an Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence the words ",for the time being" in the first part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366 can only come into play if there was any error in locating the person who entered into the Covenant or Agreement, the condition for, the recognition being that the person recognised must be the person who entered into the Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the second part is concerned the expression "for the time being" is relevant as the question of recognition of a new Ruler arises on the death of each Ruler.
