RatioOfTheDecision	On each of those occasions, the President has to find out as to who is the successor according to law and in the absence of law, according to custom, of the Ruler who ,entered into the Covenant or Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The procedure of recognition of Rulers appears to have been intended as a status symbol and also to a void the necessity of hunting up Covenants and Agreements at the time of payment of privy purses and while affor ding other privileges and rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) contemplates that for each Indian State, there shall be a Ruler at any given point of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Article does not say that the, President may recognise a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand it speaks of the Ruler who "for the time being is recognised by the President", an expression which contemplates the continuity of Rulership and not merely of its possible existence.
RatioOfTheDecision	A Rulership of an Indian State can only disappear if both the original, Ruler who entered into the Covenant or Agreement as well his success ors cease to exist as in that case President cannot recognise any one as the Ruler of that State.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the above discussion it follows that the power of the President under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is fully regulated.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this context we may refer to the definition of the "Ruler" in s. 311 (1) of the Government of India Act, 1935 which says ""Ruler" in relation to an In dian State means the Prince, Chief or other person recoginised by His Majesty as the Ruler of the State".
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to recognise given to His Majesty,under this section is blanket power.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is subject to no limitation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under that section Any one could have been recognised as the Ruler of an Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	No such power is conferred on: the President under Art.. 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	I shall now proceed to consider whether the President has power to say that he will not recognise a Ruler for an Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was urged on behalf of the respondent that a power to recognise includes a power not to recognise.
RatioOfTheDecision	Evidently this contention is based on s. 21 of the General, Clauses Act which Says "Where, by any Central Act or Regulation, a power to issue notifications, orders, rules or bye-laws is conferred, then that power includes a power, exercisable 'in the like manner and subject to the like sanction and conditions if any, to add to, amend, vary or rescind any notifications, orders, rules or bye-laws so issued."
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of  Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	367 of the Constitution unless the context .otherwise requires, the General Clauses Act , subject to any adaptations and modifications made therein under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	372 applies for the interpretation of the Constitution as it applies for the interpretation of an Act of the legislature of the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have not thought it necessary to go into the question whether the recognition referred to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) can be considered as a power to issue notifications or orders as in my opinion that clause imposes a constitutional duty on the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	No discretion.
RatioOfTheDecision	is left to the President to recognise or not to recognise the Ruler of an Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	In that view, s. 21 of the General Clauses Act, 1897 is irrelevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have already.
RatioOfTheDecision	seen that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) contemplates that each Indian State must have a Ruler, at all times so long as the Ruler who entered into the Covenant or Agreement or a successor of his is in existence otherwise Arts.- 291 and 362 will become meaningless.
RatioOfTheDecision	They will be empty shells if "R uler" referred to in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 (b)  Art.
Precedent	362 and Entry 34 of List I of the Seventh Schedule must ,necessarily be that person who is recognised as Ruler by the President under Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	If the President fails to or declines to discharge his function under Art.
Precedent	366(22 ), Arts.
Precedent	291 and 362 would become inoperative.
Precedent	In effect the benefit conferred bythose Arts.
Precedent	will be denied to the person entitled to be recognised as a Ruler of a particular Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further the legislative power given under Entry 34 of List I of the Seventh Schedule would disappear.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is to give meaning to Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291, 362 and Entry 34 of List I of the Seventh Schedule, a duty is imposed on the President to recognise the Ruler of each Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) imposes a constitutional duty on the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	To enable him to discharge that duty, certain limited powers are; conferred on him.
RatioOfTheDecision	While discharging his duty under the first part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22 ), he has to locate the person who according to law can be said to have e ntered into the Covenants or Agreements and under the second limb his duty is to find out the successor of the Ruler coming within the scope of the first limb.
RatioOfTheDecision	As mentioned earlier the recognition of the Ruler who executed the Covenant or Agreement is a mere formality.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the recognition of the successor of that Ruler is concerned, in case of dispute, it becomes the duty of the President to decide as to who is the successor of the Ruler who executed the Covenant or Agreement at the relevant- time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Evidently the Constitution makers were of the opinion that any dispute as to who is the "Ruler" for the purpose of the Constitution should not be left to be decided by courts of law because such a procedure would involve years of delay in determining the person who is entitled to the benefit of the privy purse and the privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence that question was left to the exclusive decision of., the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	Despite the fact that exclusive power was given to the President to recognise the successor of the original  Ruler, the procedure that invariably adopted in case of disputed succession was to act on the basis of the recommendation of either of 'a <ORG> judge who had inquired into the matter or of a committee presided over by a <ORG> judge, set up for that purpose.
Precedent	That is what happened when disputes arose as to the succession to the Rulers of the States, of Sirohi and Dholpur.
Precedent	In my opinion Art.
Precedent	366 (22) imposes a duty on the President and for that purpose has conferred on him certain powers.
Precedent	In other words the power conferred on the President under that provision is one coupled with duty.
Precedent	There are similar powers conferred on the President under the Constitution.
Precedent	Under Chap.
Precedent	XVI of the Constitution certain special provisions were made for the benefit of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Seats were reserved for them both in the <ORG> as well as in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain other benefits were also secured to them in the matter of appointments to services and posts in connection with the  affairs of the <ORG> or of a State.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the Constitution did not specify which castes were Scheduled Castes and which Tribes were Scheduled Tribes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	341 (1) and 342(1) of the Constitu-  165  tion, the President was given power to specify the castes which he considered to be Scheduled Castes and the Tribes which he considered to be Scheduled Tribes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though both the Articles say the President "may" specify the Castes which he considers as Scheduled and Tribes which he considers Scheduled, it is clear that a constitutional duty was imposed on him to specify which castes were Scheduled Castes and which Tribes were Scheduled Tribes for the purpose of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "may" in those clauses must be read as "must" because if he had failed or declined to.
RatioOfTheDecision	specify the Castes and Tribes, Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	330, 332, 334, 335, 338 and 340 would have become inoperative and the constitutional guarantees given to the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes would have become meaningless.
RatioOfTheDecision	At this stage it may be noted  that under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(24) and (25) Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes are defined as such Castes, races, tribes, tribal communities or their parts or groups within them as are deemed under Art 341 and 342 respectively.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again under 'cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(7) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366 , the President is given power to determine for the purpose of the Constitution the "corresponding Provinces" "corresponding Indian State" or "corresponding State" in case of doubt.
RatioOfTheDecision	This, again is a duty imposed on the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	He cannot refuse to discharge that duty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now coming to the contention that power to recognise the: Rulers includes power not to recognise, we shall test the correctness of that contention with reference to some other Articles in the Constitution which deal with certain constitutional duties of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to appoint <ORG> is that of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> alone can hold the elections of the President, Vice-President, members of that <ORG> and the State legislatures.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President can not decline to appoint the Election Commissioners.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not in the power of the cabinet to advise the President not to appoint one or more of Election Commissioners even if some future cabinet should think that the elections are trappings of feudalism.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly the cabinet cannot advise the President not to appoint a Governor and thus destroy the federal structure of our Constitution or not to appoint the Chief Justice of <ORG> or of <ORG> and' thereby remove those courts and thus make a mockery of the fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President cannot do indirectly, what the legislature cannot do directly.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is wrong to mistake a duty for a right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ruler as referred to in some of the Provisions of the Constitution is an entity created by the Constitution to further certain, purposes recognised by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That entity cannot be abolished either by the executive or by the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore the argument advanced on behalf of the respondent that the power to recognise the Ruler includes w ithin itself the Power not to recognise is clearly a fallacious one.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary for our present purpose to go into the question whether a Ruler once recognised can be de-recognised by the President and if so under what circumstances.
Precedent	We were told that there was one instance of derecognition of a recognised Ruler namely that of the former Ruler of Baroda.
Precedent	That derecognition was not challenged before any court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence its validity remains undecided.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case we are concerned not with derecognition of one or more Rulers for some reason or.
RatioOfTheDecision	other but of the abolition of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the reasons mentioned earlier, it is not possible to spell out a power to abolish the <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	3 66 (22).
Precedent	It was strenuously argued by the learned Attorney General that the power of recognition of the Rulers found in Art.
Precedent	366(22) is a facet of the paramountcy enjoyed by the British <ORG> before the 15th August, 1947.
RatioOfTheDecision	No such plea was taken in the counteraffidavit.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument of the learned Attorney General  on this point was somewhat indefinite.
RatioOfTheDecision	He was hesitant to call the power embodied in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) as a paramount power but yet he was repeatedly asserting that it contains certain aspects of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is strange that the learned Attorney General representing <ORG> should have claimed that <ORG> inherited any aspects of the paramountcy exercised by the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paramountcy as claimed by the British Rulers was one of the manifestation of imperialism.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is surprising that the <ORG> of this country whose people had fought imperialism for years and who are even today supporting both morally as well as materially the countries which are fighting imperialism should claim to have inherited even a fraction of impeperialism should claim to have inherited even a fraction of impemountcy is the very antithesis of rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was a power exercised by a superior sovereign over the subordinate sovereigns.
RatioOfTheDecision	I fail to see how <ORG> can consider itself as a s uperior power in its relationship with the citizens of this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The doctrine of paramountcy even during the days of the Imperial rule had nothing to do with <ORG> relationship with its subjects.
RatioOfTheDecision	Herein we are concerned with the power exercisable by the President under a provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nature and scope of that power must be spelled out from the language of the provision and from the purpose intended to be served by that provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is an insult to our Constitutionl to say that any facet of imperialism has crept into it.
RatioOfTheDecision	One should have thought that paramountcy so far as this country was con- cerned was dead and was deeply buried as far back as on the 15th August 1947.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its resurrection in any form is repugnant to our 'Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that even after August, 1947, on some occasions some of our leaders, referred to the existence of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that reference is not to the paramountcy which was the insignia of imperialism but the paramountcy of geographical c ompulsions, economic compulsions, the compulsions, of public opinion and need for common defence, all operating in favour of the unity of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The effect of these forces was pithly described, as a sort of paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that paramountcy has nothing to do with paramountcy claimed by the British.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned orders are also unconstitutional for the reason that the power conferred under Art 366(22) is exercised for a collateral purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier, power to recognise Rulers was conferred for the purpose of implementing some of the provisions of the Constitution and not for denuding the contents of those provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have earlier seen how the impugned orders came to be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Government of India sought to amend the Constitution by deleting Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291, 362 and clause 22 of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366.
RatioOfTheDecision	But as the bill seeking the amendment of the Constitution failed to get the required majority in <ORG>, that attempt failed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Within hours after the said bill was rejected, the cabinet met and advised the Presid ent to pass the impugned orders.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is clearly an, attempt to do indirectly what the Government could not do directly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an exercise of power is impermeable under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	Exercise of a constitutional power for collateral' reasons has been considered by this <ORG> in several decisions as a fraud on that power- <CITATION_599701> (-).
RatioOfTheDecision	Breach of any of the Constitutional provisions even if made to further a popular cause is bound to be dangerous precedent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Disrespect to, the Constitution is bound to be broadened from precedent to precedent and before long the entire Constitution may be treated with contempt and held up to ridicule.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is what happened to the Weimar, Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution or any of its provisions have ceased to serve the needs of the people, ways must be found to change them but it is impermissible to by-pass the Constitutions its provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every contravention of the letter or the spirit of the Constitution is bound to have chain reaction.
RatioOfTheDecision	For that reason, also the impug ned orders must be held to be ultra vires Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned orders also violate Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	53 (1 ) of the Consti- tution which directs the President that the executive power of the Union shall be exercised by him either directly or through the, officers subordinate to him in :accordance with the Constitution.
Precedent	Further Art.
Precedent	73(1) prescribes that the executive power-of the Union must be exercised subject to the provisions of the Constitution.
Precedent	The executive is bound to obey this mandate.
Precedent	It, has no, competence to exercise the executive power in violation of the mandates given by the C onstitution.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	291 gives a mandate to the executive to pay the privy purses guaranteed to the Rulers exempt from all taxes on income.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	366 (22) (1) (1963).
Precedent	Suppl.
Precedent	1.
Precedent	S.C.R.
Precedent	439.
Precedent	168  imposes a constitutional duty on the President to recognise the Rulers of the Indian States.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	362 requires the executive that due regard should be given to the guarantees and assurances given under the Agreements or Covenants entered into  with the former Rulers of the Indian <ORG>s.
Precedent	The President on the advice of the cabinet has disregarded the mandate of Arts.
Precedent	53(1), 73(1), 291,362 and 366(22).
Argument	That being so his order must be held to be ultra vires the Constitution, hence a nullity.
Argument	It was urged on behalf of the petitioners that the members of the cabinet who advised the President to issue the impugned orders were bound by their oath to bear true faith and allegiance to the Constitution; but they have shown scant respect for their oath, treating the same as a mere formality; they have thereby not only broken their oath but have damaged the Constitution as well.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary to pronounce on this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion it is not open to, the executive or for that matter to any of the organs of the <ORG> to disregard the provisions ,of the Constitution merely because those provisions do not accord ,with its views.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mandate of every provision of the Constitution is a binding mandate.
RatioOfTheDecision	No one has power to depart from that mandate or  circumvent it, whatever his views about the appropriateness of the mandates may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution or any part of it has now become out of tune with the present day society of ours, appropriate steps may be taken to alter the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is no virtue to uphold the Constitution when it suits vs. What is important, nay necessary, is to uphold it even when it is inconvenient to do so.
Argument	It was contended on behalf of the respondent that the im- pugned orders were made in exercise of the political power of the <ORG> which according to it, is an incident of the sovereignty.
Precedent	In support of that contention reliance was placed on the decision of this Court in <CITATION_347856> .
Precedent	(1) The facts of that case are :  After the death of the previous Ruler of Dholpur who had been recognised by the President under Art.
Precedent	366(22 ), there was dispute as regards his successor.
Precedent	That dispute was inquired into by a committee presided over by the Chief Justice of <ORG>.
Precedent	On the recommendation of that committ ee, the President was pleased to recognise Maharaja <NAME> as the successor of the previous Ruler.
Precedent	Kr.
Argument	Shri Vir Rajendra Singh challenged that decision by means of a .writ petition under Art.
Precedent	226 of the Constitution.'
Precedent	That petition was dismissed by <ORG>.
Precedent	In appeal this <ORG> affirmed-  (1) [1970] 2, S.C.R.
Precedent	631 the decision of <ORG>.
Precedent	I was a party to that decision.
Precedent	In that decision, it was held that the recognition granted by the President under Art.
Precedent	366(22) could not be challenged in court of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only point in dispute in that case was as to who was ;the successor to the deceased Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> came to the conclusion that under the circumstances of that case the decision of the President was not open to challenge.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the course of the judgment it was observed :  'The recognition of the Ruler is a right to succeed to the gaddi of the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	This recognition of Rulership by the President is an exercise of political power vested in the President and is thus an instance o f purely executive jurisdiction of the President.'
RatioOfTheDecision	What is said in that case is that the President while acting under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) is exercising his executive jurisdiction and that jurisdiction was described as "political power".
RatioOfTheDecision	That expression may be inappropriate but that is not the ratio of the decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was a casual observation.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is nothing like a political power under our Constitution in the matter of relationship between the executive and the citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution recognises only three powers viz.
RatioOfTheDecision	the legislative power, the, judicial power and the executive power.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not recognize any other power.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our country the executive cannot exercise any 'sovereignty over the citiznes.
RatioOfTheDecision	The legal sovereignty in this country vests with the Constitution and the political sovereignty is with the people of this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The executive possesses no sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no analogy between our President and the British Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President is a creature of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	He can only act in accordan ce with the Constitution.
Precedent	It is true that some aspect of the executive power of the <ORG> is for the sake of convenience called political power but it is nonetheless an executive power derived from the Constitution.
Precedent	It was next urged that we cannot go into the validity of the impugned orders or even as to the scope of Art.
Precedent	366(22) in view of Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	We shall, while examining the ambit of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 see the hollowness of this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	Earlier, I have in a general way, referred to some of the political events that took place in the years 1947 to 1949.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to consider some of the contentions raised by the Counsel for the parties, relating to the scope and effect of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , it is now necessary to refer in some detail to some aspects of those events.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have earlier referred to the instruments of Accession executed by various Rulers of Indian States.
RatioOfTheDecision	By means of those Instruments, the concerned Indian States became federating units of the Dominion of India though under those Instruments, powers wer e con-  170  ferred on the Dominion legislature, executive and judiciary only in respect of three subjects viz.
RatioOfTheDecision	Defence, External Affairs and Communications.
RatioOfTheDecision	But nonetheless as a result of the accession, the concerned Indian States became parts of the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the time those Instruments were executed, no question of either guaranteeing the privy purses to <ORG> or preserving their privileges arose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence those Instruments did not refer to any rights and privileges of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Very soon after the execution of the Instruments of Accession other developments took place in quick I succession.
RatioOfTheDecision	Most of the small Indian States fully merged in the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under the merger Agreements the privileges then enjoyed by the <ORG>, their right to get the, privy purses fixed under the agreement as well as some of the rights of the third parties referred to in the agreements were guaranteed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Excepting in the case of Bhopal, the privy purses to be paid to the <ORG> were to be paid from out of the r evenues of their former <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under the Merger Agreement entered into between the Governor- General and <NAME>, the Nawab was entitled to receive the privy purse stipulated therein from <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not stated in the agreement that the same has to come out from the revenues of Bhopal <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The privy purses payable to all those Rulers were free of all taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	In some of the Merger Agreements rights were also created in favour of the third parties, such as guaranteeing the continuity of the services of the permanent members of <ORG> of those <ORG>s as well as the payment of pensions due to the retired civil servants.
RatioOfTheDecision	In several of the Merger Agreements it is provided that if there was any dispute as to whether a particular item of property is the private property of the Ruler or the property of the <ORG>, that dispute was to be decided by an authority to be appointed as provided in those agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	In most of those agreements, it is provided that the succession to  the <NAME>hip should be according to law and custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	That provision was a redundant provision as succession means succession according to law or custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	No one can succeed to a deceased person excepting according to law or custom.
RulingByLowerCourt	Those agreements also provide that no enquiry should be made by or under the authority of <ORG> and no proceedings should be taken in any court in their former States in respect of anything done or omitted to be done by the <NAME> or under their authority, whether in a personal capacity or otherwise during the period of their administration of their States.
RulingByLowerCourt	In those agreements, it is further provided that no suit should be brought against the <NAME> of the merged States in any of the Courts in the Dominion except with the previous sanction of <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	Under the Merger Agreement executed by the Ruler of Bilas- pur, the Ruler was entitled to a privy purse of Rs.
Fact	7,0,000/- per year but that included a sum of Rs.
Fact	10,000/- as allowance to the Yuvraj.
Fact	Un der the Merger Agreement executed by the <NAME> of Bhopal, the State of Bhopal was merged into the Dominion of India for a period of five years only.
Fact	Art.
Fact	IV of the Merger Agreement provided that the income derived annually from the share of the <NAME> in the original investment by <NAME> in the Bhopal State Railway, which share was agreed to be Rupees five lakhs and fifty-five thousand, shall be treated as the personal income of the <NAME> and shall be paid by <ORG> to the <NAME>, and his successors.
Fact	Article VII of the Agreement provided that the succession to the Throne of Bhopal State shall be governed by and regulated in accordance with the provisions of the Act known as 'the Succession to the Throne of Bhopal Act of 1947' which was in force in the State at the time of the agreement.
Fact	Under the Merger Agreement entered into by the Maharaja of Manipur, he was given a right to the use of the Residences known as 'Redlands' and 'Les Chatalettes' in Shillong and the property in the town of G auhati known as "Manipuri Basti" though all those properties were considered as the <ORG> properties.
Fact	Then came the <ORG>s Merger (Governors' Provinces) Order 1949, an order made under s. 290(A) of the Government of India Act, 1935.
Fact	Under this Order, several of the <ORG>s that had merged in the Dominion of India were added on to one or the other of the Provinces.
Fact	Thereafter those <ORG>s became a part of those Provinces.
RulingByLowerCourt	Section 7(1) of that Order provides "All liabilites in respect of such loans, guarantees and other financial obligations of <ORG> as arise out of the governance of a merged <ORG>, including in particular the liability for the payment of any sums to the Ruler of the merged <ORG> on account of his privy purse or to other persons in the merged <ORG> on account of political pensions and the like, shall as from the appointed day, be liabilities of the absorbing province, unless the loan, guarantee or other financial obligation is relatable to central purposes."
Fact	This Order was mad e on July 27, 1949.
Fact	Under this Order fifty-five Indian States merged in the Bombay Province, three in Madras, two in Bihar, fifteen in Central Provinces and Berar, three in East Punjab and twenty-four in Orissa.
Fact	It was not disputed that the Merger Order is a legislative measure.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its validity was not challenged before us.
Fact	In view of that Order, the liability to pay the privy purses of the Rulers whose former States had been added to any particular Province, became the liability of that Province,- a liability imposed by law.
Fact	Whatever might have been the nature of the liability undertaken by the Govern-  -L744 Sup.
Fact	CI/71 ment of the Dominion of India under the various Merger Agreements those liabilities came to be recognised by law and made a part of the Municipal law and thereafter they became enforceable as against the concerned Province.
Fact	It may be noted that this Order was made long before the Constitution came into force.
Fact	This Order was subsequently amended and a few more Indian States were included in one o r the other of the Provinces.
Fact	From the foregoing, it is seen that before the Constitution came into force, the liability to pay the privy purses to several of the <ORG> whose States had directly merged with the Dominion of India became that of some of the Provinces and ceased to be that of the Dominion of India.
Fact	Under the Merger Agreements excepting in the case of Bhopal, the privy purses to the former <ORG> were payable from the revenues of their former States.
Fact	But after the Merger Order they became payable from the revenues of the concerned provinces.
Fact	At this stage we may also note that under the Merger Agreements, the privy purses payable to the <ORG> were free of all taxes.
Fact	We may further note that under the Merger Agreements, there were several other rights created either in favour of the concerned <ORG> or in favour of the third parties.
Fact	The Merger Order is silent about those rights.
Fact	Now we come to those States which formed unions.
Precedent	There were five such unions namely  1.
Precedent	United States of Kathiawar;   2.
Precedent	United States of Gwalior, Indore and Malwa (Madhya Bharat).
Precedent	3.
Precedent	Patiala and East Punjab States Union.
Precedent	4.
Precedent	United States of Rajasthan and  5.
Precedent	United States of Travancore and Cochin.
Fact	Those unions were formed on regional basis.
Fact	Various Indian States in a particular region merged together and formed a union.
Fact	The concerned States entered into a Covenant under which the union was formed.
Fact	To those Covenants, the Dominion of India was not a party.
Fact	Under those covenants, the covenanting States agreed to entrust to <ORG> to be formed in accordance with the provisions of the covenant the work of framing a Constitution for the union.
Fact	Each of those unions were to have a <NAME> who was to be the head of the union.'
Fact	There were provisions in those covenants for the formation of <ORG> to aid and advise the <NAME> in the exercise of some of his functions.
Fact	Under those covenants, the Ruler of each of the covenanting State was entitled to receive a fixed privy purse annually from th e revenues of the concerned union.
RulingByLowerCourt	That amount was to be free of all taxes, whether imposed by the Government of the concerned union or by <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	In the matter of raising, maintaining and administering the military force of the concerned union, the Rajpramukh was to act subject to any directions and instructions that may from time to time be given by <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	The covenants provided that the Rulers of the covenanting States as also the members of their families should continue to be entitled to all their personal privileges, dignities and titles enjoyed by them.
RulingByLowerCourt	The succession to the <NAME> was to take place according to law and custom.
RulingByLowerCourt	Questions of disputed succession in regard to covenanting <ORG> were to be decided by <ORG> on the re- commendation of <ORG> to be constituted in accordance with the provisions of the covenants.
RulingByLowerCourt	The Secretary, <ORG> on behalf of <ORG> concuffed to the covenants and guaranteed t o all its provisions.
RulingByLowerCourt	The concurrence of <ORG> to the covenants was necessary as, the covenanting <ORG> had earlier acceded to the Dominion of India.
RulingByLowerCourt	In view of the formation of unions, in the place of old Indian <ORG> new units were to come into existence and therefore it was necessary for them to execute fresh Instruments of Accession and that could be done only with consent of the Dominion of India.
RulingByLowerCourt	So far as the guaranteeing of these covenants is concerned it could only mean a political guarantee and not a guarantee in the sense of undertaking any financial obligations.
RulingByLowerCourt	What the Dominion of India guaranteed was the provisions of the covenant which included provision relating to the formation of <ORG>, the appointment of <ORG> etc.
RulingByLowerCourt	Under the covenants the liability to pay the privy purses of the covenanting <ORG> was that of the concerned union and not that of the Dominion of India.
RulingByLowerCourt	Further the privy purses to be paid to the <ORG> were to be paid free  of all taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	From these it is seen that before Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291,362, 363 and 366(22) came into force, <ORG> had no liability in the matter of payment of privy purses to <ORG> of the covenanting States.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the matter of deciding any dispute as regards succession, the Dominion of India had no responsibility.
RatioOfTheDecision	That had to be decided by the agencies created under the covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under some of the covenants some of the covenanting <ORG> were given special rights e.g.
RatioOfTheDecision	under Art.
Fact	XVIII of the covenant under which Madhya Bharat union was formed, it was provided "Notwithstanding anything in the preceding provisions of this covenant, the <ORG> of Gwalior and Indore shall continue to have and exercise their present ,powers of suspension, remission or commutation of death sentences in respect of any person who may have been or is hereinafter, sentenced to death for a capital offence committed within the territories of Gwalior or of Indore, as the case may be."
Fact	Under Art.
Fact	VIII of the covenant entered i nto by the Rulers of Travancore and Cochin forming the United State of Travan- core and Cochin, it was provided that the obligation of the covenanting State of Travancore to contribute from its general revenue a sum of Rs.
Fact	50 lakhs every year to the <ORG> fund shall from the appointed day be the obligation of the United State and the said amounts shall be payable therefrom and the Rajpramukh shall cause the said amount to be paid every year to the Travancore <ORG> Board and <ORG> referred to in sub-clause (b) of that article respectively.
Fact	In respect of the administration of Padamanahhaswamy Temple the right of the Ruler of Travancore was preserved under Art.
Fact	VIII(b) of the covenant.
Fact	Similarly the existing rights of the Rulers of Travancore and Cochin as regards the management of certain temples and funds were preserved.
Fact	They were also given a right to nominate some members to some of the statutory Boards.
Fact	From the foraging it is seen that under the various covenants, several rights in a ddition to the right of receiving privy purses had been created in favour of the <NAME> of some of the covenanting States.
Fact	In the draft Constitution, there were no articles similar to Arts.
Fact	291, 362, 363 and 366(22).
Fact	Sometime before October 14, 1949 <ORG>, which was instrumental in bringing about the merger of the States with <ORG> wrote to the drafting committee that the guarantees given to the <NAME> in regard to privy purses should be given constitutional section.
Fact	Further it desired that so far as the privileges and other rights of the <NAME> are concerned, the same must find recognition in the Constitution though it may not be possible to give any constitutional guarantee in respect of them.
Fact	It is in pursuance of this request the drafting committee introduced Art.
Fact	267(A) (present Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 ), Art.
Fact	302-A (present Art.
Fact	362) on October 13, 1949 Art.
Fact	303(1) (present Art , 366) (22) on October 14, 1949 and Art.
Fact	302(A) (present Art.
Fact	363 ) on October 16, 1949 into the draft Constit ution.
Statute	Art.
Statute	291 of the Constitution as it now stands after its amendment by the 7th Amendment Act reads :  "Where under any covenant or agreement entered into by the Ruler of any Indian State before the commencement of this Constitution, the payment of any sums, free of tax, has been guaranteed or assured by the Government of the Dominion of India to any Ruler of such State as privy purse--  (a) such sums shall be charged on and paid out of the Consolidated Fund of India; and  (b) the sums so paid to any Ruler shall be exempt from all taxes on income."
Statute	Dealing with Art.
Statute	291 , this is what the White Paper says in paragraph 238 :  " Art.
Statute	291 , thus, embodies constitutional sanction for the due fulfilment of <ORG> guarantees and assurances in respect of privy purses and provides for the necessary adjustments in respect of privy purse payments necessitated by changed conditions."
Statute	Art.
Statute	291 , has four principal ingredients namely (1) the conditions giving rise to the liability to pay the privy  purses; (2) charging of the privy purses payable on <ORG>; (3) the payment of the same from out of the Consolidated Fund; and (4) the sums so paid to any Ruler to be exempt from all taxes on income.
Argument	According to Mr. <NAME>, learned Counsel for some of the petitioners, Art.
Argument	291 , guarantees the payment of privy purses referred to in various Merger Agreements and Covenants to the concerned <NAME>, charges the same on <ORG> and makes them payable out of that fund to the <NAME>, exempt from all taxes on income.
Argument	He contended that Art.
Argument	291 confers a legal right on a Ruler to claim the privy purse to which he is entitled to, from the Dominion of India.
Argument	He asserts that the right created in favour of the <NAME>, is enforceable in court of law.
Argument	But according to the learned Attorney-General, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 does not create any legal right in favour of the <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	merely gives a moral assurance to the <NAME> that the privy purses guaranteed under the Covenants an d Agreements will be paid by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	He further contended that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 merely recognizes the obligation undertaken by the Dominion of India either under the Merger Agreements or under the Covenants and it does not create any new right or obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him the expression that "such fund shall be charged on <ORG>" does not mean that a lien on <ORG> is created for the payment of privy purses; it only means that the amount payable as privy purses is not votable.
RatioOfTheDecision	He asserted that the expression "paid out of" in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 merely refers to the Fund out of which the payment is to be made and not that it should be paid to any person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (b) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 does not according to him give any direction to <ORG> to pay to the Rulers the agreed privy purse but it merely says that the privy purse, if and when paid to any Ruler will be exempt from all taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion the contentions advanced by the learned At- torney-Gen eral are falacious.
RatioOfTheDecision	The liability undertaken under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is a new liability and not an affirmation of an existing liability.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier, the liability to pay the privy purses of most of the Rulers who merged their States with the Dominion of India had been transfered to one or the other provinces.
RatioOfTheDecision	The liability to pay privy purses to the Rulers who entered into Covenants for forming unions was that of the concerned union and not that of the Dominion of India.
Precedent	In the case of most of the Rulers of States which merged in the Dominion of India until Art.
Precedent	291 came into force, the Dominion of India had no liability to pay the privy purses.
Precedent	For the first time after Art.
Precedent	291 came into force, the privy purses were made payable from out of <ORG>.
Precedent	Till then they were payable firstly out of the revenues of the concerned State which merged into the Dominion of India and later on by one or the other provinces from out of its revenues and in the case of the covenanting States, the privy pur ses payable to the covenanting <ORG> were payable from out of the revenues of the concerned union.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier, the privy purses payable either to the <ORG> of the merged States or to those of the covenanting States, were free of all taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the privy purses payable under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 are only exempt from all taxes on income and riot all taxes.
RatioOfTheDecision	To summarize, under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , <ORG> for the first time undertook the liability to pay the privy purses in respect of most of the <ORG> of the Indian States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fund from which the privy purses are made payable under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is different from those from which they were payable earlier.
RatioOfTheDecision	The terms of payment, to some extent are also different inasmuch as the privy purses provided under the Merger Agreements and Covenants were free of all taxes but the privy purses guaranteed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 are exempt only from tax on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	In support of his contention that the liability undertaken under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , is merely a continuation of the earlier liability  the learned Attorney-General strongly relied on the first part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 which says :  177  "Where under any covenant or agreement entered into by the Ruler of any Indian State before the commencement of this Constitution, the payment of any sums, free of tax, has been guaranteed or assured by the Government of the Dominion of India to any Ruler of such State as privy pure........"  From this he wants us to conclude that the liability under- taken under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is nothing but a continuation of the liability arising under the Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here again the learned Attorney-General is not correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	That part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 does not create any liability.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only a legislation by incorporation.
RatioOfTheDecision	That part of the Article points out the person who is entitled to the privy purse and the amount payable to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was a legislative device adopted for the convenience of drafting.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would have been a cumbersome process to list all the names of the Rulers who are entitled to privy purses and the amount payab le to each of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	To avoid that difficulty, relevant portions of Agreements and Covenants were bodily lifed from those documents and incorporated into Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	This is a well known drafting device.
Precedent	Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is no way linked with the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Convenants and Agreements only continue as evidence as to matters mentioned in the first part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291.
RatioOfTheDecision	After Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 came into force, there is no legal relationship between the Covenants and Agreements and that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Article read with Article 366(22) constitute a self-contained code in the matter of payment of privy purses.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those Articles operate on their own force.
RatioOfTheDecision	In several provisions of the Constitutions, the device of legislating by incorporation has been adopted-see Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	105(3 ), Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	106 , cls.
RatioOfTheDecision	2, 3, 7, 8, 9(5) and 12(3) of the second Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am also unable to accept the contention of the learned Attorney-General that the expression "charged on........ the Consolidated Fund of India" in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 merely means that the amount s payable as privy purse are not votable and that expression neither creates a right in favour of the person in whose benefit the charge is created nor is <ORG> pledged for the payment of the privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution does not define the word "charge".
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore we must understand that word as it is understood in law.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to law the creation of a charge over a fund in respect of an item of payment to a person means a conferment of a legal right on that person to get the amount in question on the pledge of the fund.
RatioOfTheDecision	If an item of expenditure charged on the consolidated fund merely means that that expenditure is non-votable then there was no need to provide in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	113 that "so much of the estimate as relates to expenditure charged upon <ORG> of India shall not be submitted to the vote of <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	That part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	178  113(1) was evidently enacted to make effective the statutory lien over <ORG> created in favour of the person to whom the payment ha s to be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	It emphasises the fact that the pledge created in favour of the person for whose benefit the charge is created by the Constitution cannot be taken away even by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Attorney-General and Mr. <NAME> read to us passages from May's <ORG>ary Practice and other treatises on <ORG>ary Practice and Procedure to show how the practice of charging certain items of expenditure on <ORG> came into being.
RatioOfTheDecision	They also invited our attention to some of the statutes passed by the British <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the treatises on which they relied nor any of the statutes to which they referred show that the charging of an item of expenditure on the <ORG> Fund in favour of a person does not create a legal right in him to get that amount or that the same does not pledge the <ORG> fund for the payment of that amount.
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact some of the Statutes referred to by them do show that some of the items of expenditure charged on the <ORG> f und were required to be paid in preference to the other items.
Argument	On the other hand Mr. <NAME> referred to us to the Dictionary of English law by <NAME> (1959 Ed) Vol.
Statute	1, page 459, wherein the meaning of the expression 'charged on the consolidated fund' is explained thus "Consolidated Fund, a repository of public money which now comprises the produce of custom, excise stamps and several other taxes, and some small receipts from the royal hereditary revenue, surrendered to the public use.
Statute	It constitutes almost the whole of the public income of the United Kingdom (Consolidated Fund Act, 1816).
Statute	This fund is pledged for the payment of the whole of the interest of the national debt of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (National Debt Act, 1870- s. 6 ); and besides this, is liable to several other specific charges imposed upo n it at various periods by Act of Parliament, such as the civil list, and the salaries of the judges and ambassadors and other high official persons; after payment of which the surplus i s to be indiscriminately applied to the service of the United Kingdom under the direction of <ORG>"   Section 6 of the National Debt Act, 1870 reads "6.
Statute	Stock Charged an consolidated fund.--The annuities and dividends aforesaid shall continue to be charged on and payable out of the consolidated fund."
RatioOfTheDecision	179  The language of this section is similar to that of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 so far as the creation of "charge' 'is concerned.
Statute	Section 6 of the National Debt Act, 1870 is according to <NAME> pledges the consolidated fund of Great Britain and Northern Ireland for the payment of the whole of the interest of the national debt of Great Britain and Northern Ireland.
Statute	If that is the true effect of s.6 of the National Debt Act , 1870 the same must be the position under Art.
Statute	291.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the passage quoted above from the Dictionary of English law by <NAME>, it is seen that as soon as an item of expenditure is charged on the consolidated fund, the said act creates a legal obligation to pay out of the consolidated fund th at item of expenditure to the person for whose benefit the charge is created.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly that item has to be paid before paying the non-charged item of expenditure.
RatioOfTheDecision	And lastly the charge created, pledges the consolidated fund for the payment of that item of expenditure.
RatioOfTheDecision	The practice of creating charges on the consolidated fund was started for the first time in this country under the Government of India Act, 1935 which Act was passed by the British <ORG> evidently following the British practice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	112 to 115 of the Constitution are similar to the corresponding Sections in the Government of India Act, 1935.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention of the learned Attorney General that the ex- pression "paid out of" in cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(a) of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 refers to the fund out of which it is to be paid out and not to the person to whom it is payable is also not correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 as it now stands, there is only one fund and that is the Consolidated fund of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore there is no question of pointing out the fund from out of which the p ayment is to be made.
RatioOfTheDecision	If some amount is required to be paid out of <ORG>, it must be paid out to somebody.
RatioOfTheDecision	There cannot be any paying out in abstract.
RatioOfTheDecision	To whom that payment is to be made is made clear by cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) of Art.
Precedent	291. it is to be paid to the Ruler as defined in Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	Even before Art.
Precedent	291(2) was deleted the privy purses were to be paid out of the Consolidated fund of India though some of the States had a liability to reimburse the Union to a certain extent.
Precedent	According to the learned Attorney-General on the date when Art.
Precedent	291 came into force, no Ruler had been recognised under Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	Therefore we cannot spell out any commitment under Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	We have earlier seen while discussing the scope of Art.
Precedent	366(22) that the President has a constitutional duty to recognise a Ruler.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	291 proceeds on the basis that President has to recognise a Ruler to each one of the Indian States contemplated by Art.
Precedent	366(15).
RatioOfTheDecision	By recognising the President merely locates the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	He does  not appoint or create a Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	No sooner the President recognizes the Ruler of an Indian State, he becomes entitled to the privy purse guaranteed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 from the date the Constitution came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are told that as a fact most of the <NAME> who entered into the Covenants and Agreements were recognised only in the year 1952 but yet they were being paid the amounts agreed to be paid as privy purses ever since the Constitution came into force and the privileges guaranteed to them were also extended to them even before they were recognised.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, we were told that in the case of successors of the <NAME> when there was no dispute as to succession, they were treated as <NAME> for all purposes though they were recognised several months after they succeeded to the <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This shows that the recognition under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) was considered as a mere formality except in the case of disputed succession.
RatioOfTheDecision	To my mind Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is plain and unambiguous.
RatioOfTheDecision	It says in the clearest possible language that the pri vy purses payable to the <NAME> under the Merger Agreements as well as under the Covenants are charged on the Consolidated fund of India and that they shall be paid out to the <NAME> exempt from all taxes on income.
RatioOfTheDecision	No provision of a statute much less a provision of a constitutional statute should be read in a pedantic way.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor is it justifiable to hair split the clauses in a provision and quibble about their words.
RatioOfTheDecision	A constitutional provision is not to be interpreted by taking words of the provisions in the one hand and the dictionary in the other or by taking the meaning given in a decision to a word in different setting.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each provision must be read as a whole and its meaning understood.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have earlier seen that under the Merger Agreements and Covenants, various rights, liabilities and obligations mere created.
RatioOfTheDecision	What <ORG> did was to separate two obligations out of them and give those obligations constitutional sanction or guarantee.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier, under the Convenants entered into by t he Rulers of Travancore and Cochin certain contribution was to be made every year to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This payment is guaranteed under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	290 (A).
RatioOfTheDecision	, Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 the payment of the privy purses is similarly guaranteed Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	290(A) and 291 are more or less similarly worded.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the mandate contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is an unenforceable mandate, similar would be the position so far as Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	290(A) is concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the mandates contained in these Articles are unenforceable these Articles can only have ornamental value.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is difficult to believe that <ORG> would have indulged in an exercise in futility.
RatioOfTheDecision	We repeatedly asked the learned Attorney General that if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 did not create a legal 181, right, what purpose that Article was intended to serve and why did <ORG> put that article, in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	His answer was that under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 while the payment of privy purse received a constitutional sanction, it received no, constitutional guarantee.
RatioOfTheDecision	This distinction to my min d appears, to be a distinction without difference.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every constitutional sanction for payment is necessarily a mandate to pay if that sanction relates to the discharge of an obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is an enforceable mandate.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier that a fair reading of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 shows that there is a direction to pay the privy purses to <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention of learned Attorney General was that by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 <ORG> merely wanted to give some sort of assurance to the <NAME> about the payment of privy purses to them in future so as, to allay their apprehensions that may not be paid privy purses in future but in reality, no legal right was created in favour of the <NAME> nor any binding obligation imposed on <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is difficult to understand this Argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be an uncharitable insinuation to make against the founding fathers that all that they wanted was to, create an illusion in the mind of the <NAME> while in reality giving them no guarantee as regards the future payment of the privy  purses.
RatioOfTheDecision	If all that <ORG> desired was to, give some assurance about the payment of privy purses in the future then Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 would have served that purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a general sense the words "personal rights" include privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if <ORG> wanted to make things clear they could have easily said in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 " personal rights including privy purse" instead of wasting a whole article.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further there was nor purpose in charging the privy purses on the <ORG> fund or giving a constitutional exemption from payment of all taxes on income in respect of privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	No word in the Constitution can be considered as superfluous.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the hearing some the members of the <ORG> felt that it may not be necessary to go into the scope and effect of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	191 in the present proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was felt that if the <ORG> came to the conclusion that the impugned orders are valid orders then there is an end of the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	If on the other hand, the <ORG> came to the conclusion that those orde rs are violative of the Constitution then status quo ante would be restored.
RatioOfTheDecision	But both the learned Attorney General and Mr. <NAME> insisted that we should pronounce on the scope and effect of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 , each one for his own reason.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Attorney General repeatedly made it plain to us that even if we come to the, conclusion that the impugned orders are invalid, the privy purses will not be paid by the <ORG>, unless we hold that the right given to the Rulers under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is an enforceable one.
RatioOfTheDecision	This, is a strange stand particularly in view of the fact that even according to him the Constitution has recognised the liability to pay the privy purses to the Rulers and the obligation in question has received constitutional sanction.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is clear from the stand taken by him that the <ORG> will not respect the mandate of the Constitution if that mandate is not enforceable by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have to proceed on the basis that the learned Attorney General made that submission on the strength of the instructi ons received by him from the respondent.
RatioOfTheDecision	But yet, it is difficult to believe that the executive which is a creture of the Constitution, whose head (the President) and the members of the cabinet had taken the oath of allegiance to the Constitution would take the stand that they will not respect a mandate of the Constitution unless that mandate is enforceable in a court of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The enforceability of a constitutional mandate is one thing, the existence of such a mandate is another.
Precedent	Whether a particular constitutional mandate is enforceable or not, it is all the same binding on all the organs of the <ORG>.
Precedent	No organ of the <ORG> can choose to disregard any of the mandates of the Constitution.
Precedent	There are many mandates in the Constitution which are not enforceable through courts of law.
Precedent	If the executive or the legislature or the judiciary refuse to comply with those mandates they will be not only breaking the oath taken by them but they will be breaking the Constitution itself.
Precedent	I doubt whether the grave implications  of the stand taken on behalf of the Government have been realised.
Precedent	I shall now proceed to Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	That Article reads "In the exercise of the power of <ORG> or of the legislature of a <ORG> to make laws or in the exercise of the executive power of the <ORG> or of a <ORG>, due regard shall be had to the guarantee or assurance given under any such covenant or agreement as is referred to in article 291 with respect to the personal rights, privileges and dignities of the Ruler of an Indian <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	This article clearly links itself with the Agreements and Covnants.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has no independent exercise apart from the Agreement and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> conceded that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 is a provision of the Constitution relating to the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, it follows that if any dispute arises in respect of anyright accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of Art.
Precedent	362 then the same would be covered by the second .part of Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	But Mr. <NAME> sought to place his own interpretation  on the word "dispute" found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363. it is not necessary for us in this case to decide what controversy relating to Art.
Precedent	362 can be considered as a "dispute" under Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	At present we have no concrete complaint before us relating to the contravention of Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	It is not proper to decide the scope of an article in the Constitution in abstract.
Precedent	The scope of Art.
Precedent	362 as well as the meaning of the expression "dispute" in Art.
Precedent	363 can be best considered when a proper case comes up for decision.
Precedent	In this view, I have not thought it necessary to go into the scope of Art., 362.
Precedent	This takes me to Art.
Precedent	363(1).
Precedent	That Article reads "Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution but subject to the provisions of article 143 , neither <ORG> nor any other court shall have jurisdiction in any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument which was entered into or executed before the commencement of this Constitution by any Ruler of an  Indian State and to which <ORG> or any of its predecessor Governments was a party and which has or has been continued in operation after such commencement, or in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of this Constitution relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument."
Precedent	Under cl.
Precedent	(2) of that Article "Indian State" is defined for the purpose of that article as meaning any territory recognised before the commencement of the Constitution by His Majesty or <ORG> as being such a State, and the "Ruler" for the purpose of that article is defined thus " "Ruler" includes the Prince, Chief or other person recognised before such commencement by His Majesty or <ORG> as the Ruler of any Indian State."
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	363 has two parts : the first part deals with disputes arising out of any provisions of  a treaty, agreement or covenant etc., and the second part with dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of the Constitution, relating to any such treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument.
Precedent	184  Dealing with Art.
Precedent	362 and 363 this is what the White Paper says in paragraph 240 (at p. 125) "Guarantees regarding rights and privileges.- Guarantees have been given to the Rulers under the various Agreements and Covenants for the continuation of their rights, dignities and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rights enjoyed by the Rulers vary from State to State and are exercisable both within and without the States.
RatioOfTheDecision	They cover a variety of matters ranging from the use of the red plates on cars to immunity from Civil and Criminal jurisdiction and exemptions from customs duties etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in the past it was neither considered desirable nor practicable to draw up an exhaustive list of all these rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	During the negotiations following  introduction of the scheme embodied in the Government ,of India Act , 1935.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> had taken the position that no more could be done in respect of the rights and privileges enjoyed by the <NAME> than a general assurance of the intention of <ORG> to continue them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Obviously, it would have been a source of perpetual regret if all these matters had been made as justiciable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 363 has, there- fore been embodied in the Constitution which excludes specifically the Agreements of Merger and the Covenants from the jurisdiction of Courts except in cases which may be refered to <ORG> by the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, <ORG> considered it necessary that constitutional recognition should be given to the guarantees and assurances which <ORG> have given in respect of the rights and privileges of <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 , which provides that in the exercise of their legislative and executive authority, the legislative and e xecutive organs of the Union and States will have due regard to the guarantees given to the <NAME> with respect to their personal rights, privileges and dignities."
RatioOfTheDecision	From the above passage, it is clear that according to the Government's understanding of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , that article merely deals with matters coming under Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	That is also the contention of the petitioners.
Precedent	But according to the learned Attorney 'General that article excludes from the jurisdiction of all courts including this <ORG> not merely those matters that fall within the 'scope of Art.
Precedent	362 but also the right arising from Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	It was urged by him that Art.
Precedent	291 also protects only.
Precedent	a personal right.
Precedent	Therefore it is a matter that falls within the scope of Art.
Precedent	362 , Consequently any dispute relating thereto is excluded from the jurisdiction of this <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Privy purse was taken out for special treatment by the Constitution under Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	Therefore it is excluded from the general provision in Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 and 362 have  to be construed harmoniously.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a well known rule of construction that a special provision excludes the general provision.
Precedent	Hence I have to reject the contention that Art.
Precedent	363 includes the right to get privy purses because it also comes within the scope of Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	If it is otherwise, there was no need to enact Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	Further there was no purpose in guaranteeing the payment of privy purses under Art.
Precedent	291 and then taking away the right to recover them under Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have earlier seen that in the case of most of the <NAME>, the right to receive privy purse was an enforceable right even before Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 came into force.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is not easy to accept the contention that what was an enforceable right was made unenforceable under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the contention advanced on behalf of the respondent is correct the, purpose of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 was to take away an existing enforceable right, at any rate in the case of several <NAME> and substitute the same by a recognition, devoid of all legal contents.
Precedent	To say that i s to be cynical about the august body i.e.
Precedent	<ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> could not have enacted Art 291 to show its contempt for the Rulers of Indian States as well as for the recommendation of <ORG> headed by <NAME>, the maker of modern India.
RatioOfTheDecision	If two or more provisions in the Constitution deal with one group of topics, those provisions have to be read together and interpretted harmoniously.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not proper to say that the Constitution is speaking in two voices, as the learned Attorney General wants us to do or that it takes away by the right hand what is gave by the left hand.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore we have to read Art.
Precedent	363 harmoniously with Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	That is equal- ly true of Arts.
Precedent	363 and 366(22).
Precedent	The rule of harmonious construction is a well known rule.
Precedent	If the aforementioned articles are harmoniously interpretted then the position becomes clear.
Precedent	The purpose of Art.
Precedent	363 is made clear in the White Paper.
Precedent	Under the Merger Agreements as well as under the Covenants, various rights were  conferred and privileges assured to the <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of the agreements entered into between the former <NAME> and His Majesty's Government or the Dominion of India are undoubtedly acts of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the Covenants ,are concerned, the question whether they were acts of <ORG> or constitutional documents is a highly debatable question.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rights accruing as well as liabilities and obligations arising under acts of <ORG> were not enforceable in the municipal courts unless they were recognised by the new sovereign.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the purpose of giving necessary direction to the <ORG> and <ORG> executives as well as to the <ORG> and <ORG> legislatures, the Constitution recognised the rights accruing and liabilities and obligations arising under various Agreements and Covenants which recognition made.
RatioOfTheDecision	those rights, liabilitie and obligations enforceable.
RatioOfTheDecision	But <ORG> did not want to open up the Pandora's box.
Precedent	Without Art.
Precedent	363 ,Art.
Precedent	362 would have opened the flood gates of litigation.
Precedent	<ORG>  evidently wanted to avoid that situation.
Precedent	That appears to have been the main reason for enacting Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Evidently there were other reasons also for enacting Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Some of the Rulers who had entered into Merger Agreements were challenging the validity of those agreements, even before the draft of the Constitution was finalised.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of them were contending that the agreements were taken from them by intimidation; some others were contending that there were blanks in the agreements signed by them and those blanks had been filled in without their knowledge and to their prejudice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The merger process went on hurriedly.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution makers could not have ignored the possibility of future challenge to the validity of the Merger Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	Naturally they would have been anxious to avoid challenge to various provisions in the Constitution which are directly linked with the Merger Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	As seen earlier Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 has two parts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first part relates to disputes arising out of Agreements and Covenants  etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The jurisdiction of this <ORG> as well as of other courts is clearly barred in respect of disputes falling within that part.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then comes the second part of Art.
Precedent	363 which refers to disputes in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of the Constitution relating to any agreement, covenant etc.
Precedent	We are concerned with this part of Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before a dispute can be held to come within the scope of that part, that dispute must be in respect of a right accruing under or liability or obligation arising out of a Provision of the Constitution and that provision of the Constitution must relate to agreements, Covenants etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The principal dispute with which we are concerned in these cases is whether the President has the power to abolish all Rulers under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	Quite plainly this dispute cannot be held to be dispute in respect of a right accruing or a liability or obligation arising under any provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Herein we are not concer ned with any right, liability or obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are concerned with powers of the President under Art.
Precedent	3 66 (22).
Precedent	What is in dispute is the true scope of the power of the President under Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	That dispute does not fall within Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Power is not the same thing as right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Power is an authority whereas a right in the context in which it is used in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , signifies property.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact that the court's decision about the scope of the power of the President under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) may incidentally bear on certain rights does not make the dispute, a dispute relating to any right accruing under any provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	A dispute as regards the interpretation of a provision of the Constitution is not a dispute within the contemplation of the second part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 as it is not a dispute in respect of any right, liability or obligation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention of the petitioners is that the impugned orders are ultra vires the powers of the President, hence null and void.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a dispute does not come within  Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	It cannot be said that Art.
Precedent	366(22) is a provision relating to Merger Agreements and Covenants.
Precedent	The word 'relating to' is a word of wide import but in the context in which it is used in Art.
Precedent	363 it must receive a narrower meaning otherwise all rights accruing or liabilities and obligations arising under one of other of the provisions of the Constitution to the former Rulers of Indian States as well as to their subjects has to be held to come within the mischief of Art.
Precedent	363 because they became Indian citizens as a result of the merger of the Indian States in the Dominion of India in pursuance of Merger Agreements.
Precedent	Nothing so startling could have been intended by <ORG>.
Precedent	If it is otherwise, the life, liberty and property of that section of our citizens would be under the mercy of our <ORG> because if they complain against any high handedness on the part of the <ORG>, the <ORG> can seek shelter under Art.
Precedent	36.
Precedent	The word 'relating' in Art.
Precedent	363 , in my judgment means "to br ing into relation" or "establish relation between".
Precedent	In other words the provision of the Constitution in question must be linked with the Merger Agreements or Covenants directly and immediately.
Precedent	It must have no independent existence.
Precedent	That is not the position under Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	It is an independent provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has nothing to do with the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not take any strength from the Covenants and Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to recognise the Rulers is a new power conferred on the President by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was no such power under the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Between 1947 and 25th of January, 1950 there was no question of recognising the Rulers of Indian States.
RatioOfTheDecision	In respect of several of the Indian States, the Dominion of India had no right to decide the question of successorship.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provision in the Merger Agreements that succession will be according to law and custom is merely a statement of the legal position.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same cannot be considered as a part of the 13-L744 Sup CI/71 Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reference to Agreements and Covenants through Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 is a convenient drafting device.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if all the Agreements and the Covenants are abrogated the provision will stand intact.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME>, appearing on behalf of the respondent contended that Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	291, 362 and 363 should be considered as one group of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	which group together relates to Agreements and Covenants; Art.
Precedent	3 66 (22) was enacted to effectuate Arts.
Precedent	291 and 362; Articles 291 and 362 are related to Agreements and Covenants; therefore Art.
Precedent	366(22) must also be held to be related to Agreements and Covenants.
Precedent	I have earlier considered the meaning of the word 'relating' in Art.
Precedent	363.
Precedent	Further I have held that Art.
Precedent	291 is not related to Art.
Precedent	363 as it not linked with the Agreements and Covenants; it is an independent provision.
Precedent	I have also held that the definition of "Ruler" in Art.
Precedent	366(22) is not merely for the purpose of Art.
Precedent	291 and Art.
Precedent	362 but also for the purpose of supplying contents for the legislative ,entry 34 of of List I o f Sch.
Precedent	VII of the Constitution.
Precedent	Hence the group relation theory ingenuously advanced by Mr. <NAME> cannot be accepted.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	363 speaks of "any provision of the Constitution relating" to Agreement and Covenants.
Precedent	If the contention of Mr. <NAME> is analysed, it means that at Art.
Precedent	366(22) is related to the Agreements and Covenants through Art.
Precedent	2912 and 362.
Precedent	In other words that Art.
Precedent	is a relation of the relations of the Agreements and Covenants.
Precedent	That is the type or relationship contemplated by Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	That article contemplates direct relationship between the concerned articles and the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	The further contention of Mr. <NAME> that in finding out whether an article is related to Agreements and Covenants, we should look to its origin or genesis, is not correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is otherwise it must be held that all the articles of the Constitution in so far as hey deal with the former Rulers of Indian States and their subjects are con- cerned are related to Agree ments and Covenants as they had their origin or genesis in the Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that is so Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 becomes all pervasive.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have earlier noticed the far reaching implications of such conclusion.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioners contend that the plea of the respondent that Art.
