RatioOfTheDecision	291 does not confer a legal right on the Rulers to get privy purses cannot be considered as raising a genuine dispute and that contention is a mere manoeuvre to oust the jurisdiction of this <ORG> and hence the same cannot be considered as dispute within Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the petitioner the said plea of the respondent is a mere pretence and not a dispute because dispute in law means a triable issue and not an assertion which is ex-  189  facie untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary to examine these contentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The basic issue arising for decision in these cases is of far greater significance than it appears at first sight.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question whether the Rulers can be derecognised by the President is of secondary importance.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is of utmost impo rtance for the future of our democracy is whether the executive in this country can flout the mandates of the Constitution and set at night legislative enactments at its discretion.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it is held that it can then our hitherto held assumption that in this country we are ruled by laws and not by men must be given up as erroneous.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before, proceeding to consider the decisions relied on by the learned Attorney General and Mr. <NAME> in support of their contention that the disputes with which we are concerned in these cases are disputes falling within the ambit of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 , it is necessary to mention at the very outset that the question whether the orders similar to the impugned orders are within the powers of the President under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	366(22) did never come before these <ORG> for decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	No such orders had been passed by the President in the past.
Precedent	There was just one derecognition in the past i.e.
Precedent	that of the former Ruler of Baroda.
Precedent	That matter did, not come before courts.
Precedent	Hence there was no occasion for  this <ORG> or for that matter any court in this country to consider the scope of Art.
Precedent	366(22).
Precedent	The observations made by this <ORG> in <NAME> case (supra) had been considered by me earlier.
Precedent	Even the scope of Art.
Precedent	291 had not directly arisen for consideration in any of the decisions of this <ORG>.
Precedent	It is true that there are a few observations in some of the decisions to which I shall presently refer about the nature of the right guaranteed under that Art.
Precedent	291 and the impact of Art.
Precedent	363 on that right.
Precedent	Let me now consider the decisions relied on by the learned Attorney General.
Precedent	The first decision relied on by him is <CITATION_440324> and anr.
Precedent	etc.(1).
Precedent	Therein certain States which had acceded to the Dominion of India and which had merged in the Province of Bihar and administered as part of that Province instituted suits in the Federal <ORG> of India 'before the 26th January 1950 for a declaration that various orders under which States came to be administered as part of Bihar and the laws under which thos e orders were made were ultra vires and void and the Province of Bihar had accordingly no authority to carry on the administration of the States.
Precedent	Those suits stood transferred to <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	374(2) of the Constitution after the Constitution came ino force.
Precedent	(1)  S.C.R.474 In those suits the principal question that fell for decision was whether the dispute as regards the validity of the merger could be gone into by this Court in view of Art.
Precedent	363 of the Constitution.
Precedent	This Court held that as the suits were really to enforce the plaintiffs' right under the Instruments of accession and the dispute between the parties really arose out of those instruments, in view of Art.
Precedent	363(1) is court had no jurisdiction to hear the suits.
Precedent	The principal controversy in that case came squarely, within the ambit of the first part of Art.
Precedent	363(1).
Precedent	Hence that decision is not relevant for our present purpose.
Precedent	The next case referred to is <CITATION_45759> (1).
Argument	Therein the dispute was about the validity ,of some  of the Provisions of the Madhya Pradesh Abolition of Proprietory Rights (Estates Mahals, Alienated Lands) Act .
Argument	One of the contentions advanced on behalf of the petitioner in that case was that by the terms of the Merger Agreement, the properties concerned in that case were declared as the, petitioner's private properties and were protected from <ORG> legislation by the guarantee given under Art.
Argument	363 of the Constitution and hence the impugned Act was bad as that contravenes the provisions of that Art.
Argument	The Court rejected that contention with these observations :  "It is true that by the covenant of merger the properties of the petitioner became his private properties as distinguished from properties of the <ORG> but in respect of them he is in no better position than any other owner possessing private property.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 362 does not prohibit the acquisition of properties declared as private properties by the covenant of merger and does not guarantee their perpetual existence.
RatioOfTheDecision	The guarantee contained in the art icle is of a limited extent only.
RatioOfTheDecision	It assures that the, <ORG> properties declared as their private properties will not be claimed as <ORG> properties.
RatioOfTheDecision	The guarantee has no greater scope than this.
RatioOfTheDecision	That guarantee has been fully respected by the impugned statute, as it treats those properties as their private pro- perties and seeks to acquire them on that assumption.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover it seems to me that in view of the comprehensive language of article 363 this issue is not justiciable."
RatioOfTheDecision	From this it is clear that the decision in question does not bear on the points in controversy in these cases.
Precedent	The learned Attorney-General next relied on the decision in <CITATION_1414177> and (1)  S.C.R.
Precedent	1020.
Precedent	191  Anr.(1).
Precedent	Therein a former Ruler of an Indian <ORG> challenged the levy of agricultural income-tax on his agricultural properties under the Orissa Agricultural Income-Tax Act, 1947 (Orissa Act 24 of 1947).
Argument	He contended that in view of the guarantees given to him under cls.
Argument	(4) and (5) of the merger agreement entered into b etween him and the Dominion of India, no agricultural income-tax can be levied on the income from his private agricultural properties.
Argument	That contention was repelled by this Court holding that the privileges granted under cls.
Argument	(4) and (5) of the Agreements of Merger were his personal privileges as an ex-Ruler and those privileges did not extend to his private properties and that the claim made by him of immunity from taxation relying upon the Agreement of Merger was not justiciable.
Argument	The ratio of that decision is of no assistance in these cases.
Argument	But the learned Attorney-General relied on the observations found at pp.
Argument	785 and 786 of the Report.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those observations are :  "Even though Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	362 is not restricted in its recommendation to agreements relating to the privy purse and covers all agreements and covenants entered into by the Rulers of Indian States before the commencement of the Constitution whereby the personal rights, privileges and dignities of the Ruler of an Indian State were guaranteed, it does not im port any legal obligation enforceable at the instance of the erstwhile Ruler of a former Indian State.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, despite the recommendation that due regard shall be had to the guarantee or assurance given under the covenant or agreement, the <ORG> or the <ORG> of a State makes laws inconsistent with the personal rights, privileges and dignities of the Ruler of an Indian State the exercise of the legislative authority cannot, relying upon the agreement or covenant, be questioned in any court and that is so expressly provided by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	363 of the Constitution."
RatioOfTheDecision	The only remark in the above observation relevant for the purpose of the present cases is : "Even though Art.
Precedent	362 is not restricted in its recommendation to agreements relating to the privy purse" thereby meaning that guarantee as regards the privy purse also comes within the scope of Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	This is a casual remark.
Precedent	In that case the <ORG> had no occasion to consider the scope of Art.
Precedent	291 or Art.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	The decision of this <ORG> in (' <CITATION_195 3650> ., referred to by the learned Attorney-General during the (1)  1, S.C.R.
Precedent	779 (1)  5 S.C.R.
Precedent	1.

Precedent	<NAME>, J. speaking for the <ORG> merely set out the arguments of the parties as to the scope of Arts.
Precedent	291, 362 and 363 but declined to go into them as those Arts had not been relied on in the High <ORG>.
Precedent	The next decision relied on by the learned Attorney-General is the decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_19878> (1).
Precedent	The material facts of that case were that the Ruler of the, State of Sant had issued a Tharao dated 12th March, 1948 granting full right and authority to the jagirdars over the forest in their respective villages.
Precedent	Pursuant to the agreement dated March 19, 1948 the.
Precedent	State of Sant merged with the Dominion of India.
Precedent	At the time of the merger, it was expressly agreed that no order passed or action taken by the Maharana before the day of April 1, 1948 would be questioned but after the merger the Government  of Bombay in which province the former State of Sant had merged in consultation with <ORG> cancelled the <ORG> in question holding that it was not a bona fide grant.

Precedent	363.
Precedent	The last case relied on by the learned Attorney-General is <NAME> v. <NAME> (2 ).
Precedent	In that case a creditor of a former Ruler sought to attach the privy purse payable to the Ruler under Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	The Ruler objected to the same on the ground that attachment is invalid in view of cl.
Precedent	(g) to the Proviso of s.60(1), C.P.C, which provision says that political pensions are not liable to be attached.
Precedent	The word "pension" in s.60(1) (g) implies periodical payment of money by the <ORG> to the pensioners-see <NAME> of Murshidabad v. <NAME>  <ORG> (3).
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME>), Lord <NAME> observed "A pension which the Government of India has given a guarantee that it will pay, be a treaty obligation contracted with another sovereign power, appears to their Lordships to be, in the strictest sense a political pension.
Precedent	The obligation to pay as well as the actual payment of the pension, must in such circumstances, be ascribed to reasons of State Policy.
Precedent	(1) [1964] 6, S.C.R.
Precedent	461 (2) [1965] 3, S.C.R.
Precedent	201.
Precedent	(3) [58] 1.
Precedent	A.
Precedent	215;  (4) [1890] L.A. XVII 18.
Precedent	193  Relying on these decisions and taking into consideration the nature of the liability in relation to the payment 'of privy purse, this <ORG> held that Privy Purse is a political pension and as such, the same is not liable to be attached.
RatioOfTheDecision	This, in short is the ratio of the decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the decision had said nothing more it would not have advanced the case of the respondent.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in the course of the judgment <NAME> who spoke for the <ORG> after summarising Art s. 291, 362 and 363 observed as follows "On the coming into force of the Constitution of India the guarantee for the payment of periodical sums, as privy purse is continued by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	291 of the Constitution but its essential political character is preserved by Art.
Precedent	363 of the Constitution, and the obligation under this guarantee cannot be enforced in any municipal court.
Precedent	Moreover, if the President refuses to recognise the person by whom the covenant was entered into as the Ruler of the State, he would not be entitled to the amount payable as privy purse under Art.
Precedent	291.
Precedent	Now, the.
Precedent	periodical payment of money by the Government ,to a Ruler of a former Indian State as privy purse on political considerations and under political sanctions and not under a right legally enforceable in any municipal court is strictly a political pension within the meaning of s.60(1) (g) of the Code of Civil Procedure."
Precedent	But these observations are obiter.
Precedent	The learned judges in that case had no occasion to consider nor did they go into the  scope of Art.
Precedent	291 or Art.
Precedent	363.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every observation of this <ORG> is no doubt, entitled to weight but an obiter, cannot take the place of the ratio.
RatioOfTheDecision	Judges are not oracles.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the very nature of things, it is not possible to give the same attention to incidental matters as is given to the actual issues arising for decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	Fur- ther much depends on the way the case is presented to them.
Precedent	<CITATION_1656601> .
Precedent	(1) dealing with the question as to the importance to be attached to the observations found in the judgments of this <ORG>.
Precedent	this is what this <ORG> observed "A decision is only an authority for what it actually decides.
Precedent	What is of the essence in a decision is its ratio and not every observation found therein nor what logically follows from the various observations made in it.
Precedent	On this topic this is what <NAME> of <ORG> said in <NAME> v. <NAME> (1901) A.C 495 :  "Now before discussing the case of <NAME> v. <NAME> 1898) A:C.1 and what was decided therein, there are (1) [1968] 2, S.C.R.
Precedent	154.
Precedent	194  two observ ations of a general character which I wish to make, and one is to repeat what I have very often said before; that every judgment must be read as applicable to the particular facts proved or assumed to be proved, since the generality of the expressions which may be found there are not intended to be expositions of the whole law, but governed and qualified by the particular facts of the case in which such expressions are to be found.
Precedent	The other is that a case is only an authority for what it actually decides.
Precedent	I entirely deny that it can be quoted for a proposition that may seem to follow logically from it.
Precedent	Such a mode of reasoning assumes that the law is necessarily a logical code, whereas every lawyer must acknowledge that the law is not always logical at all."
Precedent	It is not a protable task to extract a sentence here and there from a judgment and to build upon it.
Precedent	In my opinion none of the questions of law arising for deci- sion excepting that relating to the petitioners' right to move this Court under Art.
Precedent	32 is  res Integra.
Precedent	The only question remaining for consideration is whether the petitioners have been able to establish any construction of their fundamental rights in order to entitle them to move this Court under Art.
Precedent	32.
Precedent	This question need not detain us for long.
Precedent	The petitioners have complained that the rights under Arts.
Precedent	14, 19, 21 and 31 have been contravened.
Precedent	As I am satisfied that the rights under Arts.
Precedent	31 and 19 (1) (f) have been contravened it is not necessary to examine the alleged contravention of other rights.
Precedent	I have earlier come to the conclusion that the right to get the privy purse under Art.
Precedent	291 is a legal right.
Precedent	From that it follows that it is a right enforceable through the courts of law.
Precedent	Thai right is undoubtedly a property.
Precedent	A right to receive cash grants annually has been considered by this Court to be a property- <CITATION_1963913> (1).
RulingByLowerCourt	Even if it is considered as a pension as the same is payable under law namely Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	291 , the same is property- <CITATION_1261287> (2).
RulingByLowerCourt	We have also earlier se en that certain benefits have been conferred on the <NAME> under the Wealth Tax Act .
RulingByLowerCourt	As a result of the impugned orders, all those benefits are purported to have (1)  3, S.C.R.
Fact	489 (2) (1961) 1, S.C.R.
Precedent	957 been taken away.
RatioOfTheDecision	The denial of those benefits which had been afforded to the <NAME> under law is again a contravention of the petitioners' fundamental right to property.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was conceded by the learned Attorney General that an illegal deprivation of any pecuniary benefit to which a person is entitled under any law is.
RulingByPresentCourt	a deprivation of his fundamental right.
RulingByPresentCourt	In view of this concession it is not necessary to refer to decided cases.
RulingByPresentCourt	For the reasons mentioned above, I allow these petitions with costs, quash the impugned orders which means that the status quo ante is restored.
RulingByPresentCourt	The declaration asked for in relief No.
RulingByPresentCourt	2 is unnecessary.
Precedent	There is no need at present to go into the, other reliefs asked for.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Fact	These are eight petitions.
Fact	The petitioners are des- cribed as <NAME> of Gwalior, Udaipur, Nabha, Nalag arh, Kutch, Dhrangadhra, Patna and Benaras.
Fact	On 6 September, 1970 in exercise of the powers vested in the President under Article 366(22) of the Constitution, the President directed that with effect from the date of the said order His Highness Maharajdhiraja <NAME> do cease to be recognised as a Ruler of Gwalior.
Fact	Similar orders were made by the President in regard to the other seven petitioners.
Fact	All the petitions are in substance the same.
Precedent	It will not, there fore, be necessary to refer to all the petitions separately.
Precedent	The case of the petitioner in Writ Petition No.
Precedent	376 of 1970 can be arbitrary, malafide and a fraud on the Constitution.
Argument	The petitioner challenges the aforementioned order (herein- after referred to as the order) as violative of Articles 14, 19(1) (f) and 31(1) and (2) of the Constitution.
Argument	The order is also challenged to be unconstitutional,.
RatioOfTheDecision	ultra vires, void, inoperative,, arbitrary, malafide and a fraud on the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The grounds for challenge alleged i n the petition are these First, the privy purses have been guaranteed under Merger Agreements and Covenants.
RatioOfTheDecision	Merger Agreements and Covenants are inextricably linked up with Instrument of Accession.
RatioOfTheDecision	There pledge to pay privy purses and the guarantee regarding privileges are inseparable from accession and merger.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obligation to, pay privy purse and the guarantee regarding privileges cannot be abolished by an executive order.
RatioOfTheDecision	The whole purpose of the order is to deprive the petitioner of privy purse and privileges, guaranteed under the Covenants and Merger Agreements and also guaranteed and asured by Articles 291 and 362 of the Constitution.
RulingByPresentCourt	The whole object of the order is to override and overrule the Constitution on the point of Rulers rights, privileges and privy purses after the rejection of the Constitution (24th Amendment) Bill by <ORG>.
RulingByPresentCourt	Secondly, derecognition of all the Rulers en masse is itself .the clearest possible proof that the whole object is to abolish the institution of Rulership al together and the rights and privileges .attached thereto including the right to privy purse.
RulingByPresentCourt	Under-the Merger Agrements and Covenants a Ruler is entitled to privy purse, rights and privileges enjoyed before 15 August, 1947 and succession to the gaddi in accordance with the law and custom of the family.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> in discharge of the obligation to ensure the fulfilment of these rights has been recognising successors to Rulers and paying privy purses to the Rulers and to their successors.
RatioOfTheDecision	'The procedure of recognition of the persons, so entitled by the President for the purpose of Articles 291 and 362 has to be read with the contractual obligation which still survived between <ORG> and the Ruler.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once the President has recognised a person who is entitled to receive privy purse and to be accorded rights and privileges as a Ruler, there can be no interference with the right to receive privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, there is no substantive provision in the Constitution .conferring on the President  a right to recognise or not to recognise a Ruler or to withdraw recognition.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once the procedure of recognition has been exhausted the President becomes functus officio and has no further authority to withdraw the recognition which he has accorded.
RatioOfTheDecision	In recognising a Ruler the President has to ,conform to the fact of a certain person being Ruler or to the fact of succession in accordance with the position under the Covenants and Merger Agreements and in accordance with law and custom of the family.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 366(22) imposes a constitutional duty on the President to recognise an existing fact in accordance with the provisions of the Covenants and Merger Agreements and the President has no power or authority independent of such facts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The President is bound by contractual obligations in the Covenants and Merger Agreements and by the Constitutional duty imposed upon him to recognise a person entitled to receive privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	The order derecognising Ruler en masse brings the institution of Rulership to an end.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ord er is in contravention of Articles 291, 362, 366(22) and 53(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Fourthly, the order violates Article 14 , because it singles out the <NAME> for hostile discrimination and deprives them of their valuable rights to property without compensation and violates solemn agreements and the express provisions of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	'There is deliberate defiance of the Constitution by wilful repudiation of contractual obligations against a class of citizens.
RulingByLowerCourt	197  Fifthly, the right to receive privy purse and other rights constitutes property within Articles 19 ( 1 ) (f ) and 31 and the order seeks to deprive the petitioner of his right to privy purse and other rights in violation of Article 19(1) (f).
RulingByLowerCourt	The right to taxfree privy purse and other rights are properties of the petitioner and the petitioner is deprived of the same without authority of law in violation of Article 31(1).
RulingByLowerCourt	The privy purse is in substance and in reality compensation for the transfer by <NAME> of inter alia their properties and it is not competent t o the Government to abolish the right without compensation in the form of privy purse.
RulingByLowerCourt	Sixthly, the <ORG>, it is alleged, acted on the faith of the undertakings and guarantee given by <ORG> regarding privy purses and preservation of <ORG>hip and of personal rights and privileges.
RulingByLowerCourt	The <ORG> acted to their detriment by giving away vast properties.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Government is, therefore, estopped by the doctrine of promissory estoppel from refusing to pay the privy purse.
RatioOfTheDecision	A fiduciary duty is cast on <ORG> to respect and implement the provisions of the Merger Agreements and the Covenants: The Government is bound by its pledged words to pay privy purse and to recognise <ORG>hip.
RatioOfTheDecision	Alternatively, the order leaves the Merger Agreements and Covenants untouched and the <ORG> is bound to pay privy purse and to recognise the personal rights and privileges and to discharge all obligations under the Covenants and Merger Agreements and the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finally, the petitioner alleged that Art icle 363 does not cover the case of a policy to abolish the institution of Rulership and rights and privileges and privy purses of Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	The questions whether en masse derecognition of Rulers is ultra vires Article and whether the Government by executive action can abolish the institution of Rulership and wipe out Articles 291 and 362 by policy decisions are said to be outside Article 362.
RatioOfTheDecision	On these allegations in the petition the petitioner seeks three declarations; First that the order is ultra vires, secondly, that the petitioner continues to be a Ruler and continues to be entitled to privy purse and privileges, and thirdly, a. writ 'under Article 32 directing the Government to pay privy purse, recognise Rulership and pay compensation.
Argument	The respondent denies that the petitioner is legally entitled to privy purse and privileges or that the Government is bound to pay privy purse and accord the privileges by reason of the Covenants or Merger Agreements.
Argument	The Government denies that the petitioner is entitled  to privy purse or to privileges or that the Government is bound to pay privy purse or accord privileges under Arti-  198  cles 291 and 362 respectively.
Argument	The Government denies that the alleged obligation to pay privy purse or the alleged guarantee regarding privileges cannot be abolished by executive order.
Argument	The Government denies that independently of Article 366(22) the petitioner is entitled to privy purse or to privileges.
Argument	The Government denies that the President is bound by contractual obligations or constitutional duty to recognise a person to be entitled to privy purse.
Argument	The Government denies that the Government has no right to refuse to pay privy purse or to derecognise Rulers.
Argument	The Government denies that the order violates Articles 19 and 31 or that the petitioner has been deprived of privy purse or privileges because of the grounds alleged.
Argument	The Government denies that Article 366(22) imposes any duty on the President to recognise any existing fact in accordance with the Covenants or that any existing Rul er is an existing fact for recognition.
Argument	The Government denies that the order is ultra vires or there is any institution of <ORG>.
Argument	Finally, the Government denies that derecognition is outside Article 363 or that questions of abolition of <ORG> or wiping out Articles 291 and 362 are outside Article 363.
Argument	The Attorney General raised the plea of the bar of jurisdiction of this Court under Article 363 at the threshold.
Statute	Article 363 is as follows :  "363.
Statute	(1) Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution but subject to the provisions of Article 143 , neither <ORG> nor any other court shall have jurisdiction in any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument which was entered into or executed before the commencement of this Constitution by any Ruler of an Indian State and to which <ORG> or any of its predessor Governments was a party and which has or has been continued in operation after suc h commencement or in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any of the provisions of this Constitution relating to any such treaty,, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad or other similar instrument.
Statute	(2) In this Article-  (a) "Indian State means any territory recognised before the commencement of this Constitution by His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as being such a State; and   (b) "Ruler" includes the Prince, Chief or other person recognised before such commencement by His Majesty or the Government of the Dominion of India as the Ruler of any Indian State".
Statute	The first bar is in any dispute arising out of any provision of a treaty, agreement, covenant entered into before the commencement of the Constitution and which has continued in operation after such commencement.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second bar is in any dispute in respect of any right accruing under or any liability or obligation arising out of any provision of the Constitution relating to  any treaty, agreement, covenant, engagement, sanad and other similar instruments.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, vitally necessary to ascertain first whether there are disputes; secondly, as to what those disputes are; and, thirdly, whether the disputes fall within Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason why I referred to the rival allegations is to indicate the nature and character of disputes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the petitioner contended that there was no dispute as to privy purse or to recognition of a Ruler and the only contention was that the order of the President was a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is indisputable that no one comes to a court of law unless disputes have arisen.
Precedent	When the petitioner alleges that the order is a nullity and the Government alleges that the order is valid a dispute arises at once.
Precedent	Mr. <NAME> contended that the first limb of Article 363 was clearly not applicable because there is no dispute arising out of any Covenant or Merger Agreement and the bar under the second limb was not attracted for four reasons.
Precedent	First, rights, liabilities and obligations are not to be confused with powers or jurisdiction or limits of legislative or executive powers or jurisdiction.
Precedent	Any executive or legislative action which goes beyond the scope of Article 366(22) or violates Article 291 or Article 362 would raise a question as to the limits of executive or legislative competence and it cannot be said to raise a dispute as to any right, liability or obligation.
Argument	It was emphasised that the only dispute is whether the President's order is a nullity and it is a dispute as to the limits of the President's jurisdiction and not a dispute in respect of any right, liability or obligation.
Argument	Secondly, it was said that Articles 291 and 362 are mandatory Articles and if the Government chose to raise disputes about those Articles it would amount to saying that the Government was disputing the very obligation enacted by those Articles in the Constitution.
Argument	Dispute in Article 363 was said not to cover a dispute the raising of which was expressly prohib ited by the other provisions of the Constitution.
Argument	Thirdly, any executive action in violation of Arti-  200  cles 291 and 362 or beyond the ambit of Article 366 (22) would be a violation of Articles 53 and 73 of the Constitution and the latter Articles did not at all relate to Covenants or Merger Agreements.
Argument	The refusal to pay privy purse was said to be in viola 4 of Articles 112, 113 and  114.
Argument	Again it was said that if a law was passed in violation of Articles 291 or Article 362 it would be a breach of Articles 245 and 246 which Articles were not related to Covenants or Merger Agreements at all.
Argument	Fourthly,, an executive action which is ultra vires or <NAME> fide is a nullity and the bar of jurisdiction under Article 363 would apply only where the action is bona fide and cannot apply where the order is ultra vires and nullity.
Argument	Article 363 bars the jurisdiction of all courts in respect of any dispute covered by the Article.
Argument	It is seriously challenged and controverted by the Government that Articles 291 and 362 hav e any mandatory character as alleged by the petitioner.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is disputed that the order is a nullity.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is.
RatioOfTheDecision	equally disputed that there cannot be any dispute as to rights or liabilities or obligations under the Articles aforesaid.
RatioOfTheDecision	If both parties say that an order is bona fide there can be no dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only when one party alleges the order to be a nullity and the other party affirms the order to be valid that parties will have a dispute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioner's contentions bristle with disputes which in the ultimate analysis resolve into keenly debated disputes as to rights of Rulership and Privy Purse.
Argument	The dispute as to jurisdiction of the President under Article 366(22) is not in vacuo but is a dispute as to rights of recognition of Ruler for the purposes of payment of Privy Purse and enjoyment of rights and privileges.
Argument	Mr. <NAME> submitted that he did not want any relief as to Privy Purse now and if the petitioner succeeded in getting a declaration that the order is nullity and if the Government thereafte r did not pay Privy Purse the petitioner would then apply for that relief.
RatioOfTheDecision	This position indicates beyond any doubt that the heart of the matter is dispute as to Privy Purse which is stopped by the Order of the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	The order is for purposes of payment of Privy Purse and that is what the petitioner is seeking to enforce.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to appreciate the true scope and content of Article 363 it is necessary to find out as to why this Article and Articles 291, 362, 366(22) (hereinafter referred to collectively as the allied Articles) found place in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	These allied Articles deal with privy purses, princely privileges guaranteed under the Covenants and Merger Agreements entered into by Rulers of Indian States and recognition of Rulers by the President under Article 366(22).
RatioOfTheDecision	The roots of these Articles lie deep in the past.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the history and chronicle of events will have to be told.
RatioOfTheDecision	The transition from the British Rule to the Indian Independence and the establishment of the Republic of our  country is a great constitutional development.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution which was evolved represented the national ethos forged by the aims and aspirations of the, people throughout the length and breadth of our country.
Precedent	A great problem which awaited solution on the eve of our independence was the relation between our country and the Indian States.
Precedent	<ORG> came to India in the month of March, 1946.
Precedent	The <ORG> came to bring about a change in the British policy towards India.
Precedent	Imperialism was crumbling after the Second World War.
Precedent	The Cabinet <ORG> in no uncertain terms said that when India was going to be an independent country it was not only necessary but also desirable that the Indian States should combine with free India for security, stability and solidarity.
Precedent	The Rulers of Indian States also realised the importance of such a measure in an advised age when the leaders of our country impressed upon the Rulers the wisdom.
Precedent	of such a course of action to avert the upheaval and upsurge of the peopl e in the Indian <ORG>s which were also tottering with the decline of British imperialism.
Precedent	It is in this background that <ORG> declared in May, 1946 that paramountcy of the British <ORG> which provided the basis of relations between British India and the Rulers of Indian <ORG>s could neither be retained by the British <ORG> nor transferred to the new <ORG>.
Precedent	The paramount power in British India was derived from the Royal Prerogative.
Precedent	The rights which the paramount power claimed in exercise of the functions of the <ORG> in relation to the <ORG> covered both external and internal matters in the <ORG>s.
Precedent	The Indian <ORG>s had no international status.
Precedent	The paramount power under <ORG> recognised succession to the gaddi and settled disputes as to succession and imposed the duty of loyalty to the <ORG>.
Precedent	The Indian <ORG>s Committee in 1927 had expressed the view that 'paramountcy must remain paramount, it must fulfil its obligations, defining or adapting itself according to the sh ifting necessities of the time and the progressive development of the States".
Precedent	This was the essence of the doctrine of paramountcy in British India.
Precedent	Paramountcy could not be defined.
Precedent	It was an imperialist imposition on the Rulers of Indian States.
Fact	<ORG> issued a Memorandum dated 12 May, 1946 and announced a plan on 16 May, 1946 later on known as <ORG> Plan.
Fact	In the memorandum <ORG> affirmed that the rights of the Indian States which flowed from their relations with the British Crown would no longer exist when the British would leave India and that the rights surrendered by the States to the paramount power would revert to these States.
Fact	<ORG> Plan was a statement embodying suggestions and recommendation towards the speedy setting up of a new Constitution for India.
Fact	Referring to the States, <ORG> Plan said that with the attainment of the Independence of our country, the relationship which had existed between the States and the British Crown wo uld no longer be possible and paramountcy ,could neither be retained by the British nor transferred to the new <ORG>.
Fact	The Plan further said that the <NAME> had given assurances that they were ready and willing to co- operate in the new development of India.
Fact	On 3 June, 1947 the British <ORG> superseded the Cabinet Mission Plan in so far as it referred to the States and made it clear that the decisions announced related only to British India and the British policy towards Indian States contained in the Cabinet Mission memorandum of 12 May, 1946 remained unchanged.
Fact	As a prelude to the transfer of power from the British Crown to our country the <ORG> of India decided to set up a <ORG> called the States <ORG> to conduct their relations with the States in matters of common concern.
Fact	On 5 July, 1947 <NAME> defined the policy of the <ORG> of India by stating that "the people of India were knit together by bonds of blood and feeling no less than of self-interest" and "no impassable  barriers could be set up between us" and he said that the alternative to co- operation was anarchy and chaos.
Fact	There was special meeting of the Rulers on 25 July, 1947.
Fact	The then <ORG> re- presentative Lord <NAME> in the course of his address to the Rulers advised them to accede to the appropriate Dominion in regard to three subjects of <ORG>, <ORG> and Communications and assured them that their accession on these subjects would involve no financial liability and in other matters there would be no encroachment on their internal sovereignty.
Fact	Barring three States the other Indian States acceded to the Dominion of India by 15 August, 1947.
Fact	The Indian Independence Act was to come into existence on '15 August, 1947.
Precedent	Section 7 of the Indian Independence Act, 1947 provided that with the lapse of suzerainty of the <ORG> over Indian States all treaties and agreements between the <ORG> and the Rulers of Indian States, all functions exercisable by the <ORG> with respect to India in States, all obligatio ns of the <ORG> towards Indian States or Rulers thereof and all powers, rights, authority or jurisdiction exercisable by the <ORG> on that date in or in relation to Indian States by treaty, grant, usage, suzerainty or otherwise would also lapse.
Precedent	The proviso to section 7 of the Indian Independence Act, 1947 was that notwithstanding the lapse of suzerainty and lapse of treaties, effect shall, as nearly as might 'be, continued to be given to the provisions of any such agreement referred to in section 7 (b) of the Act which related to customs.
Precedent	transit, communications, posts and telegraphs or other like matters until the provisions in question were denounced by the Ruler of the Indian State or by the Dominion or Province or were superseded by subsequent agreements.
Precedent	The Instruments of Accession executed by <ORG> declared accession to the Dominion of India on three subjects, viz., Defence, External Affairs and Communications.
Precedent	In the Instruments of Accession the Rulers provided that nothing in  the instrument wag to be deemed to commit the Ruler in any way to acceptance of any future Constitution of India or to fetter a Ruler's discretion to enter into arrangements with the Government of India under any such future Constitution.
Precedent	The Instrument concluded by stating that nothing in the Instrument would affect the continuance of the Ruler's sovereignty in and over the State or save as provided by or under the Instrument, the exercise of any powers, authority and rights then enjoyed by him as a Ruler of the State or the validity of any law then in force in his State.
Fact	The Instrument of Accession was followed by Stand Still Agreement.
Fact	The Stand Still Agreement between the Ruler and the Dominion of India provided that until new agreements were made all agreements and administrative arrangements as to matters of common concern then existing between the Crown and the Indian States should, in so far as might be appropriate, continue as between the Dominion of India or as the case might be, the part thereof,  and the <ORG>.
Fact	In a Schedule were enumerated the various matters of common concern.
Fact	The important matters were, inter-alia, Air communications, Arms and equipment, Currency and coinage Customs, <ORG>, External Affairs, Extradition, <ORG>, Irrigation and Electric Power, Motor vehicles, National Highways, Posts, Telegraphs and Telephones, Railways, Salt, Central Excises and 'Wireless.
Fact	The pattern of integration of Indian <ORG>s was not uniform in all cases.
Fact	There were 562 Indian <ORG>s whereof 216 merged in Provinces, 61 were taken over as centrally administered areas and 275 integrated in different <ORG>.
Fact	The Merger Agreements were entered into by the Rulers with the Dominion of India.
Fact	The two important clauses in the Merger Agreements were one whereby the Ruler, was to be entitled to receive from the revenues of the <ORG> annually for his privy purse the sum mentioned therein free of taxes and the other whereby <ORG> guaranteed succession accord ing to law and custom to the gaddi of the <ORG> and to the Ruler's personal rights, 14- L744supCI/71 privileges,, dignities and titles.
Fact	These two principal clauses are to be found in all Merger Agreements.
Fact	There were differences in the Merger Agreements as to the amount of privy purse and in some cases as to the rights of successors to Rulers with regard to privy purses.
Fact	The Rulers of Centrally merged <ORG>s also entered into similar agreements with the Dominion of India.
Fact	Those agreements also had two similar principal clauses for privy purse the sum mentioned free of taxes and guaranteed succession according to law and custom to the gaddi of the <ORG> and to the Ruler's personal rights, privileges, dignities and titles.
Fact	The third type of integration was the formation of a Union of <ORG>s whereby certain <ORG>s described as the Covenanting <ORG>s entered-into a Union of <ORG>s with a common executive, legislative and judiciary.
Fact	These Covenants provided for <ORG> with the <ORG> as the Presid ent of the <ORG>.
Fact	These Covenants also had similar provisions with regard to privy purses and succession.
Fact	The Ruler of each Covenanting State was to be entitled to receive annually from the revenues of the United State for his privy purse, the amount mentioned free of all taxes.
Fact	The succession according to law and custom to the gaddi of each Covenanting State and to the personal rights, privileges, dignities and titles to the Rulers was guaranteed.
Fact	<ORG> concurred in the Covenants and guaranteed all the provisions.
Fact	The Covenant for the United State of Madhya Bharat came into existence in the month of April, 1948.
Fact	The other <ORG> also came into existence near about the same time.
Fact	The Merger Agreements came into existence near about the months of April and May, 1948.
Fact	In the month of September, 1948 <ORG> executed revised Instruments of Accession, and these were signed by the Rajpramukhs of the different <ORG> of States.
Fact	These <ORG> accepted all matters enumerat ed in List I and List III of the Seventh Schedule of the Government of India Act, 1935 as matters in respect of which <ORG> might make laws for the Union of States other than items relating to any tax or duty in the territories of the United State.
Fact	These Revised Instruments of Accession were accepted by the Governor-General on behalf of the Dominion of India.
Fact	In the month of November, 1948 <ORG> by their Rajpramukhs issued proclamations accepting the Constitution of India.
Fact	The Government of India Act, 1935 was amended in the year 1947 to effect necessary changes on the passing of the Indian Independence Act, 1947.
Fact	Sections 5 and 6 of the Government of India Act, 1935 as amended in 1947 provided first for the accession of Indian States to the Dominion and secondly that an Indian State was to 'be deemed to have acceded to the Dominion if the Governor-General signified his acceptance of an instrument of accession making a declaration in terms of section 6 thereof.
Fact	Accession was  to be subject to the terms of the instrument.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has already been noticed earlier that all Rulers of Indian States executed Instruments of Accession but some Indian States thereafter merged with the Governors' Provinces and some were centrally administered areas after merger and then formed <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should be noticed that the Government India Act, 1935 did not provide for any Merger Agreement.
RatioOfTheDecision	These Merger Agree- ments in the case of Provincially merged and Centrally merged States did not have any legal basis and sanction under the Government of India Act, 1935.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Extra Provincial Jurisdiction Act 'was therefore passed in the year 1947 giving power to <ORG> to exercise extra provincial jurisdiction over a Provincially merged or a Centrally merged State only if the <ORG> had by treaty, agreement, acquired full and exclusive authority and jurisdiction and power for and in relation to the governance of the State.
RatioOfTheDecision	The administration of the merged Indian States could not be done ei ther under the Government of India Act, 1935 or the Instrument of Accession.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Extra Provincial Jurisdiction Act, 1947 was passed for exercising powers of administration and legislation in regard to provincially merged and centrally merged States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Extra Provincial Jurisdiction Act was really a half way house between complete separateness and full integration.
RatioOfTheDecision	A law passed by the Dominion <ORG> did not automatically apply to the merged States but had to be made applicable by a notification under the Extra Provincial Jurisdiction Act, 1947.
Precedent	That is why sections 290A and 290B were inserted by the Government of India Act Amendment Act, 1949 into the Government of India Act, 1935 for effecting integration of merged States.
Precedent	Section 290A of the Government of India Act, 1935 provided for administration of certain Acceding States as Chief Commissioners' Provinces or as part of a Governor's or Chief Commissioner's Province.
Precedent	Section 290B provided for administration of areas included within a Governor's Provi nce or a Chief Commissioner's Province by an Acceding State.
Precedent	Under the said section 290A there came into existence the States Merger (Governors' Provinces) Order, 1949 issued on 27 July, 1949.
Precedent	This order was applied to the provincially merged States with effect from 1 August, 1949.
Precedent	Under the States Merger (Governors' Provinces) Order, 1949 the provincially merged States were to, be administered in all respects as if they formed part of the absorbing Provinces and all laws including orders made under the Extra Provincial Jurisdiction Act, 1947 were to continue in force until repealed or modified.
Precedent	Under the States Merger Order,  provision was made for representation of the merged States in the Legislature of the absorbing Province,, the apportionment of assets and liabilities as between the <ORG> and the Provinces and the institution of suits and other proceedings against the Government and the continuance of pending proceedings.
Precedent	A similar order known as the States' Merger (Chief Commissioners' Provinces) Orde r 1949 was made applicable to the centrally merged States with effect from 1 August, 1949.
Precedent	The provisions of the States' Merger (Chief Commissioners' Provinces) Order, 1949 were simil ar to the States Merger (Governors' Provinces) Order, 1949.
Precedent	With the issue of the States Merger (Government Provinces) and 'States Merger (Chief Commissioners' Provinces) Orders, 1949 the position of the provincially merged States became to all intents and purposes, the same as that of the provinces.
Precedent	Similar progress was also made in the direction of improving the administrative machinery of the Chief Commissioner's Provinces which :assimilated the centrally merged States.
Argument	Mr. <NAME> on behalf of the petitioner contended that the developments and integration of Indian States on the basis of the Instruments of Accession and the Covenants and Merger Agreements were constitutional developments and provided constitutional obligations.
Argument	The Attorney General on the other hand rightly contended that the entire relationship of the D ominion of India vis-a-vis the Indian States was in the-domain of Acts of State and the Instruments, Merger Agreements and Covenants did not have any constitutional sanction and obligation and were totally unenforceable in municipal courts.
Precedent	The British Crown as Sovereign State dealt with the Indian States and either conquered or annexed their territories or Rulers of these States ceded their territors or some Rulers entered into alliances with the British ,Crown.
Precedent	Such action of the British Crown was held by long series ,of decisions to be an Act of State and treaties and stipulations arising out of Acts of State could not be enforced in municipal courts.
Precedent	This Court has in several decisions held that Covenants and Merger Agreements with the Indian States are Acts of State and not enforceable under municipal law.

RulingByLowerCourt	207  Mr.<NAME> contended that on the accession of Indian <ORG>s there could be no Act of <ORG> between the Dominion of India and the Rulers who acceded to the Dominion and thereafter between the Republic of India and the Rulers who were citizens.
RulingByLowerCourt	This argument is also fallacious.
RulingByLowerCourt	This <ORG> in the same case, <CITATION_19878> (1) "interpreted the integration of Indian <ORG>s with the Dominion of India as an Act of <ORG> and has applied the law relating to an Act of <ORG> as laid down by <ORG> in a long series of cases.
RulingByLowerCourt	..........
RulingByLowerCourt	The Act of <ORG> comes to an end only when the new sovereign recognises either expressly or impliedly the rights of the: alliens.........
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> further said "we are not concerned with the succession of India from the British Crown but with <ORG>: succession between Sant <ORG> and India and there was no second succession in 1950.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whatever had happened had al ready happened in 1948, when Sant <ORG> merged with the Dominion of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Act of <ORG> which began in 1948 could continue uninterrupted even beyond 1950 and it did not lapse or get replaced by another Act of <ORG>".
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_19878> (1) the citizen claimed right on the basis of a Tharao granted by the Ruler before the merger.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from the fact.
RatioOfTheDecision	that <ORG> cancelled the right this Court held that the right granted by the Ruler was not recognised before 1950 and the Constitution gave support to those rights which were extant on 26 January 1950.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> failed on both the grounds of recognition and existing law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Act of <ORG> is illustrated by the making of peace and war, the annexation or cession of territory, the recognition of a new <ORG> or new Government of an old <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such acts have been held not to form the basis of action because they form the subject of political action in an Act of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	'The sanction of an Act of <ORG> is political to all sovereign powers and that is wh y municipal courts accepted that position.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is in this background that the Attorney General described Article 363 as embodying the concept of paramountcy being recreated in the form of a constitutional provision excluding interference by Courts in disputes relating to Instruments of accession,.
RatioOfTheDecision	Covenants and Merger Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Attorney General did not submit that there was any paramountcy between the Republic and its citizens nor that there was any doctrine of paramountcy subsisting in our country after 1950 or that it survived as a constitutional provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 363 and the other allied Articles really reflect what the makers of the Constitution picked up from the historical past and inserted in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution provided for recognition of Rulers by the President.
RatioOfTheDecision	This recognition was necessary because without it the Rulers could not be paid privy purses or enjoy their rights and privileges.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) [1964] 6 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	416.
RatioOfTheDecision	208  These four Articles in the Constitution appear to be sligh tly unrealistic or anachronistic in a Republican Constitution as it deals with citizens and the sovereignty of the people being reposed in the Republic.
RatioOfTheDecision	The founding fathers inserted these four allied Articles as rich hangings in a homely house.
RatioOfTheDecision	The real basis for Article 363 was that when the Constitution recognised the ,guarantee of privy purses and succession to the gaddi in the Merger Agreements and Covenants it was appreciated that if any dispute in regard to such agreements or covenants or any dispute as to any right accruing under or any obligation arising out of any pro-vision of the Constitution relating to such covenants or agreements were allowed to be brought in a court of law, the entire political relationship of the Dominion of India with the Indian States in an aegis of Act of State might be upset and upturned by such litigation in municipal courts and there would be room for regret on many courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	If Article 363 were not inserted litigations would have gone on endlessly as some of the, Orissa  Rulers commenced in the State of Seraikella(1) case to undo the Orissa merger agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution contemplated political power of the Presi- dent to recognise Rulers.
RatioOfTheDecision	If people or disgruntled contenders for Rulership were allowed to litigate by challenging either the recognition or by preferring a claim of recognition, the courts would not be capable of adjudicating these disputes because the character and content of, the President's power of recognition of Rulers is political and is not limited by the personal law of succession.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, if the President withdrew recognition of a Ruler and the latter came to a court of law it would be equally impossible for .courts to decide in an area which was consigned to the President as an inheritance of political power from the domain of Acts of State and privileges of Paramountcy.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is why Article 363 really embodied the principles of Acts of State which regulated and guided the rights and obligations under the covenants or merger agreements by incorporatin g the doctrine of unenforceability of covenants or merger agreements coming into existence as Acts of State.
RatioOfTheDecision	The other reason for insertion of Article 363 was that the rights accruing under or obligations arising out of provisions of the Constitution relating to covenants or merger agreements were imperfect rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	A question was posed that if there were rights as to succession, privy purse and privileges there should be a remedy.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first place, there are no legal rights to recognition of Rulership, payment of privy purse and enjoyment of rights and privileges.
Precedent	Prior to the Constitution, the Rulers of Indian States could not start proceedings in municipal courts to enforce agreements or obligations arising out of covenants or merger (1)  S.C.R.
Precedent	474.
Precedent	209  agreements because such rights and obligations were unenforceable on the ground of dealings under Acts of State.
Precedent	The Constitution gave recognition to guarantees under covenants and agreements by the allied Articles 291, 363 and 366(22).
Precedent	The Attorney Ge neral characterised the payment of privy purse, enjoyment of rights and privileges and the recognition of <ORG> as imperfect rights and obligations.
Precedent	Whatever rights and obligations are to be found in the merger agreements and covenants were recognised by the Constitution in relation to those covenants and agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the Constitution made such rights unenforceable in a Court of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is why these rights and obligations are called imperfect rights and imperfect obligations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Examples can be found of such imperfect legal rights when claims are barred by lapse of time or claims are unenforceable because of lack of registration.
RatioOfTheDecision	These imperfect rights and obligations are described in <ORG> 12 Ed.
RatioOfTheDecision	at pages 233-234 to be exceptions to the maxim ubi jus ubi remedium because "the customary union between the rights and the rights of action has been for some special reasons severed" <NAME> warns against confusing obligatriness with enforceability.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is "because of unenforceability" that  "these rights are sometimes termed imperfect".
RatioOfTheDecision	Take for instance an ordinary contract of a merchant with the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the contract is not in compliance with Article 299 it is unenforceable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The merchant has a mere imperfect right.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The ordinary imperfect right is unenforceable because some rule of law declares it to be so.
RatioOfTheDecision	One's rights against the <ORG> are un- enforceable, not in this legal sense but in the sense that the strength of the law is none other than the strength of the <ORG> and cannot be turned or used against the <ORG> whose strength it is".
RatioOfTheDecision	Imperfect rights are not based on morality.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many rights are wrecked on the rock of unenforceability.
RatioOfTheDecision	Act of indemnity is one illustration.
RatioOfTheDecision	Duty is legal, when sanction is attached to its breach.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sanction means the appointed consequences of disobedience Sanctionless duties are imperfect obligations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Really speaking imperfect rights and obligations are what authors of Jurisprudence describe as no claim in the jural opposites of claim and no claim".
RatioOfTheDecision	A st atute barred debt cannot be recovered in a court of law but if for some reason the debtor pays it he cannot later sue to recover it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The creditor had no liability but only liberty to pay.
RatioOfTheDecision	Liberty or privilege begins where duty ends and no right exists.
RatioOfTheDecision	These imperfect rights are thus in the category of "no claim" because of lack of legal sanction for enforcement by the bar of unenforceability laid down in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution Article 363 is a positive Rule of unenforceability of certain rights and obligations.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is supreme and the provisions cannot be circumvented.
Precedent	This Court held in the <NAME> case(1) that Article 363 is a bar in any (1)  S.C.R.
Precedent	474.
Precedent	210  dispute relating to covenants and merger agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_19878> (1) this Court held that Article 363 precluded the municipal courts from considering and adjudicating upon any right under the Merger Agreement and guarantees were matters for the political department of the <ORG> and were thus outside the jurisdiction  of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, in <NAME> case, this <ORG> held 'that the privy purse was a political pension and the payment was in relation to covenants and Merger Agreements, and, therefore, Article 363 was a bar.
RatioOfTheDecision	in a recent decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_347856> 's OrS.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) it has been held that the recognition of rulership by the President is not an indicia of property but it entitles the Rulers to the enjoyment of Privy Purse contemplated in Article 291 and the personal rights, privileges and dignities mentioned in Article 362 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was also held that the recognition of rulership by the President was an executive and political power and Article 363 constitutes a bar to interference by courts in a dispute arising by reason of recognition of rulership.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> submitted that there was no political power of the President who had only executive power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words "political power" denote power belonging to the <ORG>,, its government and policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Executive power has the  political facet in many cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	To illustrate the exercise of rights, authority and jurisdiction by virtue of any treaty or agreement ( Article 73 ); Foreign Affairs (Entry 10 in List I; of the Seventh Schedule) Entering into treaties and agreements with foreign countries and implementing of treaties, agreements and conventions in foreign countries (Entry 14 in List I of the Seventh Schedule); War and Peace (Entry 15 in List I of thE Seventh Schedule) and Foreign jurisdiction (Entry 16 in List I of the Seventh Schedule): The power of recognition of Rulership is political because it is exercised by the President in relation to Prince or Chief by whom any Covenant or Merger Agreement was entered into and the necessity for recognition arises from the Covenants and Merger Agreements.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a political power because it is not limited only to the law of succession or 'custom.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reasons of State Policy will enter the field.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also a political power because it is not a compulsive power.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the scope of the power  permits the President to recognise some one who is not entitled by law and custom then law and custom does not control it.
RatioOfTheDecision	By political power is meant that the consideration which moves the President is a matter on which the <ORG> will find no standard for resolving, it judicially.
Precedent	"There is no judicial process to adjudicate upon such political consideration".

Statute	(1) section 3 of Ordinance No.
Statute	19 of 1946 contained a non-obstante clause with the words "notwithstanding the expiration of the Defence of India Act, 1939, and the rules made thereunder, all requisitioned lands shall continue to be subject to requisition-until the expiry of this Ordinance and the appropriate Government may use or deal with any requisitioned land in such manner as may appear to it to be expedient".
RatioOfTheDecision	The non-obstante clause was invoked in support of the submission that only those orders which would have ceased to be operative and come to an end on the expiration of the Defence of India Act and the Rules were the orders which were intended to be continued under section 3 of the Ordinance.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court held that although o rdinarily there should be a close approximation between the non-obstante clause and the operative part of the section, the non-obstante clause need not necessarily and always be co-extensive with the operative part, so as to have the effect of cutting down the clear terms of an enactment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The, non-obstante clause was held not to cut down the construction and restrict the scope of the operation of the enactment, but was to be understood to have been incorporated in the enactment by way of abundant caution and not by way of limiting the ambit and scope of the operative part of the enactment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result was that all immoveable properties which when the Defence of India Act expired were subject to any requisition effected under the Defence of India Act and Rules thereunder were to continue to be subject to requisition until the expiry of the Ordinance.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> submitted that the petitioner's contention that the order of the President was a nullity was not a dispute within Article 363.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ordinary meaning  of dispute is a contention, a controversy, a difference of opinion, a conflict of claims, and assertion of right on one side and the denial of it by the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <ORG> it will appear that dispute as to whether a thing is ultra vires is nonetheless a dispute within an arbitration clause.
Argument	In <ORG> Council(2) the plaintiff asked for a declaration that certain, provisions of the Cantonment Act, 1924 were ultra vires.

RatioOfTheDecision	In the present case the dispute is whether the President has or has not the power to make the order impugned in these proceedings'.
RatioOfTheDecision	The next question Which falls for consideration is the meaning of the words "right accruing under", "any liability or obligation arising out of", "any of the provisions of the Constitution".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that if any right is said to accrue under or liability is said to arise out of any provision of the Constitution, the matter ends there as far as those words are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention' of the petitioner that the President has no power under Article 366(22) to make an order for derecognition is a right asserted by the petitioner under the provisions of the Constitution and it is also the petitioner's contention that the President has no right arising out of Article 366(22) n ot to make an order of derecognition.

RatioOfTheDecision	Ordinarily, the word "relate" means to bring a thing or person in relation to another, to connect, establish a relation between, to have reference to, to be related, having relation to and to stand in some relation to another thing.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the dictionary meaning.
Argument	Mr. <NAME> submitted that the provi sions of the Constitution, viz., Articles 366(22), 291 and 362 might have reference to the Covenant but were not related to the Covenant.

Precedent	Mr. <NAME> relied on the decisions of this <ORG> as also the recent decision of <ORG> in support of the proposition that if the order was a nullity there was no bar of jurisdiction.

Precedent	It is a general rule that where <ORG> has created new rights and duties and has appointed a specific <ORG> for their enforcement recourse must be had to that <ORG> alone.
Precedent	The jurisdiction of the courts of Law in those cases is ousted until the statutory process has been completed except in so far as the courts may prohibit the <ORG> from proceeding on the ground that it had no jurisdiction to determine a particular matter.
Precedent	In situations, where the courts have no jurisdiction to intervene, they may nevertheless revie w the validity of the final determination by the chosen <ORG> either on the, ground that the authority was not the one designated by the Act or where it was empowered to determine an issue it did not address itself to the matter committed to it or where it violated the rule, of natural justice.
